Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160Â£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.

Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.

Mr SPEAKER-I inform the House that on 22 October 1982 I received a letter from the Right Honourable Sir Phillip Lynch, KCMG., resigning his seat as member for the electoral division of Flinders. I will give consideration to the date of the by-election as soon as practicable.
MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS

FRASER GOVERNMENT
Notice of Motion
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House censures the Government for its abject failure in administering the economy, especially as reflected by record unemployment, skyrocketing inflation, negative economic growth and loss of business and consumer confidence.
Sir JAMES KILLEN (Moreton-Leader of the House)-I seek leave of the House to move a motion to enable the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) to move forthwith the motion of censure of which he has given notice for the next day of sitting.
Leave granted.
Sir JAMES KILLEN-I wish to inform the House that I accept the notice of motion given by the Leader of the Opposition as a motion of censure of the Government for the purpose of Standing Order No. 110.

Suspension of Standing Orders
Motion (by Sir James Killen) agreed to:
That so much of the Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent the Leader of the Opposition moving forthwith the motion of censure of the Government of which he has given notice for the next day of sitting.
Motion of Censure
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (1.49)-1 move:
That this House censures the Government for its abject failure in administering the economy, especially as reflected by record unemployment, skyrocketing inflation, negative economic growth and loss of business and consumer confidence.
Nothing exemplifies the failure of the Government more than the release of the inflation figures yesterday. They display beyond any doubt that the Government's tightlipped crusade against inflation has been converted into an unholy rout. On every front in the past several weeks it is clear that the Government is suffering more and more setbacks in economic administration. The indisputable fact is that on any or all of those fronts the severity off the setback finally rests in responsibility on the Government. The Government is elected to administer the economy of this country. It has had seven years in which to fulfil promises which were easily given and more easily broken year by year. No one in the corporate sector proposing for the year ahead such optimistic prospects as this Government has done year by year but then failing to achieve those prospects would be kept in a position of senior administration. Any such person would be regarded as a failure. This Government is a failure in conducting the affairs of this nation and ought to be scourged from office.
I said that on every front more and more setbacks afflict the Government. The Budget was an inappropriate instrument with which to administer the economy of this country in the year ahead. One freely acknowledges that it is extremely difficult to handle a national economy at present, given the state of the world's economy. But, on the other hand, other countries are doing much better than this country is and have been consistently doing much better than this country has been doing. They have been able to achieve that in spite of difficult economic circumstances. The outstanding example is Austria. It has far less inflation, far lower unemployment and a pricesincomes policy in place with public confidence, trust and respect. They are all of the essential ingredients to put in place a policy to give greater stability to an economy but all the essential ingredients that this Government is incapable of achieving. Does anyone think for a minute that

2488 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Fraser Government

the Fraser Government is capable of capturing that elusive quality, trust? The Government chases it from the Parliament in the way in which it conducts the nation's affairs.

I said that on every score the evidence is that
the economy is in reverse. It gives us no satisfaction to declare this obvious, indisputable fact, a
fact based on the economic statistics produced from various official and authoritative sources in
this country. They all add up to this: We have record unemployment, skyrocketing inflation,
negative economic growth and loss of business
and consumer confidence. Look at the facts. The Melbourne University Morgan research survey
for September-after the Budget-showed the
second lowest level of consumer confidence since the survey started several years ago in 1974. The
lowest level was just before the Budget. In August
only 21,500 job vacancies existed for 459,000 unemployed, that is, only one for every 21 unem-
ployed. The previous August there had been one
vacancy for every II unemployed. The WestpacConfederation of Australian Industry survey
shows business confidence at its lowest ebb for nine years. A detailed examination of production
statistics reveals that industrial production in the
year to September had slumped by about 10 per cent. Australian industry is currently producing at the same level as it was six years ago. The Govern-
ment has the economy in the productive sector in
reverse. In the housing industry the Indicative Planning Council forecasts a 22 per cent underutilisation of building industry capacity.

They are only some of the more obvious and

disturbing statistics that show that the Budget

which this House has before it has not even been

passed-it is a totally inappropriate instrument

with which to manage the economic affairs of this

country. In the Budget Speech, the Treasurer (Mr

Howard) declared that the anticipation of the

Governmen.t was that inflation would stay some-

where near ItS. present level. In fact, it is going up.

The expectatIOn was that interest rates would

lodge somewhere near where they were at the

time the Budget was introduced. The evidence

s~em~ to be that there has been a temporary res-

pite In some areas but there has been none in

housing and there is little hope that there will be

in the course of this year. The Treasurer ack-

nowledged that there would be a collapse in the

building industry and that there would be far

worse unemployment. What sort of economic strategy ~s that for a.co,untry as well set up as this

country IS, a country With so many natural advan-

tages over other .countries? We have a resource

bcaaps~eblea,n

industnal intelligent,

base and a work force of diligent people who, when

properly led, will respond to the challenge and make sure that this country is doing better than it
has been.
No one on this side of the House is even seeking to imply that we can get back, least of all quickly, to where we were in the 1960s and early 1970s before the international economic situation became so difficult. On my calculations it will take several years of sustained healthy levels of economic growth to get us back to what we normally regard as a full employment level. But the point is we can do much better if we try. After seven years of these economic policies of failure, of the Government putting its thumb on the windpipe of the economy and choking it year after year, is it not about time that we tried a different way? Is it not about time that we tried a way which respected people and acknowledged the massive social distress which takes place in Australia as a result of the unrelieved experience of these policies of austerity and recession-the policies that guarantee high inflation? Inflation in this country is high for a number of reasons. One of the major reasons is this Government's pricing policy. I will come back to that.
I ask honourable members to look at the massive social distress that must be measured in terms of the suffering of ordinary, decent people who have no control over their destiny when a government goes about handling the economy as though it were hammering it with a sledgehammer. Unemployment belleficiaries increased to 446,000 in September of this year, an increase of 54 per cent in males and 27 per cent in females.
This is the daunting, indeed disgraceful, achievement of the Fraser Government in its term of office which it brazenly acknowledges. We now have three million people who are living in poverty in this country today-three million impoverished fellow Australians. Think of the waste of human resources and human potential. In this potentially wealthy country there are as many people living in poverty as in total live in Sydney or in total live in Melbourne. More people in Australia are living in poverty than live in the whole of Queensland. That is the sort of social distress, the waste of human potential, for which the Fraser Government has been responsible.
Honourable members will remember all those grandiloquent promises that h~ve proved to be so fickle for example the promise to preserve the benefit level of pensions. One of the things for which I am greatly proud as a personal achievement is that when the Labor Government went into office in 1972 the standard rate of pension was 18 per cent of average weekly earnings. As

Fraser Government

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2489

Minister for Social Security and subsequently Treasurer I had lifted that rate of pension, according to the Labor Government's undertaking, to just on 25 per cent of average weekly earnings. It is lower than 22 per cent now and will only get back to 22 per cent when the November increase comes through. This confirms what I am saying. The people who are paying the highest price for the austerity imposed by this Government are not the Horgans of the Liberal Party, the Frasers of the western districts of Victoria or the Anthonys of the pig farming industry of the northern rivers of New South Wales, all wealthy in their own right--
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable gentleman will use the appropriate term for members of the House.
Mr HAYDEN-The people who are suffering are those who have no reserves of resourcesphysical, emotional or financial-to carry them safely through this distress. I ask honourable members to look at this empty promise from the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) who brazenly sits in this chamber and who moralised at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Conference last year about how much he had done for Aboriginals in this country-one of the most exposed, dependent and abused groups in our society. In the 1975 election speech of the right honourable Malcolm Fraser we find:
We will maintain present levels of assistance to Aborigines.
Let me give honourable members the facts. The cumulative real loss for Aborigines over progressive Fraser Budgets amounts to some $416.5m. Involved in these losses have been: Aboriginal health spending currently 23 per cent less than in 1975. Does it matter that trachoma is at unacceptably high levels equalled rarely in the world; that in some parts of Australia leprosy is in epidemic proportions ahead of Third World countries; that communicable social diseases are rampant; or that a whole range of health deficiencies that can be properly attended to with a sustained, well directed nutritional, housing, fresh water, job opportunity and education program could be over- ~ome? That does not matter. The levels of spendmg are cut. Aboriginal spending is down 37 per cent. Housing funds are down an estimated 54 per cent. The list goes on and on of cut-backs in the provision of funds for this extraordinarily dependent group in our community.
Let us look at a selection of some of the headlines in the daily newspapers lately. Let us measure what contemporary comment has to say about the success of the slogan: 'Fight inflation

first and then we will create more jobs.' The Sydney Morning Herald of 2 August said: '1,000 a week are being laid off.' The Adelaide Advertiser a few days ago, on 23 October, said: 'GMH plans shut-down.' The economy is in such good con-
dition under the Fraser Government that big business has to ask the workers whether they will cop a shut-down without pay for a week as starters because it cannot sell motor cars. The manufacturers cannot sell their motor cars because people cannot afford them. People cannot afford motor
cars because the Fraser Government has been too busy ripping them off with the most extortionate tax charges in our history while the tax parasites have been ripping off the rest of the community with immunity. These are the standards of the Fraser Government. Another headline reads: 'BHP plans to sack 1700 despite inquiry.' If the motor car industry is now collapsing, Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd will collapse even further. Another headline reads: '800 idle for a fortnight at Goodyear.' According to the National Times, in the past 18 months the works at Whyalla have pruned staff by 1,200. The upshot has been a rise in unemployment at Whyalla to 10 per cent.
I was in Adelaide on Friday night watching the Australian Broadcasting Commission television news at 7 o'clock. I saw our beloved national
leader, the Prime Minister, whose word is his bond. We take it for what it is worth. Its market value is such, however, that one would not be able to live on it for a week. There he was on television standing arrogantly on some raised platform in front of a group of workers who were making a mild grumble of protest. They gave every evidence of being demoralised by their experiences and at the prospect of more unemployment in the Iron Triangle. There are extraordinary high rates of unemployment there amongst the young people. Does the Prime Minister know that in
South Australia unemployment among the young is 30 per cent? These are Great Depression levels. That is what the Prime Minister's policies have
done. He arrogantly stood on the back of that raised platform and said to these workers: 'What is wrong with you? You have got jobs here, haven't you?' Well, they do not know whether they will have jobs next week. This is all a product of the sorts of policies for which the Prime Minister has been responsible. Sure, these people have got jobs, but the Prime Minister does not know what it is like in our sort of society to be battling on the bare margin of existence. Let me quote some case studies from an Australian Council of Social Service document entitled 'Living on the edge'. This is what our society is giving ordinary decent people today. We have a Prime Minister

2490 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Fraser Government

who, when he is hungry, rings a dinner bell and someone brings in his food. He cannot understand why the poor do not do the same thing. It is not quite as easy as that.
Mr Good luck-Practise what you preach. What about you? You get a big salary.
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member for Franklin will return to his own seat and cease interjecting.
Mr HAYDEN-I do not happen to wear the poverty I experienced in my early life as a badge of honour at all. I rarely refer to it, but I read this sort of thing and I know what it is like. Let me tell honourable members what it is like. I hope they will understand it. The rich men in the Government would not understand. Under the heading 'John-A lone father', the article states:
. . . John's income totals $108.88. With this he supports 2 children, 8 and 5 years old.
with the $14.38 that is left after costs, John struggles first to pay the power bills, then to clothe the kids and himself, and then pay any 'extras'. Getting any left over to put towards debts or repairs is impossible.
Often John finds the house is short on food 'sometimes the kids have Weeties for tea'.
There is poverty and depression in this country for tens of thousands, indeed, three million Australians today, such is the nature of the policies of the Fraser Government. The article goes on quotingJohn:
'I can't give things to the kids; I will go out with others whose kids have nice clothes and it gets me down when other kids have nice dresses and Lisa has used clothes'. Preschool is also 'a luxury'.
Colin and Jenny are another family. They find that they borrow from friends nearly every week and are on an account at the local garage. They find they have to seek the help of welfare agencies extensively-six times in the past 12 months. They pawn their old furniture. All members of the family need shoes desperately. Several school excursions have had to be missed-even to the zoo.
Let us look at another family, George and Brenda. George supports his family on an income of $161.90 a week. The article goes on:
All that remains is $8.60 and from this has to come the quarterly gas and electricity, all the family's clothing, any recreation and any extras . . . it's not surprising to find that George and Brenda are behind in their chemist payments by $25 and still owe $1500 On the car and $100 to a Waltons
account.
Let us take this quote from the Age of May 1982 discussing the struggles for supporting mothers. The article states:
She feeds Jamie and Joanie with lots of soup, mince and carrots, clothes them in opshop specials and her sister's

cast-offs. She scours the papers for news of free entertainments. And when there is enough petrol for her car-- "a crappy old '64 Falcon, it cost me $399"-she makes some soup and they go off for a picnic.
What a grand treat! Some people have to celebrate on watered-down vegetable soup. That is the product of the Fraser Government's policies.
I am getting away from the economic statistics, the level of inflation, the level of unemployment, the lack of-in fact the negative-economic growth in the community, the high interest rates, the fact that people are being forced to sell homes they desperately want and cannot afford to pay off. I am talking about the dimensions of human suffering that is going on in this country. What did the Treasurer say about all this after seven years? He said this last night:
A reversal of current economic policy is not the answer to our present level of unemployment.
Well, what is the answer the Government has? For seven years the Government has been talking about the need for sacrifices for a better day. But the sacrificial blood on the altar of the narrow minded ideology of the Prime Minister is the blood of ordinary, decent Australians who deserve better. It has to come to an end. What is the Government's defence in respect of all this? Is it the drought? Certainly, the drought creates problems. Wherever we can we will assist the Government in explaining its problems and promoting policies to assist people who are disadvantaged by the drought. But do not forget that it is not unusual for natural calamities to befall a country during a term of a government's administration. We experienced it in 1974. We had to reconstruct Darwin and reconstruct the south-east corner of Queensland in the wake of a cyclone and a massive flood. We got no credit for that from the Government, then in opposition. That added to our economic difficulties. We at least are generous and acknowledge the Government's problem. The Government says it is the States who are to blame. The Government has cut real per capita provision of funds to the States by 20 per cent in the last seven years. But the spending of the Government shows no abatement. Commonwealth tax revenue is up by 150 per cent; Commonwealth resources to the States are up by only 90 per cent. So, the States are left with no alternative. They will have to raise revenue or engage in widespread closing down of services and retrenchments. So they raise revenue as much as they can. They have to resort to charges and indirect levies. They are all inflationary. The Government, through its cleverely concocted economic policies of fighting inflation with increased costs, forces the States to join in that sort of spurious battle. The Government says that the inflation is caused

Fraser Government

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2491

by wages. Average real labour costs have increased by 7 per cent since 1975-76 and average labour productivity by more than 9 per cent.
But the real fault on the wages front rests with the Prime Minister. He destroyed the centralised wage fixing arrangement and in destroying it declared that it would be better for wage fixing to be adjusted by market forces. Well, he now has market forces. And he is whingeing. He was whingeing at the Metal Trades Industry Association of Australia dinner last night. But last year, at his annual Christmas party held at The Lodge, according to Mr Des Keegan of the Australian when the Prime Minister was talking to journalists there he said that he was 'not unduly worried about the metal trades proposals or fears of a flow-on to the rest of the work force'.
If the Government is in trouble over wages I acknowledge-it would be economically illiterate not to acknowledge-that wages can be an inflationary pressure. But if the problem is wages, where is the Government's wages policy? It is running the affairs of this country, not the trade union movement. The Government says that industrial disputes are to blame. The total number of days lost through industrial disutes for the seven months to July 1982 as against the comparable period last year shows a reduction of 37 per cent, and in the metal trades industry a reduction of 79 per cent. It is spurious nonsense for the Prime Minister to try to concoct this sort of defence for failures which are explicitly his. The trouble with this country is that it has had Fraser's seven fatal years of failure in economic management. Look at what he said in 1975. He said:
Let us all as Australians determine to restore prosperity, defeat inflation and provide jobs for all.
Why did he not do that? He said:
We will work positively in cooperation with trade unionists.
He then set about berating and abusing them in a way which could be described only as a continuous smear against a whole class of Australian citizens-trade union officials. How does he respond to that? He said:
We will fully index personal income tax for inflation over three years.
He never did it. He said:
.Only under a Liberal-National Country Party Government Will there be a return to business confidence.
There is not such a return. In all this there is, indeed, a gem of all gems. The Prime Minister talks about the restoration of trust, confidence and respect for government in the community. He ha.s been able to achieve absolutely none of those thmgs. He said that the fault is concerned with wages. If the fault is concerned with wages it is the

fault of the Government to the extent that it may be a contributing factor. It is the fault of the Government because it has no orderly, fair, centralised wage fixing system and that is exactly what we are working towards achieving through our association with the trade union movement.
What is desperately needed in this country today is an immediate rescue operation on the economic front. I make some suggestions: In view of the fact that so much of the contribution to inflation is now coming from increased government charges, the Government should call a Premiers Conference urgently and explore with the States, by proposing to them, the possibility that they establish a 12-month freeze on State government charges, that the Commonwealth agree to do the same with its charges and that the Commonwealth make up the difference for all the States in the revenue they would have gained from any forthcoming increases in charges they would have lost through this freeze. Next the Commonwealth should provide the States with a one-off increase in capital and recurrent grants to be used during the forthcoming 12 months to provide jobs in the State government sector. The Government should also immediately increase its funding for housing in particular. Part of the increased allocation to the States would be specifically directed towards local government which can quickly use it for community works and so on.
Next, the Commonwealth should announce that it will not reduce the level of protection currently provided to any Australian industry for a 12-month period and that, in view of the seriousness of the employment situation, it will review its decision on temporary assistance for the steel industry in exchange for a commitment from BHP about employment security. Further, again in view of the seriousness of the situation and the urgency with which it must be dealt, the Commonwealth should seek an immediate conference with union peak councils with a proposal that over the next 12 months both the unions and the Commonwealth 'vould support the maintenance of real wages but nothing more than that, in view of the circumstances, forgoing productivity increases entirely.
This is not, nor is it meant to be, a comprehensive incomes policy. However, it should be the first element, together with the proposal for restraining government charges, towards such a policy. Despite our scepticism about the Budget estimates the Opposition acknowledges that from the Government's point of view the economy is now in a worse state than it was when the Budget was put together. This means that the Budget

2492 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Fraser Government

measures will be insufficient to cope with the worsened economic situation. We therefore propose that the Government introduce a mini-Budget before Parliament rises this year directly aimed at providing new job opportunities and, where possible, job security and, where possible, increases in disposable incomes in order to restore consumer demand. We want people to have the capacity and willingness to increase their spending before Christmas. Elements of such a mini-Budget would obviously include increased capital works funding and proposed increases in pensions and benefits. The key thing about this proposal, if it were directed in these ways, is that it would receive the unequivocal support of the Opposition, which would guarantee its rapid passage through Parliament in the interests of Australian families. If the Government introduces measures along these lines straight away the Opposition will do everything it can to gain the support of the public and State Labor governments as well as the trade union movement. We regard this as a critical situation.
On every front the economic indicators are giving a disastrous reading. There has to be a change of economic policy. Government policies are pushing up inflation. Just as they push up inflation, by reversing them the Government can bring inflation down. OUi' universal health insurance program alone can reduce inflation by 2 per cent in one year. Capital works programs and increased spending on public sector and private sector housing will create jobs and accelerate demand activity in the economy. All this has to be underpinned by a responsible, fair, orderly pricesincomes arrangement with the trade union movement. But that cannot finally be achieved unless trust, respect and confidence are established between government and that sector of the community. That, I suspect, is almost impossible with the present Prime Minister. We censure the Government.
Mr SPEAKER-Is the motion seconded?
Mr Willis-I second the motion and reserve my right to speak.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (2.17)-The speech of the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) was remarkable for two reasons. Firstly, it was not until the last five minutes that he rushed out what purported to be the elements of some alternative economic strategy. The second remarkable feature of the Leader of the Opposition's speech was his almost total refusal even to acknowledge the possibility that wage increases may be an element in high inflation in Australia at present. The very most that

the Leader of the Opposition could do to acknowledge the fact that the extent to which large wage increases of people who are lucky enough now to have jobs have contributed not only to the disemployment around Australia over the past 12 or 18 months but also to the level of inflation, which the Government regards as not only disturbing but also unacceptable, revealed by the consumer price index figures released yesterday, was simply to say that wage increases can have an impact on inflation. He did not even have the decency to acknowledge that they might have been a factor, even if not in his view the most important factor, contributing to our present economic difficulties. For 30 minutes we heard a speech from the Leader of the Opposition that was very strong on personal abuse but very short on alternativ~ economic strategy and very short on correct analysis of the reasons for our current economic difficulties.
The Leader of the Opposition derides the fact that excessive wage increases have contributed to our current economic difficulties. Of course his colleague the honourable member for GelIibrand (Mr WilIis) does not want to acknowledge that fact either. The truth of the matter is that average weekly earnings in Australia in 1978-79 rose by 7.7 per cent; in 1979-80 they rose by 9.9 per cent; in 1980-81 they rose by 13.5 per cent; and in 1981-82 they rose by about 14 per cent. In the teeth of that sort of evidence how can the Leader of the Opposition claim, to use one of his own favourite expressions, economic literacy, and make a speechabout the Australian economy which has absolutely no reference to the contribution that wage increases have made? All he can do is heap personal abuse on my colleagues and totally ignore the basic causes. He totally ignores the contribution that those increases have made not only to our present level of inflation but also to the present high level of unemployment. It is little wonder that Australian firms have difficulty selIing goods overseas when wage increases in Germany are now running at the rate of about 4 per cent, in Japan at 3 per cent to 4 per cent, in the United States of America at 6 per cent, and in the United Kingdom, which has a slightly higher inflation rate, at around 7 per cent. Is it any wonder that this country is uncompetitive? Is it any wonder that firm after firm has found it necessary to retrench people because it has proved economically impossible for those firms to retain the larger numbers of employees that they have had in earlier years?
The first thing I would say to the Leader of the Opposition and his colleagues is that, if they presume to censure the economic policies of this

Fraser Government

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2493

Government, any censure, any submission and any speech they might make on economic management at present which ignores the contribution that wage increases have made, not only in a direct sense but also in an indirect sense towards high inflation in Australia at present, is to ignore reality. I take honourable members opposite back to what has happened to average weekly earnings in Australia over the last few years. I take them back to when they rose to 16.2 per cent in 1973-74; 25.4 per cent in 1974-75; 14.4 per cent in 1975-76; 12.4 per cent in 1976-77; 9.9 per cent in 1977-78; and then 7.7 per cent, 9.9 per cent, 13.5 per cent and 13.6 per cent. A reading of that list illustrates very clearly the contribution that excessive wage increases have made to the high levels of inflation that were experienced through the early and middle years of the 1970s and to the level of
inflation being experienced by Australia at present. I made it very clear yesterday, when the consumer price index figures for the September quarter were released, that they were a matter of concern to the Government. They were frankly higher than the Government had believed was likely. There are a number of particular reasons for that.
I do not know whether the Opposition is interested in an analysis of the CPI figure for the September quarter, but some of that analysis bears study. There is no doubt that a significant contribution to the increase in the consumer price index has been made by State and Federal government charges. The contribution made by the increases in electricity charges around Australia, particularly in New South Wales but not only in that State, are an element in that. Of course, that takes us back to a time a couple of years ago when the New South Wales Government bought industrial peace with the power unions in that State by granting them a 37t hour week. We warned on that occasion that that decision would unleash a number of industrial pressures and a number of wage increases which would inevitably result in higher electricity charges in New South Wales. Anybody who has studied the pattern of what has happened in that State will see that the truth of what the Government warned at that time has indeed been borne out.
There is also no doubt that the CPI increase for the September quarter was affected by the increases in petrol prices which have occurred around Australia-in particular, the introduction of a petrol tax in New South Wales and also the imposition of the 1c levy in the Budget to finance the Australian Bincentennial Road Development Program. There is no doubt that exchange rate depreciations, which have affected the declining

competitiveness of Australian industry, have also played a major role. I think it is also worth pointing out to the House that the method of collection of statistics has recently been altered by the Statistician. This could easily have resulted in a number of prices which otherwise would have shown up in the December quarter CPI in fact being included in the September quarter. So I say to the House that although the outcome for the September quarter is a matter of serious concern, is disturbing to the Government, and ought to be disturbing to all members of the House who are concerned with greater economic growth and greater economic recovery, it really is not an alternative to embrace the policies which I understand the Opposition stands for-although the Leader of the Opposition did not provide us with much of a clue today as to what those policies really are.
Perhaps we can go behind the Leader of the Opposition and see whether we can find out from the honourable member for Gellibrand, Labor's spokesman on economic matters, what the Opposition's alternative economic policy is. Yesterday, being interviewed on the Today with Mark Day program, he said that it would be a good idea if we had a small increase in the deficit. The interviewer said to him:
How big is a small increase in your opinion?
This is what the honourable member for Gellibrand answered:
Well, one must relate this to technical things like the rate of growth of money in the community and so on, and one must not increase . . .
He meant the deficit-
to the point where there is a large increase in the money supplies, the amount of money floating around. But within the constraints that that imposes there is flexibility and a degree of possibility for increasing the deficit which enables the Government to give the economy a . . .
A boost presumably-
to create some jobs, but not have an inflationary impact.
Of course, the honourable member for Gellibrand in that answer was trying to have two bob each way. He did not really want to admit that he was a big deficit man. He said that a Labor Government would create some jobs, yet in Adelaide on Friday the Leader of the Opposition was talking about 150,000 jobs in the first year that Labor was in power. How does that compare with what the honourable member for Gellibrand was saying? He was trying to play down the impact on the money supply of the sorts of policies in which he really believes, yet his leader only a few days earlier was waxing eloquent. The Leader of the Opposition was also talking about how under a Labor government there would be a new era of Commonwealth-State co-operation. We ought to

2494 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Fraser Government

remember what the honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) has said about State government charges in the minerals area. He has made it very clear to the mining industry of Australia that if Labor ever becomes the government of this country it will ride completely roughshod over the rights and the interests of State governments so far as levies and charges are concerned. There can be no doubt that there is not only an unwillingness on the part of the Leader of the Opposition to come clean as to what his real alternative economic strategy is but also a good deal of conflict between the honourable member for Gellibrand and the Leader of the Opposition. When it comes to talking about the size of the Budget deficit, the honourable member for Gellibrand very clearly wants to run for cover. He knows as well as everybody else in this House that if an alternative economic strategy were applied it would not be more anti-inflationary, it would be less anti-inflationary. What we need at the moment are policies which exert a greater downward influence, not a greater upward pressure, on inflation. If we adopt policies that are far more permissive so far as the money supply is concerned, if we have the much larger deficit that Labor stands for, if we adopt the exchange rate policies which have been constantly advocated by the Leader of the Opposition, and if we adopt the fiscal policies that were again advocated today by the Leader of the Opposition, the net effect will be to create a far more inflationary climate than exists at present. Any sober objective economic analysis would hav~ ~o acknowledge that, if we adopt more permissive monetary and fiscal policies the prospect of securing a greater level of wag~ restraint will be even further down the track.
The Leader of the Opposition accuses us of being insensitive to the plight of poor Australians. He accuses us of adopting a confrontationist attitude to the trade union movement. He accuses us of being unwilling to talk to all sections of the Australian community. Has the Leader of the Opposition not taken a careful look at many of the benefits in the personal taxation and family allowance area contained in the last Budget whereby average Australian families will be better off to the tune of $17 to $20 a week? Many of those concessions were in fact put together by the Government after very extensive consultation not only with the trade union movement but also with representatives of Australian industry. It was an attempt by this Government to respond to the needs of average Australian families; it was not only an attempt but I believe a very successful attempt.

The speech of the Leader of the Opposition was noticeably silent on those subjects. He was noticeably silent about the fact that tax cuts on a full year basis of about $2,500m will come into effect next week and will make a significant contribution to the living standards and the position of average Australian families. Those income tax reductions, the family allowance increases and the mortgage rebate scheme have not been directed at the wealthy people of Australia; they have been directed at ordinary, average, individual Australians and ordinary, average Australian families. The Leader of the Opposition once again is very strong on abuse and on personal invective but very light on proper, objective analysis.
I reject completely the charge made by the Leader of the Opposition that this Government's policies have not been directed towards the interests of ordinary, average Australian families. I condemn the unwillingness of the Leader of the Opposition to recognise the contribution that wage increases have made not only to our present high level of inflation but also to the unemployment which is occurring around Australia at present. The way the Leader of the Opposition talks, one would imagine that Australia is the only country in the industrialised world that has a high level of unemployment at present. Does he tell us that it has reached 13 per cent in the United Kingdom, something like 12.2 per cent in Canada, something like 8 per cent in West Germany which, until recently, had a social democratic government, and about 9 per cent in France, which has a socialist government, and indeed that it is at comparable levels in many other parts of Western Europe? Yet he comes into this House and berates us and our policies without making any reference to that point.
There can be no doubt that Australia is now, in common with the rest of the industrialised world, experiencing a very severe recession. That recession hit Australia later but it is not hitting us any less hard because of the timing difference. That has inevitably meant that our growth prospects have slowed quite dramatically, but although much of the blame for the economic downturn in Australia is the result of this economic recession we must not blind ourselves to the fact that home-grown factors have played a major role. When international circumstances are running against us it is not the time to adopt domestic policies which will exacerbate rather than reduce those adverse international economic pressures. The P?!icies advocated by the Leader of the OpposltlOn-:-~he policies of a higher deficit, of a more permiSSive fiscal and monetary policy, of

Fraser Government

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2495

exchange rate policies that would be more inflationary than at present and of a general approach to wages which would inevitably underwrite a larger increase in wages in the future-are not the alternative policies that this country needs at present.
I do not for a moment pretend that the Government draws anything other than great concern from and is disturbed by the inflation figure that was revealed yesterday, but to suggest that the alternative to the policies that are being followed by the Government is to adopt policies which would be more inflationary rather than less inflationary is, in my view, absurd, If the policies advocated by the Leader of the Opposition were in fact to be implemented I put it to the House that we would have not only higher unemployment but also higher inflation,

PERSONAL EXPLANATIONS
Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth)-Mr Speaker, I seek leave to make a personal explanation.
Mr SPEAKER-I think the honourable gentleman would be wiser to ask for my indulgence rather than to seek leave. Does the honourable gentleman claim to have been misrepresented?
Mr COLEMAN-Yes, Mr Speaker.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member may proceed.
Mr COLEMAN-Last Friday, 22 October, the Melbourne Age published a report of a speech I had made during the adjournment debate the preceding Tuesday. In that speech I attempted to draw to the attention of the House the sacking by the Age of a journalist who is a respected media critic and to what I believe to be the political implications of that sacking. I said:
I do not know as a matter of hard fact precisely why hec-
That is, Anthony McAdam--
was sacked, but many observers-I agree with them-believe the sacking has all the signs of a leftist operation against an independent critic . . . In short, it looks as though the Left was out to get McAdam and succeeded.
The report of this speech appearing in the Age of last Friday contained an editor's note which said that the sacking of McAdam was simply part of an economy drive. It said I was 'misinformed' and described my comments as 'totally incorrect', 'wholly inaccurate' and 'scurrilous'. In preparing my adjournment speech on Tuesday, 19 October, I discussed the issue with a number of academics, journalists and public figures in Melbourne who were concerned about it, many of whose letters to

the editor the Age had refused to publish. I had several long discussions with Mr McAdam who also allowed me to read copies of correspondence that he had with the Editor and the Assistant Editor of the Age. I also examined the files of the Age 'Green Guide' and the correspondence that the 'Green Guide' chose to publish about McAdam's column. On the basis of these inquiries I am satisfied that it is reasonable to say that in the past 18 months, since McAdam began criticising terrorist Palestine Liberation Organisation and Irish Republican Army propaganda on the so-called community stations 3CR and 3RRR and leftist propaganda on some Australian Broadcasting Commission programs, he has been harassed professionally and censored politically before being sacked. I believe there is strong evidence that the motivation for the sacking was political--
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman is now arguing his case.
Mr COLEMAN-I do not therefore accept the Editor's statement that my speech was inaccurate, misinformed or scurrilous, or that the reason of the Age for the sacking of McAdam was an economy drive. I can see no economy when one freelance writer is replaced by another. In concluding, let me say that I raised this matter last Tuesday, not out of corRlern at any unfortunate plight of McAdam who is well able to look after himself, but rather out of concern at the unfortunate condition of the Age which has such difficulty in publishing the work of an effective and popular critic ofleftist orthodoxies.

NEW FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENTS FOR THE PARLIAMENT
Mr SPEAKER-Since becoming Speaker, have asserted that it is necessary for the Parliament to control its own financial and staffing resources so that it can exercise its role in the tripartition of power in accordance with the practice of parliamentary democracy and the provisions of the Australian Constitution. As a former Treasurer, I knew that the estimates for the House of Representatives and the other parliamentary departments were submitted by officers of the Parliament to officers of the Executive department responsible for financial control, initially the Treasury, and now the Department of Finance. Those estimates were then negotiated and finalised at that officer level. This was detrimental to the Parliament because the officers of the Parliament were not able to withstand the pressures that the Executive's strength was able to assert over them. Following this process the final figures were given Cabinet endorsement and included in the main Appropriation Bill for the ordinary annual services of government in the same manner as the proposed expenditures for Executive departments.
This process clearly did not accord Parliament its proper constitutional position. Therefore I directed, with the concurrence of Senator Sir Condor Laucke, President of the Senate, that the estimates were not to be discussed by any officer of the Parliament with any officer of an Executive

2502 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Financial Arrangementsfor Parliament

department. Specifically, they were not to negotiate in any sense. However, the officers were to provide fully any information requested by the Executive departments. We, that is the President and I, informed the Prime Minister of this decision and we suggested a changed procedure for determining the parliamentary departments'
estimates.
The procedure I proposed was that the Presiding Officers should deal directly with the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). The Prime Minister instead requested that we agree to discussions with the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) and the Leader of the Government in the Senate (Senator Sir John Carrick). This was done and it resulted in the following procedure being adopted for Parliament's estimates for the financial years 1980-81 and J98 J-82:
The Presiding Officers proposed bids of estimated expenditure directly to the Minister for Finance;
If any bids were questioned, they would be discussed between the Presiding Officers and the Leader of the House and the Leader of the Government in the Senate;
There would be no alteration of the Presiding Officer's estimates without consultation.
It was also agreed that Parliament's proposed expenditures would be listed in a truncated form in the Appropriation Bill so as to provide Parliament with greater flexibility in financial administration. Further discussions were to be held in relation to subsequent years.
While these arrangements recognised Parliament's constitutional position they did not fully establish the proper role for the Parliament. Accordingly, I continued to argue that, within the context of the Government's main Appropriation Bill, a single-line entry for the estimates of the Parliament should be a first stage, but that the best solution was the introduction of a separate parliamentary appropriation Bill funding the Parliament separate from the ordinary annual services of government.
Late in 1981 and early in 1982 events occurred which precipitated major changes. On two occasions the Executive interfered with the Parliament's funding without the agreed consultation taking place. In seeking and obtaining restoration of the funds and of the arrangement, I stated on several occasions that, in light of these develop- ~ents and if the negotiated arrangement contmued to be abrogated, the Parliament would have to reconsider its position. I indicated that the current agreement was a compromise. As such,

the agreement fell short of independence. Parliament had honoured its obligation. If the agreement was not honoured by all parties, the Parliament would be forced to re-examine its position.
During this time. I had appeared before the Senate Select Committee on Parliament's Appropriations and Staffing and presented to it the views which I have just stated. Soon after, that Senate Select Committee reported and recommended sweeping changes, incorporating many of those which I had been advocating, to ensure the independence of the Parliament.
I welcomed the Government response to the Senate Committee report. The Government agreed to the introduction of a separate Bill to provide for Parliament's funding, commencing with the financial year 1982-83. Discussions were then held between the Leader of the House, the Leader of the Government in the Senate, Mr President and myself to formalise the arrangement. The financial procedures which have been adopted to operate commencing with this year are as follows: Within the Department of the House of Representatives the Clerk will be responsible for the formulation of the estimates in the same way as hitherto. Those estimates will then be subject to the approval of Mr Speaker who will examine them closely to ensure that they are properly based. Within the departments of the Library, Joint House and Hansard the permanent heads will be responsible for the formulation of the estimates in the same way as hitherto. Those estimates will then be subject to the approval of Mr Speaker and Mr President who will examine them closely to ensure that they are properly based. In relation to the Department of the Senate a different estimating procedure has been adopted which accords with the recommendations of the Senate Select Committee. That procedure was not favoured by the Executive Government or by me in relation to the House or the joint parliamentary departments.
This procedure I have described will occur at the same time that the annual estimates for the ordinary annual services of government are being prepared by the executive departments for incorporation in the main Appropriation Bill for the year. The Presiding Officers, the Leader of the House and the Leader of the Government in the Senate will meet to consider the Parliament's draft estimates. When Parliament's estimates are agreed by this process they are sent to the Minister for Finance to be incorporated in the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Bill. The first of these Bills was presented on Budget night

Financial Arrangementsfor Parliament

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2503

1982. It appropriates sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for each of the parliamentary departments which had been agreed upon by the Presiding Officers and the Executive.
The Bill itself is a major constitutional advance in the financial independence of Parliament. The form of the Bill is a further major reform. The funding of each parliamentary department is represented by single-line totals for each of recurrent and capital expenditure so affording the Parliament enhanced financial flexibility. Further, the Bill provides Advances to the Speaker, the President and the Presiding Officers jointly to enable moneys to be made available for expenditure that is urgently required and was unforeseen at the time of the preparation of the Bill.
The Executive has agreed with Mr President and myself that there will be incorporated within the Parliament responsibility for all functions which have previously been administered by executive departments on behalf of the Parliament. Responsibility for a number of functions has been transferred in the current financial year. They are: Contributions by the Commonwealth of Australia to the Inter Parliamentary Union and the Commonwealth Parliamentary Association; members' subscriptions to the Commonwealth Record; cost of members' badges of office; and hospitality for school parties visiting Parliament House, Canberra. This is a small group at present but consultations are proceeding for the purpose of transferring all functions exercised by executive departments on behalf of the Parliament. Incorporated in the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Bill is the funding for these functions. Discussions are continuing between the Executive and the Parliament concerning the total financial provisions and administration of all functions relating to the Parliament by the parliamentary departments.
With the adoption of these revised arrangements it is important that members of parliament will be kept fully informed of the disbursement of the parliamentary departments finances. Regular reports to both Houses will be made by the Presiding Officers on the use of their Advances. For some time an annual report has been presented to the House which provides information on, amongst other things, the financial and staffing management of the Department of the House of Representatives. In addition to this, detailed explanation of House expenditures will continue to be available to House Estimates committees. Copies of a paper indicating the details of the House of Representatives Department's expenditure for 1981-82 and the basis of the estimates of proposed expenditures for 1982-83 are available from the

House of Representatives Table Office. I will be present during consideration in Committee of the Whole of the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Bill 1982 so that honourable members may raise for answer queries concerning the proposed expenditures for any parliamentary department for which I bear administrative responsibility.
The Parliament has also obtained independence in staffing matters. The staff of the parliamentary departments are currently employed under the Public Service Act, but in a way that recognises the special position of the Parliament's staff; the Presiding Officers exercise the powers and perform the duties with respect to parliamentary department staff that are vested in the Public Service Board with respect to the staff of executive departments. The Executive Government has now indicated that it will adopt measures to transfer the control over staffing numbers, the creation and reclassification of positions, and promotions procedures for all officers except the parliamentary permanent heads.
Many of these measures will come into effect with the passage of the Public Service Acts Amendment Bill currently before the Senate and the proclamation of certain of its sections. However, some provisions, for example, control over staff ceiling numbers, do not require legislative action and have now been put into effect. The measures I have outlined represent a major advance towards parliamentary reform in Australia by according the legislature its proper role as one branch in the tripartition of power under the Constitution.
