Government Anti-inflation Policy

GOVERNMENT'S ANTI-INFLATION POLICY
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPliTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIIar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The failure of the Government's anti-inflation policy.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places,
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (3.44)-The matter which the Opposition brings before the Parliament is an important matter but nowhere near as important as the censure motion which this Government squibbed this afternoon, When the Opposition puts forward a very serious censure motion against the Government, properly brought before this House, ahout a matter as basic as the economic strategy which is applying in this country and what is happening to the Australian economy, it is deplorable that the Government puts up only half a speaker. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) was the only person who spoke for the Government and he spoke for half of his allotted time, The Opposition was gagged so we had only one speaker. Despite the fact that we understood that there was an agreement with the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) that two speakers would be allowed from the Opposition side, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) apparently saw fit to overrule that arrangement. So there was only one speaker from this side of the House and only half a speaker from the Government side.
What a pathetic indictment of the Government that it is prepared to have such a truncated debate on a censure motion relating to the economy, surely the most important issue facing the Parliament at the present time. It is incredible that the Government shoul~ adopt that kind of approach to the censure mOtion and then by way of some sort of make-up to the Opposition say: 'You can have the matter, of public importance, perhaps with one speaker, Frankly, I am not sure whether we are to get one or two speakers. I doubt very much }Vhether ~he ~reasurer or any Minister will respond to me m thIS debate, The Government is really squibbing this issue, We had a Minister speaking for hal~ his allotted time in the debate on the censure motIOn. No other Minister let alone the Prime Minister was prepared to get 'up in that debate and speak for the Government in defence of its record on the economy.

What is clearly happening is that the Government is running away from the discussion on the economy. It is devastated by the fact that after seven years it has not only made no progress but also gone hackwards by a mile. The reality is that inflation is now as high as it was when the Government came into office-indeed, fractionally higher-and unemployment is twice as high. The state of the economy is much worse than it was when this Government came into office. The crushing reality which faces this Government is that after seven years it has achieved nothing; in fact, its achievements have been very negative. It is now afraid to discuss these matters in the Parliament, so important censure motions do not get discussed in the Parliament except to the minimal degree possible. Instead we are allowed to have a truncated debate on a matter of public importance, a debate which inevitably attracts less public attention. That is all part of the plan to deflect public attention from what is this Government's very vulnerable spot.
When the Treasurer spoke on the censure motion he made a couple of rather pathetic points, He said that wages were to blame for inflation. That is a fairly common line from the Government. He ignored the fact that the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) had made the point in his contribution that wages were an element in the inflationary process. We are not saying that they are not a relevant factor to take into account. The Treasurer ignored that point and simply went on to stress that this was the basic cause of the Government's problems.
Let me reiterate the point that was made by the Leader of the Opposition in opening the censure debate. If wages are seen by the Government as being a problem, it is a problem for which it must bear the responsibility. The Government destroyed the wage indexation system, a system which would have ensured rationality in wage levels, Had it been properly continued there would have been a boost to industrial relations. From the time this Government came into office it set about destroying the wage indexation system despite a promise in 1975 to maintain that system. It finally achieved its aim a year or so ago. The Government then had the situation which it wanted-a situation in which wages would be set in the market-place rather than by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission, When the market-place worked out its result the workers got an increase in real wages which was higher than the Government wanted. It then said: 'That is all the unions' fault. They have destroyed the

Government Anti-inflation Policy

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2505

economy by increasing inflation through increasing wages'. That is a system which the Government brought into operation by its own actions. This Government, more than anyone else, is responsible for the destruction of wage indexation. It must, therefore, live with the fruits of that destructive policy. It is just not a goer to say: 'This is something beyond our control. This is the unions' fault'. This Government has been applying a wages system for which it is responsible. If that is bringing about the results which it now does not like, it is something it has to live with. It cannot
walk away from its responsibility in that regard.
The Treasurer also said in his very short speech on the censure motion that if we had an increase in the deficit it would be terribly inflationary. I find it interesting that the Treasurer should say that in the context of having just introduced a Budget which involves some increases in the deficit. If he really believes what he said here today, presumably he is saying that the Budget he brought down a couple of months ago with an increased deficit will increase the inflation rate. He has not admitted this before, but the implication of what he said here today is that that is his belief. If that is his belief, it is interesting. We note with interest that the Treasurer now acknowledges that in his view, the Budget which he brought down, is an inflationary Budget.
A more substantive point is that an incredible amount of simplistic nonsense and rubbish is spoken by people from the other side, most particularly the Treasurer, about the relationship between deficits and inflation. It is simply not true that there is any sort of hard and fast relationship between deficits and inflation. I am not saying
that deficits do not matter. Of course deficits matter. They have an importance in that they add to the money supply. One cannot simply go on increasing deficits regardless of their impact on the growth of the money supply and the possible implications that can have eventually on inflation. It is just nonsense to say, as Government members keep saying, that there is some hard and fast relationship.
In other countries it is impossible to see any clear relationship between deficits and inflation rates. A table in the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development Economic Outlook which was published in the middle of the year shows the 1982 deficit for various countries as estimated by the OECD and the levels of inflation as estimated by it for 1982. There is an incredible jumble of relationships between deficits and inflation. The country which is shown as having the smallest deficit of all the major OECD countries-some 15 or 16 countries are listed-is

Australia. In fact, on the standardised basis of deficits utilised by the OECD, Australia has a Budget surplus. This estimate was done before the latest Budget was brought down; it was done in the middle of this year. The estimate for 1982 shows Australia as having the lowest Budget deficit-in fact, a small surplus. It has an inflation rate which is one of the highest. If there is some sort of relationship between deficits and inflation of the kind that the Government asserts, one would find this a rather startling result. On the other hand, Japan has a deficit far greater than Australia's as a proportion of gross domestic
product, but has an inflation rate of 3+ per cent compared with Australia's estimated inflation rate of 11 per cent. Japan is a country with a much higher deficit and a much lower inflation. Sweden, which has a deficit of 5.9 per cent, far greater than that of Japan, has an inflation rate still far below that of Australia. The deficit for all the countries shown is far in excess of that for Australia. The inflation rate, on average, is far below that for Australia.
Let us have an end to this simplistic nonsense and rubbish which we keep getting from the
Government about there being some hard and fast relationship between deficits and inflation. In the end, if we keep on belting up the deficit, it can have an inflationary impact, but it is a much looser relationship than the Government makes out. Other things which occur are very important and must be taken into account. In that regard I believe that what has been said by the Treasurer here today is ridiculous, an absurdity and a trav-
esty of the truth, something which we are used to hearing from the other side but which needs to be nailed in this Parliament.
The Government, in asking us to believe that its economic policies are on the right tram, despite the fact that inflation is now higher than when it came into office, is asking us to proceed on the basis of some blind faith that those policies will eventually work. The extraordinariness of this is vividly demonstrated by some recent comments by the Treasurer, who said that what was happening in the United States of America and the United Kingdom, where policies similar to this Government's policies are being pursued, was evidence of the correctness of the Fraser Government's policies because in those countries the inflation rate was coming down. He says that if we can only stick with these policies we can be like the United Kingdom and the United States. We can get our inflation rate down. It will be terrific.
The Treasurer slides over the fact that we will have a 14 per cent unemployment rate. He slides over the fact that people are talking about the

2506 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Government Anti-inflation Policy

United States economy absolutely collapsing. He says that the policies we are pursuing are right. Of course they are not bringing the inflation rate down now. He says that if we can just create a lot more unemployment that will bring about the terrific results that the United Kingdom and the United States are getting. What an incredible kind of rationale that is for policies in this country. It is as though we should all leap with joy about the fact that we are looking forward to that economy developing in Australia. Undoubtedly if we persist with this Government's present policies we will eventually get up to a 10 per cent unemployment rate. In fact, we will achieve that rate by the middle of next year. We are very close to it. The Flinders University Institute of Labour Studies, to take one independent source, says that it will be at 9.2 per cent by June of next year. Certainly if this Government remains in office we will reach that level. We will most probably have an unemployment rate of 10 per cent, or maybe welt in excess of that by the end of 1983.
I guess that if the Government keeps on pursuing those policies for long enough, if it creates enough unemployment, eventually, yes, the inflation rate will come down. But is that the kind of policy which we really want to pursue in this country? Do we want to go on grinding our young people into the dirt by not offering them jobs and by having people sacked from their jobs to the point where we have massive unemployment and deepening recession all the time as a means of reducing the inflation rate in this country? Is that what we think a sophisticated economic policy is all about? That apparently is the Government's belief. We regard it as one which is absolutely extraordinary. It is a policy which is totally destructive and if it works to bring inflation down, it will do so at a fantastic cost to the Australian economy both in economic terms and social terms. I do not have time to elaborate on that now.
It is true also, of course that whilst the Government has been busily creating unemployment to bring the inflation rate down it has also been counteracting that policy by busily jacking the inflation rate up. We have seen year after year, most particularly in the last two years, the Budget incorporate various policies to ensure that the inflation rate stays up and that the unemployment that the Government is creating does not succeed in bringing the inflation rate down. So the Government has this rather bifurcated policy. It keeps piling up unemployment without an impact on inflation because the Government itself keeps ensuring that the inflation rate stays up.

In this latest Budget we have over $1,OOOm of

indirect tax increases which, on the Treasurer's

own figures given in this Parliament a year and a

half ago, will add directly and indirectly some-

thing like 2 per cent to the consumer price index.

The Treasurer said that some $500m of increased

indirect taxes will on average add one per cent to

the consumer price index. On his own figures

there will be something like a 2 per cent increase

to the CPI from those increased taxes and

charges. There has been also the matter of hospi-

tal charges which have been enormously increased

by a deliberate action of this Government. In the

last year we have had a fantastic increase in hospi-

tal charges which as a result of this Government's

cutback in funds to the States and the forcing of

the States to increase those hospital charges will

mean that the health services component of the

consumer price index has gone up by 60 per

cent-not 16 per cent-in the last 12 months.

T1+hapteirs

a fantastic increase. This cent to 2 per cent to the

alone has added consumer price

index, to the inflation rate.

Here is a Government which quite clearly on
the basis of those kinds of figures has been ensuring that the inflation rate stays high at the same time as it has been creating unemployment to bring that inflation rate down. So long as the Government persists in this policy we will go on having high inflation and high unemployment.
This disastrous policy will continue to force the Australian economy into the worst economic state it has been in since the 1930s. Relative to other
countries, there will be a rapidly deteriorating situation in Australia. Already our present inflation rate at 12 per cent is about 4+ per cent in excess of the average for OECD countries. It is about 5+ per cent in excess of the average for our trading partners. Quite clearly, that kind of inflation rate is very damaging to the economy.

This Government, by ensuring that the inflation rate stays up through its idiotic action, is creating enormous damage to this country. It is ensuring that if we have the possibility of economic
recovery in the future, that recovery would start off from a higher inflationary base. For that reason of course, there will not be economic re-
covery under these policies. This Government offers no prospect of higher inflation without massive unemployment. The reality is that this Government has no economic strategy that .is
worth the name.

Mr SAINSBURY (Eden-Monaro) (3.59)- First of all, I want to say that, when we are talking about aberrations in the Australian economy such as the recent far too high quarterly inflation rate and aberrations such as the far too high unemployment rate in Australia at the present time, the Government has great concern. The Government has had continuous concern about both of these indicators-indicators which, admittedly, do not affect many Australians but which, unfortunately, still affect far too many. (Quorum formed).
It is quite evident that at least the Australian Labor Party agrees with the Government that an anti-inflationary policy is important. The honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis), by some of the confused things that he said, seemed to go along with that. So, we are agreed on one point. I think we probably agreed for very good reasons. If there are any honourable members opposite who disagree with the basis of an anti-inflationary pol-
icy they ought to ask the pensioners, the housewives, the wage earners and the businesses, why they have consistently agreed with the Government in the past few years that an anti-inflationary policy is of the utmost importance to this country. It is true, as I have just said, that in the period between May and August this yearthe period for which the recent figures applythere was a 3.5 per cent increase. This was far too
high a proportional increase. It is true also, as has been said in the media yesterday and today, that that has been due, apart from any other reasons, to two important factors: Firstly, an increase in both State and Commonwealth government charges and, secondly, a massive increase in wages in the last quarter of last year and in the first two quarters of this year. As I said, we are talking about both State and Federal government charges. In that context-certainly in my State of New South Wales-on the estimates of the officials of the Department of the Treasury something like one per cent of that 3.5 per cent increase has been due to State charges. This is a high increase but, I hope, a once and for all increase.
I want to talk at length about the state of confusion in the minds of members of the Labor Party. Whilst members of the Labor Party comdemn us today for an anti-inflationary policy that is not working, the policies which the Labor Party, by the words today of the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) and the honourable member for Gellibrand, would advocate would lead us into even higher inflation. What do ~embe~s of the Labor Party want? Do they want ~lgher Inflation by their own policies? Do they Just want to stand back and criticise us for trying

to bear down on inflation and for not being quite successful? What are their policies? It has been stated time and time again-it has just been stated by the honourable member for Gellibrand-that the Labor Party wants increased government spending fuelled either by higher taxes, presumably, or by a higher deficit. There are only two ways that can be done. What does the Labor Party want us to spend the money on? Apparently, it wants us to set up a massive new Medibank system which would entail spending many hundreds of millions of dollars. I hear from the Labor Party's recent statement about Commonwealth-State arrangements that should a Labor government get into office it would spend a greatly increased amount on the States. It wants to contribute more money towards pretty well every area of government spending. That means either higher taxes-that is, higher government charges-or an increased deficit.
It is all right for the honourable member for Gellibrand to run away from the effect of a deficit and to say that higher deficits do not matter. That is what he said. But what about the effect of a higher deficit on interest rates? What about the effect of a higher deficit on the repayments schedule in each year's Budget? In the last few years we have seen the Government having to pay off an ever increasing amount through interest on government debt. Again that amount will have to come from the taxpayer through either charges or direct taxes. Obviously the Labor Party, from its statements today, wants increased government charges. That and other statements it has made today will result in increased inflation.
What does the Labor Party think about wages? An arm of the Labor Party is frequently called the industrial wing. It is the union movement. What does the Labor Party encourage the union movement to do? It encourages the union movement to look for a 38-hour week as against a 40-hour week, and to look for a 5 per cent to 8 per cent increase in wages with no increased productivity. That is a simple piece of arithmetic. I am not saying that in the long term we should not have shorter working hours as long as people understand that that means lower real wages. The Labor Party is encouraging people in the union movement to push for shorter working hours, which will have an obvious inflationary effect. What will that do to our economy?
Let us consider the people from the mining industry who are in Canberra protesting today. They have been duped by their union leaders into looking for shorter working hours and, therefore, higher wages with no increased productivity. Honourable members should see whether people

2508 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Government Anti-inflation Policy

have been put out of work, as we warned. They should see where the union leaders are and ask whether they are still being paid ever-increasing wages. We can bet our lives that the union leaders are still wandering around in their big black cars and that they could not care one hoot about the unemployed of this country. The union leaders look for ridiculous conditions in difficult economic times, and they are duping the labour force of this country. That is what is happening in the industrial wing of the Labor Party. The Labor Party does not stand up in this place for the working people in Australia, who are being duped. That is what the Labor Party thinks about increased wages. There is absolutely no doubt that increased wages have been a major factor in producing the latest consumer price index figure which, as I have said, relates to the period from
May to August.

What does the Labor Party think about our
currency? It wants to devalue; it wants to increase the cost of imports. Therefore, it wants to increase
the amount of inflation. That has been stated
often enough by the Labor Party. What does the Labor Party want to do about the money supply?
The Leader of the Opposition often says: 'Why don't you loosen the screws on the money
supply'? Of course, he does not recognise that at
present there is enough money supply to go
around. He says: 'Let us loosen the screws'. Let us take all the soft, typical, Labor Party, fairyfloss
options. Just for the sake of a few votes in another election, let us loosen the screws and make it look
as though that is the right thing to do for Australia. What happens when the money supply
blows out? Let us consider the statistics at every time in the last generation or so when the money
supply has blown out. Let us consider the statistics on what happens to inflation after a period of perhaps 12 months.

Will the Labor Party encourage its industrial arm to take that into account in its wage demands? Of course it will not. The one thing the Labor Party has wanted since I entered this place-that is the position from which I can observe-has been to cause as much disruption, unemployment and inflation and as many increases in interest rates as it can possibly manage for pure, petty, political purposes. I appeal to the Labor Party to wake up and understand that we are all Australians. It is about time we pulled together. We do not want this confrontationist business we have had outside Parliament today. The union people have been duped by the union bosses into trying to send their bosses broke. Those people have come to Canberra thinking that somehow the situation has something to do with this Government. It is entirely due to the industrial wing of the Labor Party, and the Labor Party, with all its fairyfloss arguments, could not give a hoot for those people in the work force in Australia. That has been demonstrated time and time again. What the work force of Australia wants is production to back wages. In any economy, until we produce we cannot have the fruits of that production. We can increase wages; we can manufacture money, as the Labor Government tried to do when it was in power previously. We can go through all these fancy, ephemeral, pseudo-economic exercises, but it will not help one bit if it is not backed by production.
In Australia at the moment we are looking at two alternatives. At this stage I indicate that I am prepared to debate the economy at any stage and I am prepared to debate the fact that our Government has an economic record on which it can stand. There are two alternatives brought about by the sorts of attitudes the Labor Party has engendered. Either we will have an even more painful shake out-do not worry, at the end of the shake out we will have lower inflation-which will hurt more and more ordinary Australians, or we will have a change of attitude which says that if we work a bit harder or perhaps even a little longer for the same wages, if we have wage restraint and if we have employers who are prepared to involve their employees more closely in our capitalist system, we can get out of this recession less painfully. We have two alternatives. We will come out of the recession; we know that. This country has far too many resources and far too much innovative power amongst its people not to come out of it.

Government Anti-inflation Policy

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2509

With the way we are heading at the moment and with the attitudes engendered in the industrial wing of the Labor Party, either we will have that very painful shake out-I fear that people who have been here are already the victims of that-or alternatively, as I have said, we will have to work harder. We will have to understand that without production we cannot have the real wages to match it. We have had the real wage increases, do not worry about that. The Labor Party believes the CPI figures from the Statistician so it will obviously believe him when he says also that we have had real wage increases, but without increases in production they are futile. We have been going through a bad drought and our export markets are a particular problem because of the closing of trade barriers overseas. We are heading for a very difficult period.
I want to say one other thing in relation to inflation. In 1981-82 the CPI was 10.4 per cent--far too high-but in the three years when Labor was in power the lowest CPI movement was 12.9 per cent. I want to put that on record if we are talking about inflation. That is not to say that I have yet heard the Labor Party address the real economic issues in Australia.

PETITIONS
The Acting Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:

Australian Postal Commisssion
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The Petition of certain citizens of Australia shows:
I. that Australia as a sparsely populated continent requires the maintenance of a high quality postal service as a vital means of communication.
2. that the Australian postal service meets the needs of all Australians.
3. that any reduction in Australian Post's monopoly power to carry letters for reward will destroy the concept of the national postal service and result in:
Increased rates for mail carried outside the capital cities;
A reduction in mail delivery and collection frequencies in non-profitable areas;
The closure of post offices.
Your Petitioners therefore pray that the powers of the Postal Commission to carry letters for reward should be strengthened to ensure that the national postal service is retained in the interests of all Australians.
by Mr Cunningham, Mr Duffy, Dr Jenkins, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Mildren and Mr Milton.
Petitions received.

South West Tasmania: Wilderness Area
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members in the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned that the taxes of all Australians will be used to finance a dam that would flood the Lower Gordon and Franklin Rivers in South-West Tasmania. Your petitioners declare that the intrinsic values of this area. and the provision of funds from the taxes of all Australians. for the destruction of this area of the National Estate and of world heritage value. does make this a national issue. Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government take all possible steps, including financial and legal steps and fulfil its responsibilities under the Australian Heritage Act to prevent the destruction of this area of the National Estate. and your petitioners, as in duty bound, will ever pray.
by Dr Cass, Mr Howe, Mr Lusher and Mr West.

Petition received.
Proposed Student Loans Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the introduction of a comprehensive student loans scheme will:
I. Restrict Access to Education
People from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts. These people, if they enrol, will be forced to amass loan debts. Richer students do not face this handicap.
2. Undermine the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme
TEAS should cover the basic living costs of students. Currently the scheme only assists 38% of full-time students and the maximum level of TEAS is a mere $49.67. Government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced.
3. Disadvantaged Women
Women students would be disadvantaged by a loans scheme as they have a lesser ability to repay loan debts, because on average women earn less and have a higher unemployment rate.
4. Disadvantage Mature-Age Students
Usually mature age students forego income to study and already have large financial commitments. They could not return to study if it meant incurring substantial debts.
5. Distort Educational Choices
Mounting loan debts would force students into studying shorter courses or not continuing with post-graduate study.
6. Result in Massive Attrition Rates
Students will attempt to undertake extensive part-time work or overload courses to minimise their loans. Increased failure rates will result forcing students to "drop-out" rather than continuing study and increase their debts.
7. Cause Administrative & Financial Difficulties
Overseas experience shows high default rates will occur on student loans. Government money will be channelled away from TEAS to paying of banks. The government will also bear the administrative burden of pursuing defaulters.
Therefore, the proposed student loans scheme will have disasterous effects on tertiary education, which we believe benefits the whole community, not just the individual student.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject the introduction of a student loans

Petitions

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2511

scheme. And your petitioners as in duty bound shall ever humbly pray.
by Mr Dawkins.
Petition received.
Defence Service Home Loans
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That all members of the Women's Services 1939-45 War qualify as 'Australian Soldier' under the Defence Service Homes Act for loans because on voluntarily joining the Australian Forces they took the oath to resist His Majesty's enemies making themselves available for combat duty in War Zones, and according to Acts and Regulations of the period the Military women had enlisted for service anywhere, some Naval women to sail in any waters, with all servicewomen available for service in Papua New Guinea.
As those members of the Royal Australian Navy, the Australian Imperial Force and the Royal Australian Air Force of 1939-45 War who were not sent to War Zones are granted loans, refusal to grant eligibility to all Ex-servicewomen of 1939-45 War is unjust and unfair.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that you amend the Defence Service Homes Act by repealing Sub-section 4 (2A) (b) (d) (d) of the Principal Act as amended by No. 137 of 1978, and grant eligibility to all members of the Women's Royal Australian Naval Service, the Australian Women's Army Service, the Women's Auxiliary Australian Air Force, the Australian Army Medical Women's Service, and the Voluntary Aid Detachment of 1939-45 War.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Falconer.

Telegram Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of ~e'presentatives in Parliament assembled. We the undersigned cItIzens of the electorate of Kennedy respectfully showeth:
That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:
(I) planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
(2) closing country telegraph offices,

(3) increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service,
(4) withdrawing telegrams operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
(5) generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service through centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30% of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of the Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We the undersigned citizens of the electorate of Forrest, WA., respectfully showeth:
That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:
(I) planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
(2) closing country telegraph offices,
(3) increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service,
(4) withdrawing telegrams operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
(5) generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service through centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30% of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of t~e Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Drummond.
Petition received.

Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (4.23)-We are back to the old styie of Estimates debates; the committee experiment has gone. At this time in other years I was critical of the Estimates committee ~yst.em. I felt that it could have been improved but It did give quite a few honourable members a real chance to talk to departmental officials, through the Minister, and to do some searching inquiring and examination of the way Estimates were composed and what was at the back of some of the very large figures. There is no doubt that the reason that the Estimates in that form have been called off this year is that the estimates in this Budget are very rubbery indeed in quite a few areas and the Government did not want them to have the scrutiny that the Opposition would like to give them. I will speak mainly on the administration of the Department of Primary Industry and the standards of this inept, hopeless Fraser ?ov~r.nme~t, particularly the Country Party admmlstratlOn of the Department. I do not think ~ should call it the Country Party because as we
now, It has taken the Party out of the country and at a meeting a couple of weekends back it ~~ok the Country out of the Party. I would also I e to talk about the problems that the Government has had with maintaining any consistent standards of ministerial responsibility.

The Department of Primary Industry is an administrative body. It has gone through 2-!- years of upheaval and is only now coming out of it. I think this is something that has given the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) quite a bit of thought and trouble over the years that he has had to administer this Department. Some of the criticisms of the Department that have been levelled against it by bodies such as the Senate Standing
Committee on Finance and Government Operations and discussion and criticism within this chamber and outside, have related to the problems the Department had largely two or three years ago.
It is fair enough to say that the Department of Primary Industry is coming out of that problem. There has been quite a bit of change in the structure of the Department. There have been very definite changes in respect of inspection standards. There have been changes in personnel and in the way the Department operates, particularly through the First Assistant Secretaries. Part of the criticism which we levelled here was that the FASs at the top of the Department were too dependent on the client groups. In many instances it was very obvious that the Department was not acting in a cohesive manner to give alternative advice to the Minister or was acting in a disjointed way. It is fair to say that the Department has gone through all that. We now see the Department in better shape than it has been in for a long time.
Of course, there has been a fair amount of defensiveness within the Department. Ministers have resorted in this House to not really defending the Department but allowing people to think that all the problems that the Government has had have been due to the Department. That has been very regrettable. We have had problems with Asia Dairy Industries (Hong Kong) Ltd and with the meat substitution, including some alleged coverups arising out of those matters. These things have largely been put behind us. We have had the Landos notes and the report of the Kelly Committee, the Committee of Inquiry to Examine Commonwealth and State Meat Inspection Systems. These matters have been very much the stuff and being of the criticism by the Opposition and particularly by primary producers, who have been unhappy with the Department.
I return to the manner in which Ministers have failed to perform their duties. The former Minister for Primary Industry, now the Minister for Defence (Mr Sinclair), said that the Department was okay when he left it. The Minister said that the Department did not give him correct advice with respect to the meat substitution. That does not look like togetherness between the former

Minister and the present Minister. I thought that the present Minister was shielding his former colleague in many instances with respect to AD!. I urge all honourable members to read it. I will read part of the statement made by the Department of Primary Industry with respect to statutory authorities and the question of accountability. Paragraph 73 states:
Statutory authorities must be accountable to the Parliament and to those over whom they have legislative power to regulate or control. There should therefore be a statutory requirement for audit and examination of their activities and for regular reporting to the Parliament.
Paragraph 74 states:
There are two distinct aspects of statutory authority accountability. Obviously statutory bodies should be able to demonstrate prudent financial practice and management in their utilisation of industry and/or public funds. They should also be accountable for adequate performance of their functions and for exercising their statutory powers responsibly.

Mr GILES (Wakefield) (4.33) Over the last seven years the current Government has introduced a great G1any schemes that are very favourable to the peculiarities of Australian agriculture and the enormous range of climatic conditions with which it has to cope-quite apart from fluctuating wo:ld prices. If we compare Australia, New Zealand the United Kingdom. the United States of America. France, all the other European Economic Community countries and Canada, we see that-this is a matter of grave concern to the rural population-Australia is the only nation that has no death duties. Of course, the same applies to gift duty. Australia, once again, is the only nation of that list of our trading competitors which has an income equalisation deposits scheme. Capital gains tax is a matter of grave concern to farmers and all rural people. Australia and New Zealand are the only two countries on that list that do not have such a tax. Australia is the only country of those nations that I have mentioned that has provision for 100 per cent write-off for water schemes, piping and dams and, let us face it, we are in the middle of a bad drought today. Out of all those countries that I have mentioned, Australia is the only country that has provision for tax averaging.

I doubt whether the farming community of this

country realises the enormitv of those benefits. I have taken pleasure as Chai~man of the Govern-

ment Parties Rural Committee, in watching many

of those schemes fall into shape over the last seven

ye.ars. We can add to them matters such as tax-

atIOn concessions: special depreciation allowances

I wanted today to set the scene for a package of facilities, authorities and concessional treatment of incredible value to the rural population of Australia. I remind the Committee that only a short nine years ago an infamous document-it was infamous at the time; I guess that many honourable members have forgotten about it now--called the Coombs report, which contained certain recommendations, was published.
Mr Lloyd-What was that report called?
Mr GILES-The Coombs report. It was more or less the bible of the Government that preceded us. I think it is very important that the farming community should recognise the sorts of things that were done then that no doubt would reemerge if the Australian Labor Party were returned to power. The provision of free milk to children was phased out. Where is the honourable member for Henty (Mrs Child)? She is in the chamber. Was that phasing-out compassionate? Secondly, the accelerated depreciation allowance for income tax purposes for primary producers was phased out in the 1973-74 Budget. The immediate deductibility for taxation purposes of certain capital expenditure by primary producers was phased out in the 1973-74 Budget. Outright deductions were allowed under this scheme for a wide range of capital expenditures associated with primary production activities, notably the preparing of land, internal fencing, provision of water, storage of feed, clearing, et cetera. Next, the investment allowance which permitted an additional deduction of 20 per cent of the installed cost of new plant and equipment for farmers was phased out in the 1973-74 Budget.
The distance over which rural telephone lines could be installed free was halved. The sales tax exemption on non-alcoholic carbonated beverages was removed. This is the one of all those things for which I have a slight sympathy. In some areas it became a trifle absurd when, in order that people might claim a taxation advantage, fruit juice was denatured until it had no taste and no character at all. What did the Labor Government do in the electorate of Murray? When I visited there huge factory space was lying idle, and it probably still is to this day, because of a complete misunderstanding of the social effects that the Whitlam Government would have on that area of Australia.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2517

The superphosphate bounty was not renewed in 1974. Therefore, the attitude of the Labor Party in the Senate quite recently was utterly understandable. I have even less sympathy for that other party, which three times promised people such as me that it would support the bounty, than I have for the Labor Party. The Labor party's attitude to that matter was forecastable, but the Australian Democrats in the Senate are really quite hopeless. They could not even make up their minds on something as silly as that. They very nearly deprived the farming community of a good many million dollars. This was prevented only by the capacity and sense of this Government to pay back sums of money that were denied at that time. A freight subsidy on petroleum for people who live away from capital cities was totally removed in 1974. How often have we heard people in this chamber try to attack this Government about the difficulties involved in that subsidy? What hypocrisy.
The excise differential between brandy and other spirits was phased out-this was of particularly grave concern to my electorate-in three separate instalments, as I remember it. The Labor Government increased excise on brandy by 180 per cent. It therefore deprived the wine industry at that time-the industry is still in a similar position-of the obvious method of getting rid of grape surpluses in years when they occur.
Section 31A of the Income Tax Assessment Act, which laid down a special basis for valuation of trading stock, was eliminated. Wineries today are still trying to pay off the $22m that was levied on them as a result of that action by the Whitlam Government. One can go on. I have another five pages of examples, but I think I have made quite enough of that point.
I am proud to have worked in close association with two Ministers for Primary Industry. I speak as one who does not belong to the same party as either of them. The Government Parties Rural Committee, of which I am Chairman, has functioned extraordinarily well and, I think, very efficiently. We have had great loyalty expressed at all levels from within both parties. We have been a cohesive team. I would like to think that we have produced over that seven-year period not a rehash of the debacle of the package of Labor policies of earlier years but some semblance of economic sense. I described earlier the peculiarit!es of both climate and overseas price fluctuations that affect the farming community. I am proud to have been part of this effort. I put some of those things on record this afternoon in case I do not get a chance to debate the Estimates again.

Mr MILDREN (Ballarat) (4.43)- [ do not want to make too many comments about the Budget Estimates lest I be accused of being uncharitable to the Federal Government. Yet, of course, if I speak the truth in any kind of comment I make on this matter it will appear as though I am being uncharitable. For instance, very many farmers are wondering why the Government made no allowance in the Budget for the drought, the extent of which was actually already fully known by the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon). The Bureau of Agricultural Economics had obviously been informing him for many months of the worsening conditions throughout eastern Australia. In New South Wales the drought had been evident for four years; yet this Minister made no real allowance for it in the Budget. Why? There had to be some reason. The reason is obvious. This Budget was not intended to give relief to those people on the land who are suffering the depredations of drought. It was aimed largely at trying to sell a few lollies to the people of Australia by way of an election handout.
About a fortnight after the Budget the Minister made an announcement of drought relief sums aggregating about $356m. The farmers will tell us all just what they will get out of this. If that $356m is all that will be spent there will be very little relief for very many farmers. Not many farmers will gain from, for instance, the scheme to send sheep to Western Australia. That, of course, was a latter-day thought on the part of the Minister. It appears that unless they are sold to the west they will not go.
This is a cruel joke. It is a sleazy gimmick to attack Australian Labor Party governments. Primary producers who are trying to survive, let alone to make an honest living, have had their hopes dashed far too often by people who masquerade as their spokespersons. No wonder they suspect the motives of this callous Minister. No wonder they prefer the Opposition spokesperson on primary industry, the honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin). They know full well that he is a fighter for their interests and will speak frankly and honestly. He is not crudely using them for his own cynical purposes as is this discredited Minister. In claiming to represent primary producers the Minister is really using them for his own purposes, and they know that this is the truth. Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (4.53)-The Department of Primary Industry will be required to monitor the position of primary producers and to work out ways and means of improving their lot. The estimate of $124,445,000 for the working of the Department will most certainly be stretched to the limit. Our great primary industries are in dire straits right across Australia at present with the worst drought for 40 .years. In my State of New South Wales practically every Pastures Protection Board area has been declared a drought area. I congratulate the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) for the action he has taken during this drought in providing assistance to primary producers. This assistance is to the extent of $360m. Contrary to what was stated by the honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) that amount is certainly a massive one and in many ways will assist primary producers to provide fodder. This drought will necessitate massive financial assistance from governments and banking institutions.
Our wool, wheat, meat and other agricultural production is being seriously affected. The flow-on to the business sector is starting to show up with service industries being run down and with the resultant unemployment levels increasing in country areas. Our great primary industries are the backbone of Australia, and when they fail we have serious financial difficulties. It is all right to talk about our great resource industries. They are important but the only resource industry that can match up to our wool, wheat or meat industries is the coal industry. The drought will also have a very serious effect on our export income. Right throughout our countryside our service industries, particularly our machinery firms, are feeling the pinch and indeed are experiencing liquidity problems. Consequently they are forced

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2519

to put off staff, thereby creating more unemployment.
International Harvester Australia Ltd, one of the biggest companies in this field in Australia, is in dire financial straits at present, partially on account of the drought and a lack of machinery sales. The Government's drought assistance is timely and necessary. It will bring home to primary producers the need to conserve fodder in good times. That is one benefit of the drought but, the assistance still does not overcome the problem. It should be a lesson to our primary producers to conserve fodder in good times. The Minister at the table has indicated that the Australian Wheat Board will be given authority to import wheat into Australia if it is necessary. Of course, this wheat will have to be processed in order to ensure that Australia is not inflicted with disease,
and that is terribly important.
We are going into the summer season faced
with a lack of water. This is a problem that the rural people have yet to face. Our great dams are down to very low levels. In the Paterson electorate there are two dams, the Glenbawn Dam on the Hunter River and the Keepit Dam on the Namoi River. The water in both those dams is below 10 per cent of capacity. This is a serious situation because it will affect the irrigation of crops and the production of fodder for livestock. Most primary producers will be seriously affected, particularly those relying on irrigation water for crops of all kinds. Australia is the driest country in the world and we should be making provision for increased water storage by the provision of more dams. This year there has been an increase in Federal funds for water storage. The Budget provides $41.7m for national water resources, which is an increase of 27.5 per cent over 1981-82. However, this is not nearly enough to overcome the problem of water storage in this country. The Government's decision in 1980 to allow an immediate write-off of the cost of water storage facilities as a drought relief measure has been a great help. M~ny primary producers have taken advantage of this provision. It is invaluable at present in light of the drought with which we are faced.
Payments in the Budget to rural industries are estimated to total $313m. Owing to the depressed conditions being experienced in the cattle and meat industries the Government has decided to write-off industry debts of $23.5m in respect of
the brucellosis and tuberculosis eradication campaign. This was a matter which concerned primary producers. The Government has acted and its decision has been of great benefit to them. We ~ave been concerned for a long time to have a smgle meat inspection system across Australia. It

has been heartening to ascertain that the New South Wales Government has agreed to negotiate with the Federal Government for a single inspection system under certain conditions. I have no doubt that the Minister at the table is considering
these conditions and discussing them with the State Minister for Agriculture. Tasmania, South Australia and the Northern Territory have single inspection systems; other States have dual inspection systems. Many abattoirs in this country are in dire straits. In New South Wales works have closed down in Moree, Tenterfield, Wollongarra, Guyra, Dubbo, Forbes, and in other places. New South Wales has been overserviced by abattoirs and when a shortage of stock occurs problems soon come to light.
We must look to new markets for the export of meat, and these are limited. With the entry of
Great Britain to the European Economic Community we have lost a valuable market. Should the United States of America market collapse we would have real problems. To give an indication of the loss of Australian exports to the EEC, in 1974 we sold to that market 200 million tonnes of beef and veal. Now our only exports to that market amount to about 5,000 tonnes. It is good to see the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation holding functions in Australia to popularise the sale of beef. Housewives have always supported the Australian beef industry and they purchase in the order of 60,000 tonnes per annum. It is good that this side of the industry is not being neglected. In this Budget a $2.5m grant has been made to the AMLC over the next two years in order to establish a quality control program for meat exports. How important this is. Once again I congratulate the Minister for the part he has played in seeing that quality Australian meat is exported with advantage to this country.
There has been a 68 per cent increase in the Commonwealth contribution to compensation for slaughtering brucellosis and tuberculosis reactors. It is important that we should spend money in this area because brucellosis and tuberculosis cause a problem in the industry. There is no increase in meat export charges. The sum of $1.73m has been allocated for restructuring the meat operations of the Commonwealth Export Inspection Service. Continuing financial commitment to research programs and the development of a system of objective description for carcass meat are items under surveillance. Provision of $100,000 has been made for the purchase of equipment to take care of any outbreak of screw worm fly. Nearly $400m has been invested by the Government and the industry since 1970 to see that disease is eliminated from Australian meat. This is a very

2520 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

important part of the work of the Department of
Primary Industry. In 1981 Australia exported 710,000 tonnes of beef, the lowest level since
1974. The decline in beef and veal exports is
largely due to a reduced import demand in major markets. Australia must embark on an active
meat export sales campaign to protect a most
valuable primary industry from severe competition. The Department of Primary Industry,
headed by the Minister, will certainly take this most important aspect of the industry well and truly in hand, with great results for our country.

speech was therefore not a Department of Pri-

mary Industry document, and did not necessarily

reflect departmental policy. Accordingly, I was

a.dvised to make my own arrangements about get-

When I got a copy of the speech I found to my

amazem~n~ that it carried upon it the imprimatur of ~he Mmlster's Department. The document for which he sal~ ?e and his Department can accept

an attempt by the Minister and the Department

to crawl out from underneath some of the propositions that were being put by Mr Snelson to that seminar. I do not have any objection at all to public servants holding important positions of public responsibility making controversial statements for the purposes of informing the community and engendering public debate. If they do that then I think the Minister has to be prepared to accept that they do that within the framework of his authority. But I believe the Minister and his Department are acting in this way because of Mr Snelson's activities, because he holds a very responsible position. According to his duty statement, his responsibilities are to co-ordinate and to make available not merely to farming communities but also to State governments and other departments the whole area of pesticidal and herbicidal use. In that situation I believe that, whilst he might choose to be controversial-as I have said, I do not object to that-he has to be seen as someone who can objectively advise both Governments and communities on the basis of knowledge and scientific objectivity. I do not believe, on examining Mr Snelson's statements in this area, that they are either scientifically based or objective. I believe there is a clear conflict of interest between the official duty statement of Mr Snelson and his statements and actions in recent years. As I have pointed out, his office is a highly responsible one. He is responsible to see that highly dangerous herbicides, pesticides and other agricultural chemicals are properly governed and monitored and that appropriate safety and occupational health aspects are considered by him.
Of course, Mr Snelson has been employed by the Department of Primary Industry since 1967. He is the first and only Commonwealth Pesticides Co-ordinator and was employed prior to 1967 for 20 years by Geigy (Australia) Pty Ltd, the Australian subsidiary of a firm of chemical manufacturers established in Switzerland. It may well be argued that he is a man with practical, background knowledge and that he brings this knowledge to the benefit of the Department. I believe that any analysis of Mr Snelson's statements shows that he is still what he originally was-a salesman and a magsman for sections of the chemical industry-and that he has not been discharging his very important obligations to the community.
In 1979 he claimed that the public concern about 2,4-0 and 2,4,5-T was a plot generated by the marihuana lobby. This was when the then Victorian Liberal Premier, Mr Hamer, was considering a total ban on those herbicides in October 1978. Mr Snelson flew into that situation uninvited and he was reported in the Melbourne Age

as being a United Nations expert who defends herbicides. The Age went on to say that Mr Snelson had contacted the then Victorian Minister, Vasey Houghton, and his officials with an offer of help to allay public concern over birth defects in Yarram. with the use of the newer pesticides'. In respect of 2,4,5-T and 2,4-D the hazards which should be noted were set out by the World Health Organisation's International Agency for Research on cancer in 1977. They include: Chloracne, liver damage, cardiovascular disorders, urinary tract disorders, respiratory disorders, pancreatic disorders, neurological and psychiatric disorders, vomiting, diarrhoea, hyperthermia and tachycardia. Recent Swedish studies have shown that these chemicals may cause soft tissue carcinomas as a long term effect. Yet Mr Snelson is concernedthese are his words-that:
The involvement and influence of lawyers is increasing. Often their actions appear to disregard need, fact and scientific data and to have little respect for truth, convention or human dignity.
He is concerned to attack the legal profession. He is concerned to attack any group of concerned citizens that has a view contrary to his own. Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (5.13)-1 thank those honourable members who made a constructive contribution to the debate. I might say that the number of honourable members who made a constructive contribution was fairly limited. As one would expect, the honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) made his usual negative speech and made no progress at all in the area of Australian Labor Party policy on agricultural matters. That is all I need to say about his speech. I need say very little also about the speech of the honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) because again his speech was mostly a personal diatribe. I think the farmers of Australia are better judges than the honourable member for Ballarat on these matters.
The honourable member for Ballarat raised one or two matters to which I ought to respond. He talked about sheep going to Western Australia for the purposes of agistment and he claimed that these sheep have to be sold. Of course that is not so. As I understand it four lots of sheep have already gone to Western Australia. I think some of them from Victoria-for the purposes of agistment. The sheep do not have to be sold at all; he has got the story quite wrong. The interesting thing is, of course, that the Victorian Government and the New South Wales Government would not join in subsidising freight to Western Australia to allow sheep to be taken to the feed, nor would they join in a slaughter subsidy. So farmers in

2522 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

those two States are not able to transfer their sheep to a State where there is feed, nor are they able to obtain a slaughter subsidy for killing off starving sheep. I think this typifies the approach taken by the Australian Labor Party and shows its
total lack of understanding of the needs of the
farmers.

In this drought program we have sought to put down the greatest number of options possible for farmers to explore and to use in the best means possible. I have remained fairly flexible in terms
of introduction of new measures to ensure that farmers can take the best of the options available to them. Thus it is we have a fodder subsidy, which the Commonwealth pays. We have offered the States a slaughter subsidy. The two Labor States are refusing to take up that offer. We have offered the States an increase in freight rates and the transfer of sheep to Western Australia where there is plenty of feed. As I understand it, there is room for another half a million or a million sheep. The State Labor governments have refused to take up that offer. That is one of the tragedies of this drought debate. The Commonwealth can but offer; the administration of these matters is for the States.

Referring to the feed wheat price, the honourable member for Ballarat does not seem to understand that the increase in the price of wheat came about because of that fact that New South Wales refused to join in transferring wheat from Queensland by paying its share of the freight subsidy which is a part of the core measures. New South Wales refused to pay the freight subsidy on the transfer of wheat, expecting the wheat growers to pay it. The Australian Wheat Board therefore had no option but to put the price up ra~her than let the wheat growers carry the full pnce. That demonstrates a lack of sympathy by the New South Wales Government on this issue. The honourable member for Ballarat talks about the truth. These are simple truths that need to be stated. They need to be stated and restated so that there is a proper understanding of the issue. As to ~he personal diatribe, if he goes into the farming ~ndustry he will find that farmers are making Judgments different from his.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (5.25)-1 want to raise a matter on behalf of a person named Rhys Trevor Volkman, who I feel has been the subject of malicious prosecution by' the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) and who has suffered both physically and financially over a period because of a very simple set of circumstances.
Volkman was an officer with the Narcotics Bureau. He gave evidence in camera on 23 June 1978 to the Williams Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drugs. In the course of that evidence, which he has allowed me to quote, he said that he was concerned about the administrative ineptitude of the ~arcotics Bureau and the great deal of vindictIveness, shortcomings and and improprieties. That evidence was confirmed. In 1979 the Narcotics Bureau had to be disbanded rather urgently

because of a finding by Mr Justice Williams. It was the subject of debate in this chamber.
He gave evidence in June 1978 which, as I said, was subsequently confirmed by way of a finding by Mr Justice Williams. In the course of that evidence he referred to perjury. He referred to an investigation by Mr James Crudden of the Narcotics Bureau leading to what he called certain things relating to Nugan Hand Ltd and narcotics. He mentioned that he had passed on all that information to the appropriate officer-I will not name him-in the Narcotics Bureau. He mentioned that one purpose for using Nugan Hand was to launder money overseas in respect of the sale of cannabis. One way of doing it was by using a Swiss diplomatic courier. Cash was exchanged for diamonds. In the course of this evidence he expressed also a theory that cannabis was being exported in refrigerated containers. He named the people involved. He said that he gave all this information to his superior. He made the point that no action was taken at any time on the information that he gave. I submit that that was subsequently confirmed by the finding of Mr Justice Williams.
Some recent information has come to light about Nugan Hand. On 22 September last the CBS evening news program in the United States of America showed an interview of Mr Neil Evans, a former bank executive officer. The lead-up to the interview stated that the bank was involved in illegal currency transactions, big-time drug operations and the Central Intelligence Agency. Evans, a former bank executive said:
We were to become the paymasters for the CIA around the world. In other words, we were putting ourselves in the position to disperse funds for the CIA to whoever they were directed.
That interview occurred as recently as a few weeks ago. We then come to the evidence which Volkman, who was a pretty strong sort of an officer with a sense of duty, gave on 25 March 1982 to the Corporate Affairs Commission in its investigation of Nugan Hand. In the course of that evidence Volkman referred to the fact that there was an interest in the Narcotics Bureau in the Nugan Hand operation. He made the point that his informant was Mr Andrew Lowe. He said that his information was reported to his headquarters here in Canberra. He mentioned a report dated May 1977. He said that it was 'the biggest thing in respect of drug dealing that Australia had ever seen'. He said:
. . . Nugan Hand was closely linked to intelligence sources and our particular assistant secretary and director . . .

Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (5.35)-1 wish to speak about a matter that took place in 1979. This matter came to my attention because it was raised in a newspaper article appearing in the weekly newspaper, the National Times. It was a case involving a Mr McInnis who was charged with rape in Western Australia. The reports of that case indicated that ultimately he was convicted in proceedings in which he was legally unrepresented. He thought that he had made arrangements for legal representation but, at the last minute, his counsel withdrew because he was inadequately instructed. Presumably that meant that legal aid had been denied to him in those proceedings. But the proceedings went on without that gentleman being legally represented, even though he wished to be. The matter was the subject of certain appeals. It went to the full court of the High Court of Australia ultimately.
A number of observations were made in that case on the principles governing an accused's right to legal representation in a trial on serious charges. Although each of the justices who sat on Mr Mcinnis's appeal acknowledged the importance of persons accused of a serious crime having the advantage of counsel to assist them before the courts, the Court ruled by a majority of four to one that an accused in Australia does not have the right to present his case by counsel provided at public expense. The Court further found on the facts of the case that the trial judge's refusal to grant an adjournment to allow Mr McInnis to obtain legal representation did not amount to a miscarriage of justice.
I must say that I worried about that case when 1 read it. I have had the Commonwealth's position examined to see whether we can remedy that matter in relation to offences, against Commonwealth laws at least. I would like to think that the matter could also be remedied in relation to State laws. It seems clear to me that for indictable offences against Commonwealth law the Federal Parliament may, by virtue of the power conferred on the Commonwealth parliament under section 77 (iii) of the Constitution, enact legislation requiring that the availability of representation be a prerequisite to trials commencing. The existing Commonwealth law on this matter is contained in s~ction 69 (3) of the Judiciary Act. 1 do not partIcularly wish to quote that section but it provides a. discretion on matters of the provision of legal asSistance. The section contains a requirement that

legal representation is desirable in the interests of justice.
It seems to me that it would be a simple matter to delete from that section the permissive words and to substitute mandatory words. The section could state that the court shall adjourn a matter in proceedings in which the court thinks fit in order to permit a person to be legally represented. It seems to me that only a simple amendment is needed to the sections requiring representation for accused persons. I understand that some 350 indictable offences specifically created by Commonwealth legislation might well be effected by this provision. 1commend to the Acting Attorney General (Mr N. A. Brown) an amendment to the Judiciary Act to ensure that in Commonwealth matters in which indictable offences are involved people are represented.
I think it also behoves the Commonwealth to examine this matter at meetings of Commonwealth and State Attorneys to ensure that in relation to indictable offences in the States legal aid is provided and that proceedings will not go on in serious cases where people have sought representation for one reason or another and have not been given that opportunity. It may well be that the Commonwealth will have to examine its power to require States to offer legal asistance. I understand that we have recently ratified the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights. That Covenant has some provisions relating to criminal matters which suggest that there ought to be specific provision for legal representation in such matters. Whilst I am not suggesting that we should compel the States at this stage to make such provision, 1 think it is a matter that could be raised usefully by the Commonwealth Attorney-General with the States attorneysgeneral to see what can be done in a co-operative way. Ultimately, if nothing can be done in a cooperative way, other means might have to be examined. I commend that proposal to the Attorney in this Estimates debate.

Mr MOORE (Ryan) (5.49)-1 thank the honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock) for making some time available to me to speak. I want to make a few comments at the end of the debate on the Estimates for the Attorney General's Department in relation to corporate law. For some time. I have been concerned with

the question of proprietary companies within the
structure in Australia and the need for proprietary companies to be looked at in relation to the opportunities they offer for manipulation and the opportunities that are available for operators of proprietary companies to walk away from their responsibilities.
Some time ago I asked a question in the House in relation to $2 companies, and I thought that this might be an occasion to develop it. It is a de-
vice which might well be looked at by the National Companies and Securities Commission to ensure that the minimum subscription on the formation of a proprietary company is raised to something substantial-in other words, of the order of $1 O,OOO-and that this be associated with some rating in relation to the business or assets held by that proprietary company. By doing this we would certainly not be enforcing any great hardship on the small proprietary owner because $10,000 would certainly be a fairly small consideration. On top of that, it would ensure that consumers or the people who deal with these proprietary companies, are given adequate protection and that people do not walk away from their responsibilities or liabilities, as has occurred. For
example, in Germany a rating system is applied to the formation of proprietary companies. It is related to industry structures. I do not advocate that but I do advocate a look at the minimum subscription, and I suggest that some sliding scale be introduced to wrap in the question of assets. On top of that, the use of personal guarantees would be advantageous, I believe, to ensure that the consumer is not touched in these areas.
The other matter I want to raise is that of franchising, which is fairly new to the Australian
scene. In the United States today 30 per cent of all retail outlets are franchise operations. In Australia the figure is approaching 20 per cent, and it is a trend which in my judgment is likely to increase. Today there is no codification of franchising laws. The only operation under which they are held up is that of contract law and false advertising. As a consequence of that and the growth in this area and because of the appeal of franchising to small business in particular, the avenues for misrepresentation are substantial. The effect of contract law is after the event; it is not prior to the event. It is because of this that we need to look at the question of franchising in Australia. It is far better that when we do this the Securities Commission be once again brought into it. It is better for there to be a national approach rather than a State approach.
In days gone by, on the retirement of Dr Pengilley from the Trade Practices Commission,

2528 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

the Department and I asked Dr Pengilley to write a report on franchising. That report is with the Department and I urge that it be published. It would show to all concerned the role and extent of franchising and how Dr Pengilley sees the development of such an overall scheme in Australia. The question is not only one of the suitability of the franchisee, which in itself is very much a case of capitalisation, management and marketing; it is also one of a review of the role of the franchisor. In many cases that is not looked at adequately by the people entering into the contract. Some very deceitful practices are carried out in the representation of the product, or in the requirements that are laid upon the franchisee to take out these agreements. At present there is no codification at all covering franchising, and I urge that in terms of the Pengilley report the Securities Commission look at that matter.
Finally, I briefly draw attention to the role of the futures market in Australia. This is an area with which neither a State government nor the Securities Commission has yet come to grips. It has developed in Australia from the greasy wool futures market of years ago to the quite sophisticated operation that it is today, with a high use by both commodity traders and citizens. I believe the accounts and clearance house operation of the futures market is carried out by a highly reputable London-based firm. It is of concern that the high risk margins taken in futures trading are subject to control only by the futures exchange. Whilst I am a supporter of self-regulation, I am increasingly of the view that it is sometimes necessary for an overall view, rather than that of a particular State. I would like to see not the views of New South Wales, but a national approach imposed on the futures market so that in future, should the national futures exchange develop, it will be able to carry out its work in other States on a similar basis. It is a market that will grow. It has national significance and, because of that, it ought to have a national interest. I move:
That the question be now put.
