Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect) (8.0)-Before speaking on the main topic of my remarks I would like to congratulate the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Hodges) on the decision he took in the Pochi case yesterday. To me it is quite obvious that a person who has been here for 20-odd years and who had applied for naturalisation, for Australian citizenship, and was denied
that citizenship only as a result of a departmental mistake, clerical or otherwise, is for all practical purposes an Australian. I think it is fair enough that the High Court of Australia gave the judgment which it did. It seemed reasonable on legal grounds, but I think the person concerned was dealt with by the law in the way in which any other Australian should be dealt with. It is pleasing to see that the Minister showed some humanity.
A large proportion of the electorate which I represent is within the Fairfield City Council area. The Fairfield City Council area has one of the highest migrant populations in Australia. At the last census of the order of 30 per cent of all the residents of the Fairfield City Council area were born in non-English speaking countries. This is a very high proportion, especially if we add to it the Australian born children of those people. Lately a very high proportion of migrants coming to New South Wales has moved into that area. For the last three years of the order of 15 per cent of all migrants coming into New South Wales have moved into the Fairfield City Council area. This large influx of migrants, a large proportion of Whom are Indo-Chinese refugees, into part of the electorate has created some difficulties. I must pay tribute to the local council, the State Government and, for that matter, the Commonwealth Government, and especially to the local newspapers and the media generally for not playing up the racial problems which have arisen. I think it is obvious to all of us that there are people in the community Who want to play up friction on the basis of racial background. Friction exists in all communities. I think it is very counter-productive from Australia's point of view if racial friction is played

up. As I have said, I pay tribute to everybody concerned for not playing up the friction in that area.
We have had many waves of migrants into the general area. One of the original waves consisted of Italians and they still represent the largest proportion of the non-English speaking migrants in the area. Since then we have had many Maltese migrants; Spanish speaking migrants, many of them from South America; and refugees from both kinds of dictatorship, from the Left and from the Right. When somebody comes in from Chile we do not know whether he is a refugee from Allende or from Pinochet. We have welcomed them all. They have integrated into the community and we are pleased to have them. As I have said, the biggest influx into the area lately has been that of the Indo-Chinese. In turn they are being overshadowed now by refugees who were in many ways the original refugees from Eastern Europe. We still have a fairly large Russian community in the area and now we are receiving large numbers of Poles.
While overseas three and a half weeks ago I took the opportunity to go to a refugee camp at a place called Traiskirchen, which is some thirty kilometres outside Vienna. I spent the day there with our immigration officers. Austria has been the first haven for refugees from Eastern Europe for a long time. There has been much pressure on the Austrian people and on the Austrian Government. I think the so-called free world should pay tribute to the Austrians for what they have done. About nine months ago there were 32,000 registered refugees in Austria-mainly Poles. A number of countries have taken quite a significant number of these refugees. Australia has taken over 5,000 during the last year, Canada and the United States of America have taken similar numbers, and many others have gone to other countries. The number is now down to something of the order of 12,000.
People in Europe are concerned that there may be more large scale problems developing in Eastern Europe and that large numbers of new refugees may try to leave Eastern Europe. People who should know claim that economic conditions at present are extremely bad not only in Poland but also in Romania and Czechoslovakia. It is quite likely that a situation such as the one in Poland will develop in those other two countries-Czechoslovakia has a common border with Austria and large numbers of people may try to leave that country and cross into Austria. Undoubtedly, Australia will again be called upon to be a permanent refuge for those people.

2530 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

One problem that has arisen, as all of us know, is that of the definition of 'refugee' and whether it only includes people whose lives are in actual danger or also people who suffer from more generalised repression, people who cannot, as we say, cop living under a dictatorship any more. That is a difficult decision which our immigration officers try to make. The officers I saw in action near Vienna certainly seemed to be concerned Australians trying to help refugees on the one hand and trying to promote this country's interests on the other hand. I pay tribute to them.
I should like to do something which I have done many times before-that is, criticise most Australian politicians for their attitudes to so-called ethnics. There is a belief amongst most of what I call Anglo-Australian politicians that there is some magical godfather in the migrant community who can deliver the votes of Italians, Greeks, Lebanese, Maltese or whatever. They go to different clubs and they think that if they are endorsed by the secretary, the president or whoever it is, he can deliver the votes of all the members of the Marconi Club or the APIA Club or whatever it is. They cannot get votes in this way any more than if they go to a Returned Services League club or a rugby league club. People vote I think it is an extremely good thing-for other reasons.
I think it is racist and paternalistic for Australian politicians to believe that they can get votes in that way. They are convinced by what I call professional ethnics. By that I do not mean that they are professionals who are also ethnics, though in some cases the two correspond, but profeSSIOnal ethnics who make their living out of an ~~hnic community. They try to maintain their posItIOn .by making sure that the people learn as little English as possible so that they can sell them newspape.rs, radio advertising, travel services, legal serVices, medical services and whatever else i~ going. They try to keep those people as a particular ethnic community. Members of this chamber are Obviously aware of the fact that I came from a non-English speaking country when I was a~out 13 years old. I still feel strongly that the most. Important thing for migrants is to learn English. T~e .only way they will get a fair share of ~he cake-It IS a fairly large cake in Australia-is ~f they, for practical purposes, become assimilated IOto the community.
The CHAIRMAN--Orderl The honourable member's time has expired. .
Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (8.10)-1 commend the remarks of the honourable member for

Prospect (Dr Klugman). I have also had the opportunity of seeing the Traiskirchen refugee camp in Austria. I very much endorse the honourable member's remarks and I have written some comments of my own on that location. He commended the role of the Austrian Government in assisting to overcome some very difficult problems. One of the fascinating observations I made when I was at Traiskirchen was the extent to which people not only from eastern European countries but also from countries in surprising parts of the world found their way to Austria for refuge. The camp has people from such surprising placesas--
Dr Klugman-Uganda.
Mr RUDDOCK-It has people from Uganda and Eritrea. I think I met people from Ethiopia. There were also people from Afghanistan. People who were worried about persecution in their own countries were seeking a place of refuge. I think it is disappointing that they have to go to such a remote place for them to find sanctuary. I want to comment on the fact that the estimates for the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs and the Department of Aboriginal Affairs have been grouped together for discussion. I do not believe it is appropriate that those estimates be discussed simultaneously. I do not believe one could establish any causal link between the immigration and Aboriginals. Aboriginals, of course, are our indigenous inhabitants who were here long before the immigrant races that occupy Australia. That includes, of course, those of European or Anglo Saxon stock who are here in more substantial numbers. I think it is quite foolish to debate these estimates together. I have an interest in both matters and I want to speak on the estimates of both departments. Certainly, a contribution of five minutes on each of them can in no way be adequate. I commend to the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) that on future occasions these estimates be separated because there is no link whatever between them. The suggestion that there is a link is quite inappropriate and it should not be continued.
Like the honourable member for Prospect, I too am pleased with the involvement in the western suburbs of the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Hodges) last week. I know that he was in the electorate of the honorable member for Prospect attending a citizenship ceremony at Fairfield. He had the opportunity of seeing a number of other locations in that part of the western suburbs. On Saturday, of course, he was in the electorates of Parramatta and Dundas, and, I think, in the electorate of Reid. He saw a number of other significant locations in the west.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2531

He had the opportunity of meeting a large number of Lebanese people at a function held in
my electorate of Dundas. He had the opportunity of seeing what I regard as one of the most important initiatives of this Government, that is the decentralised office of his Department which is situated in the city of Parramatta. It is a very important office. It ensures that the services of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs are available to a large number of immigrant people in the western suburbs of Sydney and that their particular interests such as sponsorship of families seeking family reunion and citizenship applications can be considered. One might be surprised that the Minister should go to an office on Saturday morning but he had the opportunity to see in that office a large number of interviews being conducted of people seeking Australian citizenship.
I welcome the Minister's involvement in the western suburbs of Sydney, his interest in that area and his willingness to see those places. I commend to the Minister the officers of his Department, particularly those at the Parramatta office, led so ably as they are by Mr Don Hegarty, the Regional Director. I regard that office as being so important that I think it could very well be expanded and its role enhanced by the addition of further functions from the more centralised office in the city of Sydney. I certainly hope that the Minister will recognise that need and act upon this request. I indicate also to the Minister that the Migrant Resource Centre was a very worthwhile initiative of this Government. Of course, it occupies premises adjacent to the offices of his Department in Parramatta.
I take this opportunity to comment on the estimates for the Department of Aboriginal Affairs, and the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Mr Wilson) is with us this evening. I indicate my concern at the recent protests that have been mounted in London and New York to draw to attention, and presumably bring international pressure to bear on Australia in relation to, the question of Aboriginal land rights. I certainly have a strong personal support for the Government's program, particularly in the Northern Territory, in relation to Aboriginal land rights. I believe it is very appropriate that we recognise the changes in land use in Australia which have displaced many Aboriginals and the way in which our dominant culture has had an effect upon Aboriginal people, their desires and aspirations. I certainly recognise that many Aboriginals have been left on the fringe of Australian society because we have not given enough attention to these sorts of matters in the past.

Nevertheless, I think it needs to be recognised that significant programs have been put in place to assist Aboriginal people. The estimates in this Budget include a sum of $271m allocated for special Aboriginal programs. These programs are available to assist a total of 160,000 Aboriginal people out of the 15 million people in this country. It needs to be recognised that Aboriginal people, as a result of those programs which are specifically provided for them, benefit to the extent of $1,700 each, if we put it in money terms. Some $8.5m has been allocated for 30 community-based medical services; $75m for 18 special legal services run by Aboriginals and employing special advisers to assist Aboriginals in relation to matters involving the courts; and an amount of $51.4m has been allocated to the Aboriginal Development Commission. All of these programs are important and no doubt will bring long term benefits to Australian Aboriginal people over a period. I do not expect that as a result of those programs there will be rapid improvements in health, education or the standard of living of Aboriginals generally, but I think it needs to be recognised that there will be a gradual improvement as a result of programs of that sort. If we are prepared to pursue the question of Aboriginal land rights throughout the other States of Australia with the degree of enthusiasm with which the Commonwealth has pursued it in the Northern Territory I believe that there will be other benefits that can be given to Aboriginal people.
It needs to be understood that land ownership and not government programs alone is still seen as the way to provide Aborigines with the spiritual, social and physical base necessary to equip them to choose their own lifestyle and, by their own initiative, to improve their standard of living and aid in maintaining their unique identity in this country. I have mentioned the Australian Government's initiative in the Northern Territory. If land rights were hastened in other States so that those States exercised their principal role and, where they have land available, made it available, I believe that these sorts of benefits would arise. A great deal of support has been given by the Commonwealth to Queensland in encouraging it to change its approach, and we have seen some advances there. I have said on previous occasions that if the Queensland Government adopted the same approach as the Commonwealth Government in relation to land rights then we would probably not have the criticisms made of Queensland which are made frequently. So often attention is focused on

2532 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

Queensland without recognising that there are problems in other States.
I want to refer to my Own State of New South Wales in particular in relation to these matters. In New South Wales there has been a lot of rhetoric on the question of Aboriginal land rights, but little action, and that disappoints me enormously. I noted in the Press reports today-I want to draw it to the attention of honourable members--that there also appears to be a lack of consultation with AboriginaJ people. There is a report that the New South Wales Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Mr Walker, has refused to meet senior Aboriginal representatives to discuss impending land rights legislation in that State. The National Aboriginal Conference representative, Mr Ted Simpson, mentioned that the Minister was refusing to consult in relation to prospective legislation with the New South Wales Aboriginal Advisory Coul1cil, the New South Wales Aboriginal Lands Trust and the State Branch of the NAC.

Mr LEO MeLEAY (Grayndler) (8.22)-1 appreciate the chance to join in the debate on the estimates of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs this evening. My electorate of Grayndler has a very high concentration of people who are new settlers to Australia, as that area of Sydney has had over a period of years. While the present Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Hodges) follows in the tradition of a previous Minister of being a person who has a very good heart, there are certain deficiencies in the Government's immigration polICY whIch make it difficult for a Minister, with the best of intent, to carry out the functions properly.
I first address myself to the question of family reunion. The Government purports to have a polICY In favour of family reunion. As many honourable members r.ealise what the policy is and what occurs In praclJce are significantly different. The Australian community's concept of family reunion as opposed to the concept of family reunion of many people from the Middle East in particular IS somewhat dIfferent. The difficulty faced by people who are Australian citizens or who have per~anent residence in Australia, 'in sponsoring theIr aged parents to come to Australia is becoming even more acute. There are specific holdups in dealmg wIth mIgrants from the Middle East and Leban~n in particular which are causing great hardshIp to many of my constituents. There seems to be a bottle-neck of sorts in the embassy in Damascus which causes those people to be put to great hardship. The Government's policy of not

reopening the embassy in Beirut leaves a lot to be desired. Whilst the Government could properly say that it was looking to the safety of Australia's representatives in Beirut during the civil war in Lebanon, when one considers that a number of other diplomatic posts have been kept open in Beirut, it is obvious that Australia, which has one of the largest populations of Lebanese-born people in the world-over 200,000 people in Australia come from Lebanon-should have been a bit more careful about closing that post. Great hardship is caused to many people in getting to Damascus and great danger is caused to many people getting to Damascus from Beirut. I hope when the Government is considering these estimates that the Minister will give some thought to approaching the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) to reopen the Australian post in Beirut as soon as possible.
I hope the Minister will take account of the problems that are being caused to many migrants with regard to the implementation of the family reunion policy. It is a policy which was looked forward to by many people of overseas origin who are now Australian citizens. It is a policy that, unfortunately, they have found lacking in its implementation.
I do not think one can mention the question of the Lebanese community in Australia without paying attention to the fact that the Government has been unwilling to grant refugee status to people from Lebanon. That hinges on a legal interpretation of what 'refugee status' is and it hinges on some determination of who are refugees in relation to United Nations agreements. When one looks at the other groups of people who are allowed special status to come to Australia, it is rather wrong I feel that the Lebanese in Australia have not been given that special status. We welcome people whom we consider to be political refugees from South Africa to Australia. We welcome people whom we consider to be political refugees from Russia to Australia. We welcome to Australia people whom we consider to be political refugees from Poland. If one is willing to welcome those groups to Australia, I think the Government should extend refugee status to people who wish to come to Australia from Lebanon. Even the Government by its own decision has made a quite significant donation to restore war-torn Lebanon and has extended tax deductibility to donation for that purpose. Whilst we recognise that the war is still going on in parts of the country, we do not extend refugee status to its people. I consider that most unfair and very wrong.
I mention in the debate this evening also the matter of the adult mi rant education service. I

Appropriation Bill (Na. 1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2533

know that my colleague the honourable member for Sydney (Mr Les McMahon), if time permits, would like to join in on this matter as well. That service is run by the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. There is an office of that service in Belmore in my electorate. It does an excellent job. A number of teachers there who are hard-working-indeed overworked-and who provide an exceptionally excellent service to the people of my electorate are finding themselves put at an industrial disadvantage compared with other people teaching in New South Wales and even compared with other people who are members of the Commonwealth Teaching Service. In a letter to me, a number of people who teach in that centre at Belmore summed up the situation quite succinctly, I believe. In their letter, they wrote:
Were any other area of full-time education to be staffed by such a high proportion of casual teachers working under these conditions.-
By that they mean the part time and temporary employment-
there would be a public outcry. Our public is unable to cry out, and the conclusion is inescapable that what would be viewed as intolerable in any other area of education is considered good enough for migrants.
I do not believe that it is good enough for migrants that most of the teachers in the adult migrant education service should be casual teachers. I do not consider that it is good enough that they should be paid less than other teachers. I hope that the Minister will resolve the disagreements that he has with many of the States about employing those teachers full time.
Finally, because we have only 10 minutes in each contribution to this debate, I raise a matter that has caused me some considerable concern over the weekend. I refer to the report in the Sydney Sunday Telegraph which related to statements that are alleged to have been made by Canon Stanley Kurrle, the headmaster of the King's School at Parramatta. It was suggested in the Sunday Telegraph that Canon Kurrle made a number of assertions about migrants not being brought to Australia in a time of recession. Indeed, it is suggested in that article that he said that there should be assistance to repatriate those people.
I find it disturbing that a person who purports to be a christian would make those types of remarks. I think that, if Canon Kurrle did make those remarks, he should look into his own heart. He should consider that nearly 2,000 years ago Christ himself was a refugee. Christ left his own land and went to Egypt. His family fled the intolerable conditions in its own homeland. But would

Canon Kurrle have set his face against Christ if he had lived in Egypt at that time? Would he have suggested that he should return? I would hope not. When Canon Kurrle came to Australia, no doubt as a migrant, no one suggested to him that he should return.
I think that all of us as Australians should put aside these incredible racist approaches that are starting to surface in our community at present. We should realise that to live in a country such as this we are one of the most fortunate people in the world and that we as a people have a moral obligation to provide assistance to the less fortunate in the world. To provide shelter to the less fortunate is one of the more moral ways of providing assistance. I hope that we will continue in this country to have a bipartisan immigration policy that gives succour to the needy from other lands as well as assistance to the poor in Australia. I think the types of remarks that have been attributed to Canon Kurrle are repulsive; the types of remarks that are attributed to other people who would see a difference in people because of their cultural background are repulsive. I think those people should look into their own hearts and consider where they really stand as opposed to considering their own moral values. It is unfortunate that we do not have sufficient time to continue this debate. I ask the Minister to take account of the matters that I have raised here this evening.

The job is very difficult but at least they are fronting up to it and coming up with many new and innovative ideas for the pastoral industry, the tourist industry and Aboriginal housing.

CONCILIATION AND ARBITRATION (MANAGEMENT OF ORGANIZATIONS)
AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Macphee, and read a first time.

Conciliation and Arbitration Bill

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2539

Second Reading
Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations) (9.5)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill relates to two important areas of the affairs of federally registered organisations of employers and employees. The first area concerns the fitness for office of officers of those organisations or of individuals who aspire to office. The second area involves the use of the financial resources of such organisations to make loans, grants or donations. These provisions take account of and develop recommendations made in the report by the Royal Commission into the Activities of the Australian Building Construction Employees and Builders Labourers Federationthe Winneke report. The Royal Commission was established in 1981 and reported to the Commonwealth and Victorian governments in May of this year. The report of the royal commission identified serious shortcomings in the affairs of the Federation and in the conduct of certain of its officers, about which I do not propose to speak now.
I need only inform honourable members that these matters, which have been widely reported and achieved considerable notoriety, led the Royal Commission to make certain recommendations as to action to be taken by the Commonwealth and Victorian governments. One recommendation was for the Conciliation and Arbitration Act to be amended to disqualify an officer of an organisation who was convicted of an indictable offence involving an abuse of that office, from holding that or any office in the organisation, and for that person to remain ineligible for such office or offices for a period of five years. Another recommendation was made for an amendment to the Act to provide that the funds of any registered organisation, or any branch, should not be applied in the granting of loans to any officer or member of the organisation or branch unless the terms and conditions upon which the loan is to be granted have been fully disclosed to and approved by the membership of the organisation or branch in general meeting. Both of these recommendations highlight the need for the honest administration of registered organisations. These bodies play a vital role in Australian society. It is plainly against the interests of the community and the members of registered organisations if their administration is not competent and honest. The Government does not doubt that by far the majority of registered organisations are honestly run, and not only the Royal Commission into the Builders Labourers Federation but also that into the Federated Ship

Painters and Dockers Union has shown that there is an urgent requirement for legislation to combat corruption in the management of registered organisations.
In framing this Bill, the Government has had regard to the long standing prohibition in the uniform companies Acts on certain persons managing corporations. Similarly, the requirements in that legislation about loans to directors of companies and certain other individuals have been noted by the Government. While the Government does not consider that a close comparison can be drawn between companies and registered organisations, nonetheless the community and the members of these bodies are entitled to expect an equally high standard of integrity to be applied for those who are entrusted with the management of their affairs. Indeed, the Royal Commission into the Activities of the Builders Labourers Federation in making its recommendation concerning disqualification also drew attention to the standards required of company directors in this regard.
So, as I have said, this Bill contains provisions based on those contained in the co-operative companies legislation which disqualify certain persons from being involved in the management of a corporation. I would, in this regard, draw the attention of the House particularly to sections 227 and 562 of the Companies Act 1981. The policy in relation to these companies provisions is under consideration in the context of the planned exposure draft of the Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Bill 1983. It is hoped the the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities will later this year agree to this exposure draft being made available for public comment. The Attorney-General (Senator Durack) has directed his Department to ensure that when the public comments on the exposure draft are being examined, consideration is given to the question whether the companies provisions need any amendment so that, as far as possible, they cover the same range of offences as those set out in proposed sub-section 132F (I) contained in this Bill and to the question whether any other adjustments would be desirable to the companies legislation to bring it into line with the relevant provisions of this Bill. I should also inform honourable members that this present legislation has been the subject of detailed discussion with the members of the National Labour Consultative Council and takes account of their most helpful observations.
Let me outline the provisons of this Bill for honourable members. The Bill will disqualify a

2540 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Conciliation and Arbitration Bill

person from holding office or being eligible to become a candidate for election or to be elected or appointed to an office within any organisation unless a period of five years has elapsed since he was convicted of an offence of a specified type or released from prison for the conviction as set out in clause 4 of the Bill by a proposed new section 132F. The categories of offences are:
(i) an offence involving fraud and dishonesty punishable on conviction by imprisonment for a period of not less than three months;
(ii) specific and serious offences against the Conciliation and Arbitration Act and Regulations:
(iii) an offence in connection with the formation, registration or management of an association or organisation other than those covered by (ii) above: and
(iv) any other offence involving the intentional or reckless use of violence towards another person or the intentional or reckless causing of death or injury to another person or the intentional or reckless damaging or destruction of property.
The first three of these categories are derived from the Companies Act. The fourth arises from our consideration of what Royal Commissioner Costigan had to say in his various reports.
The Government recognises, of course, that there is a wide range of activities which will come within the scope of these categories. There have to be Opportunities for convicted persons holding or seeking office in organisations to have what they have done measured against the holding of or standing for office. Accordingly, the Bill provides an office holder or a candidate for election or appointment to any office who has been convicted of one of the classes of offence I have referred to with the right to seek leave from the Federal Court of Australia to continue to hold office or to be eligible for election or appointment. If the Court grants the application, the applicant will not be disqualified under the legislation. Where the Court refuses the application, the disqualificatIOn wIll continue to operate but it may, if the Court thinks fit, be reduced to a period less than five y~ars. There is provision in the Bill to enable the disqualification provisions to be enforced in the Court on application by the organisation, a member of the organisation or the Industrial RelatIOns Bureau.
The Bill will permit certain financial assistance to some individuals concerned in leave and enforcement proceedings before the Court. The Bill

will not apply to existing office holders who committed an offence before the date on which the Act comes into operation. It will apply to all office holders who commit a prescribed offence after the Act comes into operation unless within 28 days after being so convicted the office holder seeks the leave of the Court to continue in office. All persons who have been convicted of a class of offence such as I have referred to, whether before or after the commencement of the Act and who wish to become a candidate for election to an office within any organisation, or to be elected or appointed to an office within any organisation, will come within the scope of the Bill unless a period of five years has elapsed since that conviction. Such a person will be able to seek the leave of the Court to become a candidate for election or appointment to office. Where the leave of the Court is sought, the Court will be empowered to:
Grant the person leave to continue in office, to become a candidate for election or be appointed to office as the case may be; or
Refuse leave to that person to continue in office or to become a candidate for election or be appointed to office as the case may be; and
If the Court thinks fit, specify a period of less than five years during which the person concerned may not seek election or be appointed to or hold office.
Criteria to guide the Court in dealing with cases before it are set out in clause 4 of the Bill by a proposed new section 132E. They are modelled on pronouncement by the courts in this country when they have been called upon to deal with similar types of cases arising from the operation of companies legislation.
I turn now to what the Bill contains about the making of loans, grants and donations by organisations. There are already detailed provisions in the Act and the regulations designed to give persons having a genuine interest access to certain information about decisions taken by organsiations to make loans, grants and donations. However in light of what the report of Mr Winneke, Q.c., brought forth, the Government has decided that these provisions do not go quite far enough. And, as I have said, we have taken note of and developed a recommendation which Royal Commissioner Winneke made on this subject. The upshot is that, by the provisions of the Bill, all loans, grants or donations of $1 ,000 or more will have to be approved by the committee of management of an organisation or branch, as appropriate.
Moreover the Bill requires that the rules of organisations provide for the committee of management concerned to satisfy itself before it makes a

Conciliation and Arbitration Bill

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2541

loan, grant or donation that it is in accordance with the rules and, in the case of a loan, that, in the circumstances, any security is adequate and repayment arrangements are satisfactory. Consistent with the provisions in the companies legislation, the requirements relating to loans, grants or donations will not include anything done by the organisation to provide a person with funds to meet expenditure incurred or to be incurred for the purposes of the organisation or the purpose of enabling him to perform properly his duties as an officer of the organisation.
Details of all loans, grants or donations of $1,000 or more will have to be filed annually with the Industrial Registrar along with the other financial returns an organisation is required to file. However, an organisation will be able to file these details of loans, grants or donations separately. Consistent with norms of privacy, when an organisation certifies that a loan, grant or donation has been made to relieve a member or a dependant of a member from severe financial hardship it will not be necessary for the organisation to include in material it filed with the Registrar the name and address of the member or dependant. Finally, details of all loans, grants or donations filed with the Registrar will be available for imspection by a member of the organisation.
This is responsible and reasonable legislation. It is legislation which, I believe, the community
expects. It is legislation which any self-respecting organisation will welcome. Organisations run competently and honestly have nothing to fear from it. It will protect a high standard of administration in organisations. I want to emphasise that the legislation does not single out the trade union movement for attention. The Bill applies equally to organisations of employers registered under the Conciliation and Arbitration Act.
It is unfortunate to say the least that the behaviour of some notorious trade union officials may have done a grave disservice to the reputations for honest administration enjoyed by so many other officals of organisations in this country. However, the members of organisations registered under the Conciliation and Arbitration Act are entitled to have protection against untoward activities that might be engaged in by officials. At the same time, as I have pointed out to the House, there are safeguards in the Bill to protect the individual officials of organisations. The Bill is certainly not intended as a reflection on the probity of the great majority of hard-working officials of employer and union bodies in this country. Such officials should not see it as an adverse reflection upon them any more than the great majority of company directors should see similar provisions applying to them as

reflecting upon their integrity. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Hawke) adjourned.
APPROPRIATION BILL (No.1) 1982-83
In Committee
Consideration resumed.
Department of Industry and Commerce
Proposed expenditure, $164,168,000.
Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (9.22)-The Department of Industry and Commerce is a key department of government in the implementation of policies to increase the level of economic activity in our country and thus to provide jobs for all who want them. I am glad to see that the new Minister for Industry and Commerce (Mr Peacock) is at the table to hear the words of wisdom and experience that I shall be able to offer him in this estimates debate. In the last month the consequences of Fraser Government economic policies have been brought into sharp focus. I refer, of course, to events earlier today, when the understandably angry steelworkers and miners visited this House calling for jobs. I refer also to the Opposition's censure motion of the Government in relation to its economic policies. These events are but the tip of a much bigger iceberg of economic catastrophe, unemployment and economic decline.
I want to illustrate just how serious the present situation is. As I have said, this economic department of state-the Department of Industry and Commerce-is an important department in tackling these problems. There were 384 miners sacked in the Woollongong district; 190 workers employed by Associated Pulp and Paper Mills Ltd at Burnie were sacked and 100 more are threatened; 350 workers of Australian Paper Manufacturers Ltd of Geeveston have lost their jobs; 1,700 workers are to be sacked by the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd at Newcastle and Port Kembla; 250 workers by Borg-Warner (Australia) Ltd in Sydney and a similar number in Albury; and 400 workers in the Ford Motor Co. will lose their jobs. I have been abroad representing this Parliament at the annual conference of the Commonwealth Parliamentary Association. These are just a few of the details from Press clippings that were put on my desk on my return. The list appears to be virtually endless. I could have gone on quoting from the bunch of Press clippings that were given to me. They span every Australian industry, every region and every occupation. No industry or job in Australia is safe at this time.

2542 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

We in the Australian Labor Party are deeply concerned and angry about the deterioration and decline of Australia's industries. We are deter-
mined, once we have swept this moribund Government from office to do something about it. We believe that our prices and incomes agreement with the trade ullion movement will allow us to pursue a more expansionary economic strategythat is, a strategy which is expansionary in the fiscal area and also when it comes to money supply targets. We believe also that that policy will be non-inflationary. But in addition to this macro-economic policy we shall be pursuing in government an industrial strategy which aims at the speediest possible regeneration of Australia's declining industrial base. This is another area in which this important Department of Industry and Commerce and its estimates are so relevant. Macro-economic policies that more expansionary policy I mentioned, built on that prices and incomes policy we believe are a key to short term growth. But in the medium to longer term, it is the structure and performance of the underlying industrial base that determines whether the growth can be sustained. Building on the lessons from overseas, we believe that a central focus for industrial regeneration is essential. The Japanese Ministry of International Trade and Industry, MITI, plays this role in Japan and we in the Australian Labor Party believe that we must adapt our existing governmental mechanisms, in particular as they relate to the Department of Industry and Commerce, much further in this direction.
Modern Japan is a world leader in areas of industrial production such as video cassette recorders, facsimile copiers, microwave ovens, minicomputers, semi conductors, fibre optics, robots, numencally controlled machine tools motor vehicles, farm machinery, stereo compo'nents, televIsion sets and so on. But Japanese success is not based on any unique abilities of the Japanese. Nor IS It based on devious or dishonest tactics pursued by Japanese governments or management, although there may be some non-tariff barriers that perhaps I should mention under that particular h~adlI1g: But for the most part, the production of thiS equipment is not based on any devious or dishonest tactics pursued by Japanese government or m.anagement although, as in every economy, ~e will find elements of this.
What ~as been called the art uf Japanese management IS nothing more and nothing less than a powerful set of natIOnal industry policies originatm~ from the MITI body. In Japan business industnal and economic strategies evolve together and ~e expressly designed to complement each other.
e all remember the period as late as the 1950s

when Japanese goods were regarded generally as cheap and shoddy. But since that time they have progessed through a number of important stages of development. From cheap, unskilled and labour intensive activities have developed the highly sophisticated and skill intensive products and industies that Japan has today. The Japanese have continually restructured and diversified as trends in the world economy have shifted. The Japanese home-based industry policy is to accelerate market forces and MITI has used tariff and non-tariff barriers to promote growth. By contrast, our emphasis in Australia has been not on managing growth but on managing contraction.
The secret of Japanese success is really no secret at all. Japan has used long term finance for industries which it believes have good long term growth prospects. It has expert public servants who are continually devising tax incentives, export finance and subsidised research and development programs for industries. Its public servants are expert because there has been a commendable constant interchange of personnel between the public and the private sectors. But equally important is that MIT! industrial groups, associations and the large general trading companies are engaged continually in gathering detailed market information which, in turn, is also shared with the banking system and other government ministries. This is the real secret of the so called Japanese miraclea vast network of interlocking private and public bodies is continually involved in a process of information gathering and strategic planning. This is the miracle that I, on behalf of the Australian Labor Party, want to see achieved in Australia by converting our Australian Department of Industry and Commerce into a body such as the Japanese Ministry of International Trade and Industry.
To develop this point further, the lessons for our Department of Industry and Commerce are clear. We must construct in Australia an equally comprehensive process of information gathering and distribution. This process must rest on detailed consultation with the corporate sector, with State governments and with trade unions and there must be provision for special development assistance based in turn on the strategic planning which the infrastructure and consultation framework make possible. We in the Labor Party envisage a much larger and more active role for the Department of Industry and Commerce in our strategic planning process. I add that, as all experience has shown, only the most committed free market ideologue would have any problems in perceiving the benefits which such a revised

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2543

role would bring for the Department of Industry and Commerce.
We believe that the Department should become much more actively involved in links with key companies economy-wide to implement agreed industry plans. We believe that the Department should liaise closely with the other central Government departments to ensure consistency across the wide spectrum of Government demand and supply-side activity. We in the Labor Party are concerned~indeed I am more than just concerned~with the efficiency of existing industry. We are equally concerned that Australia is missing out on a wide range of industrial growth opportunities. We shall, therefore, ensure that the Department of Industry and Commerce plays a central role in the identification of viable growth opportunities for new and existing Australian industries.
The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Hon. Ian Robinson)~Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Dr EDWARDS (Berowra) (9.32)--1t is true, as the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford) has said, that the Department of Industry and Commerce has a very heavy responsibility in these times. It is true that since the downturn in business activity manufacturing has been going through a very rugged period, both from the point of view of employees, with greater job uncertainty and unemployment, and from the point of view of ownership and management in terms of reduced profitability. It is true also that one is bound to keep in mind the demonstration we witnessed here this afternoon.
The current situation, the significant increase in unemployment, especially in manufacturing, is a very serious one. The Labor Opposition has no monopoly of concern about that. However, it is unreal and simply not true to charge, as the Labor Party does, that it is all the Government's fault, as if by some simple change in, or with different, Government policies the situation would be different and unemployment would be greatly reduced or would go away. That is simply not the case.
I will have a word to say about the macroaspects of it in a moment. For the rest, what the honourable member for Adelaide was really saying was that we need more planning, more controls, more bureaucracy. I take it that that is what he really meant by his references to the Japanese Ministry of International Trade and Industry. It links in with arguments he has frequently made for an industries development commission and an Australian automotive industry authority, and his

colleagues have mentioned a hydrocarbon corporation and the like. I do not think that is any road to a solution of our difficulties.
The basic causes of the general situation, which have been much debated here today, are not difficult to state. Firstly, there is the sluggish real total demand stemming largely from the world recession as that has impacted on our exports and on the resources investment process. Major development projects have been slowed, postponed or even abandoned. There is also the prolonged drought and other factors. There is, then, that sluggish total demand together with, secondly, an alarming deterioration of Australia's international competitive position associated with continuing relatively high inflation, as yesterday's figure underlined, and the upsurge in costs with the excessive unreal increases that have occurred in wage rates, along with shorter working hours. This deterioration affects our capacity to export and more especially leads to a higher level of imports. I use the words 'more especially' in looking at this in the context of the manufacturing industry.
We have a twofold effect of the virtual nogrowth situation in total real demand stemming largely from overseas factors and, because of increased import penetration, a lower share for Australian industry in that total demand. Of course the Labor Party would address the first component of the problem~the sluggish aggregate real demand~by a large expansion of deficit financed government spending. That is not an option beyond the extent to which the Government has already gone, namely an effective 3.6 per cent increase~a very significant increase in real terms in government spending in the Budget, already enlarged with drought relief and other measures. I say that further expansion is not an option because the current inflation rate in Australia and continuing unrealistic wage expectations are far too high to permit any such further government stimulus without accelerating or perpetuating inflation to the longer term detriment of everybody.
The continuing push for higher wages and shorter hours by a large section of the labour force in all these circumstances is utter madness. But at the same time it is not denied, as I will go on to illustrate in a moment in the case of steel, that management also has a responsibility in respect of the only viable route out of our difficulties, and that is cost control. I stress that, because tied in with the cyclical difficulties to which I have just been referring there are also long term structural difficulties complicating the situation. The Australian manufacturing sector is not alone in facing

2544 REPRESENTAT1VES 26 October 1982

problems. Manufacturing industry in most of the advanced countries in Europe and North America have been experiencing difficulties-witness the recent collapse of AEG Telefunken in West Germany, the problems of International Harvester,
and so on. So manufacturing industries world wide face immense structural problems such as, for example, the steel industry to which I am led
to refer in the light of events here today.

total capacity, is extremely vulnerable to the fun-
nelling of imports into so relatively small a market.

An industry advisory council has been established to assist in the process, but let us have no illusions; the situation is enormously difficult and complex. The point I make in that connection is that modernisation is almost bound to involve a decline in employment in the industry-I mean in the long term, as distinct from the distressing cyclical reduction in employment currently taking place <md imposing enormous strains and hardships, whil:h
the Government well recognises, on the famiiIes and economies of the steel-making regions. We have to face this long term employment problem in this as in other industries-for example, in the car industry. It is not reasonable, as the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) has reaffirmed today, to demand a 'guarantee' from the BHP company in respect of employment in return for assistance.

What then needs to be said is that with restored economic growth in Australia the employment that may be lost in these spheres will be taken up in others. While we are currently experiencing a
downturn in activity do not lose sight of the factthe Labor Party is only too anxious for it to be lost to sight-that over the three years to the end of December last the total number of jobs increased by some 400,000. It will do that again as we get the fundamentals right and in place, to take advantage of world economic recovery as it gathers pace. What is equally true is this: if the steel industry and other industries--I have referred to the motor industry-do not change, adapt, keep up with modern technology, then we can be sure that in the long term there will be even fewer jobs in the manufacturing area and lower standards of living for Australians.

APPROPRIATION BILL (No.1) 1982-83
In Committee
Consideration resumed.
Remainder of Bill-by leave-taken as a whole.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (10.2)-1 think it is important that before the Bill is reported to the House certain aspects of these appropriations and estimates which have not been properly and fully debated should be brought to the attention of the Committee. I am seriously concerned that we are about to pass and recommend the passage of an Appropriation Bill which leaves unanswered a number of matters affecting the hopes and aspirations of many thousands of Australians. 1 refer specifically to those people who, this Budget indicates, will not have employment at the end of the year and those many thousands of others to whom no consideration or reference has been made in this Budget or the statements which are attached to it. This Bill is the financial management statement of the Government for the next 12 months. Every provincial city and major town in Australia has significant and serious problems, most of which are tied directly to the failure of the Government's policies in industry, in particular manufacturing industries.
I represent an area which has a very great manufacturing base such as that in Wollongong and Newcastle. People of those cities were here today expressing their anger at the failure of the Government to look after the interests of Australians first. In my electorate most of the industries, as in other provincial cities, are bound up with the success or failure of the Australian manufacturing industry base. It has been gradually downgraded over a number of years first by government encouragement to remove the significant skills of design, testing and manufacturing of initiative products. We now have basic industries under threat. In the next two weeks it is highly likely-almost certain-that the remaining 680 of the original 2,400 employees still employed at International Harvester Australia Ltd, Geelong, will lose their jobs. The Minister for Industry and Commerce (Mr Peacock) less than two weeks ago, shortly after taking office, in a response to representations made by me and many others, advised that the Government would do everything possible to assist their smooth passage to unemployment benefits. That is what this

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATlVES 2547

Government has to offer. That is what is left of an industry of 2,400 people. This year the Government actually reduced assistance to sections of that industry. The Dandenong section of the truck manufacturing industry will be down to, I understand, something like 200 employees within a month. And the Government sits passively by and tells us that it is happy to give every possible assistance, through the Commonwealth Employment Service, to people to register for jobs and apply for unemployment benefit.
Let us get the matter clear. In the Geelong area, which I believe is representative of every provincial city in Australia, some 40 per cent of the junior girls are seeking work, as are in excess of 30 per cent of the junior males. They have no prospects, receive no assistance and benefit from no initiative taken by this Government. The Government says that it is evil to try to do something about providing these people with jobs. Apparently they must make the sacrifices caused by the incompetence, lies and absolute contempt with which this Government treats Australians. We have the Prime Minister running around the country telling us that we must give more jobs to overseas people.
I gave an undertaking not to speak for longer than five minutes, but this is a matter that should be debated properly and at extreme length by this Parliament. However, that will not happen because the Government is scared stiff and wants to close the Parliament down as quickly as it can. The one thing that the Government does not want is the Parliament to operate and its policies to be exposed. In the 1975 election campaign the Liberal Party made certain promises, which I have read. Everyone of them has been repudiated. The price that we are paying for having a government of incompetents remain in office could be witnessed here today. It is the price that is paid by those who have not come to Canberra because they could not afford it. It is the price paid as a result of people being regulated to a state of destitution by a government which protects tax dodgers and does not give a damn about honest Australians.
Remainder of Bill agreed to.
Bill reported without amendment; report adopted.

APPROPRIAnON (PARLIAMENTARY DEPARTMENTS) BILL 1982-83
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 17 August, on motion by MrHoward:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (IO.27)-A number of points need to be raised about this important Bill. We have had very little opportunity to discuss so many items of the' Budget. This Bill, which refers to the operation of the Parliament itself, is, I believe, a matter of the very greatest importance. The total amount appropriated for 1982-83 is $30,208,000, representing an increase of almost $5m on the amount appropriated in 1981-82. I make the point that unlike the Parliament of Westminster it is still necessary in Australia for the Presiding Officers-you, Mr Speaker, and the President of the Senate-to go cap in hand to the Treasury, a department of state, to seek approval for practical purposes of an executive arm of government for the amount that may be spent in the service of the Parliament itself. That raises the fundamental question as to who is the master within our system-whether the decisions are really made by the Parliament itself over its own autonomy, its own destiny and the way in which it conducts its affairs, or whether we are essentially subordinate to the diligent clerks, the over-mighty subjects in the Treasury, who essentially determine the shape of overall Budget policy for the Commonwealth.
I think we have to ask very seriously whether the Commonwealth Parliament ought not to strike out for itself in the way in which the British Parliament has done in recent years. In the United Kingdom, as you are well aware, Mr Speaker, a parliamentary commission has been set up for some years, presided over by the Speaker and including in its membership nominees of the Prime Minister and the Leader of the Opposition and certain other senior members of parliament. The parliamentary commission determines the overall

2550 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation (Parliamentary Depts) Bill

quantum of the vote, subject, of course, to the consideration of certain political realities. It then submits to the Government the amount that is determined. It is the practice to accept the figure recommended. That figure then becomes part of the Budget. But the determination as to whether one is to get X million pounds or X plus 10 per cent million pounds is not a decision that is made in the Exchequer in Great Britain; it is made by the parliamentary commission itself. Of course, if an absurdly high amount were suggested no doubt negotiations would have to take place with the Cabinet as to how far it was prepared to go. Again that would be a political decision; it would not essentially be a bureaucratic decision.
The parliamentary vote then appears in the Budget as a one-line appropriation, not as we find it in this Bill, as something that extends over a number of pages where the various divisions are set out one by one from the Senate, Advance to the President of the Senate, House of Representatives, Advance to the Speaker of the House of Representatives, Parliamentary Reporting Staff, Parliamentary Library, Joint House Department, and Joint Advance to the President and the Speaker. In Britain there is a one-line appropriation as, say, the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation in Australia
under the Department of Science and Technology gets a one-line appropriation, and as the Australian Broadcasting Commission gets a one-line appropriation. It means that there is a certain amount of autonomy within the CSIRO and within the Australian Broadcasting Commission to determine how that money in the one-line appropriation is to be spent.
It seems ironic that you, Mr Speaker, do not enjoy and your august office does not enjoy, the power of determination that is enjoyed by the heads of some statutory corporations inside Australia. So there is the one-line appropriation and the parliamentary commission then has an
opportunity to vary as it sees fit the amounts to be spent within the overall limits of the appropriation for the particular departmentsfor Hansard, the Library, the refreshment rooms and the rest. I think there is a great deal to be said for adopting this method here. I am sure that the incoming Labor Government--whenever the election is held-will give serious consideration to this matter, but it is something that ought to have been considered a long time ago.
I know there are a number of people inside the organisation--l have reason to suspect that you, Mr Speaker, are one-who would like this power to be given to the Parliament. It is about time that the Parliament stood up for its own rights within

this organisation. We take for granted that the really effective decisions are taken by the bureaucracy. The situation that we see in the television program, Yes Minister, is, I suspect, uncomfortably close to the truth. I see even the cynical and battle-scarred visage of the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) breaks into what I take it is a smile, and he concedes that that is correct.
Mr Cohen-It is like sulphuric acid, that smile.
Mr BARRY JONES-Yes, it is rather. I hasten to point out that I will not speak for the full half hour unless there are very strong representations. I wish to express concern about the very long standing vacancy for the office of Parliamentary Librarian left, of course, since Mr Harold Weir retired a good many months ago. Mr Speaker, as you well know the appointment is made by the Presiding Officers. It is not an appointment by the Executive Government; it is made by the Presiding Officers-yourself and the President of the Senate. This matter is something that we have discussed at various times and it is something in which the parliamentary Library Committee has no role, formal or informal. I must say I expressed some concern, as I have expressed it in the Committee-the view that I have taken and that a number of my colleagues in the Opposition have taken is no secret-that the parliamentary Library Committee ought to have specific terms of reference and a set of riding instructions about what we can and cannot do. I believe we ought to have been involved to some extent in the process leading to the appointment of the Parliamentary Librarian. I expressed concern that one of the reasons, I think, that there has been such a long delay in the appointment of a Parliamentary Librarian has been that there is no common view held as to the precise factors we are looking for in a Parliamentary Librarian.
Dr KlugmanHe should be able to read.
Mr BARRY JONES-That is one view. But there is another view which says that, as we move more and more into an information society, formal literacy in the technical and historical sense may no longer be relevant. As I say we cannot be quite certain what the questions are because they have never really been canvassed before the Library Committee. In the year 1982, we are looking for a Parliamentary Librarian who will be supervising the operations of the Parliamentary Library as we go into the new and permanent Parliament House in 1988.
What are you really looking for in a Parliamentary Librarian, Mr Speaker? Do you want a Parliamentary Librarian who is essentially a book

Appropriation (Parliamentary Depts) Bill

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2551

man whose tradition is in librarianship, who has had a record perhaps in one of the great State libraries in Australia or overseas, or do you want somebody whose training is not specifically in the traditional book culture-librarianship as we know it. You might want somebody whose expertise is in information systems, whose expertise
may be in electronic automatic data processing, for example. I do not put that by way of recommendation but 1 simply say that obviously it is one of the alternatives open. It may be that what you really want, at this stage, Mr Speaker, is somebody who is a great organiser, somebody who knows a lot about moving into new buildings or somebody who is an expert on staff relations because there have been some problems about staff relations in recent years. It may be that what you really want is somebody who has a background in management training or a background in personnel training rather than somebody trained in librarianship. The point is that what we are precisely looking for now in a Parliamentary Li-
brarian is not a subject that has been canvassed with the Library Committee. There has been no discussion about it. It is taken for granted.
Mr Speaker, you would be in a very strong position to tell us whether it is correct that there has been a split decision between the Presiding Officers-that you, Mr Speaker, have taken a particular view as to the kind of expertise that is required in the Parliamentary Librarian and that Mr President has taken another view. The result is that, because there is this apparent difference of opinion where it has not been possible to finalise the appointment of the Parliamentary Librarian, there has been a long delay. 1suggest that the long delay is causing some harm to the way in which the organisation is run. It does not do anything to encourage morale within the institution. 1 hope that a determination will be made at an early date. 1 hope, Mr Speaker, that the Library Committee will be taken into your confidence.
I find it a matter of some regret that the view is taken that although it is commonplace for people on parliamentary standing committees not to act on a partisan line-our parliamentary committees very rarely divide along party lines-they may divide along other kinds of lines relating to the particular temperament and experience of the members on the committees. We do not very often divide along the sorts of party lines that we divide on here in this chamber or in another place. 1 think that, perhaps, there was a fear that, if the members of the Library Committee were brought into the discussions about how the Parliamentary Librarian was to be appointed, we would suddenly begin to act in a partisan way and would

attempt to push a particular line. 1 do not believe that that is true. I think that, if that fear was held, there was really no basis for it.
The final point I make is to express concern that the report of the inquiry carried out by the Sydney firm, Urwick International Pty Ltd, on the organisation of the Parliamentary Library and its operations is still something which has not been presented to the members of the Library Committee. 1 understand that representatives of the Clerks have had the opportunity to read the report and that the bureaucracy within Parliament House itself has had the opportunity to examine the report. But members of the Library Committee and members of the Parliament generally have not had a chance to read Urwick's report about our own Parliamentary Library, the organisation which, more than anything else, is able to provide us in a comprehensive way with the kinds of material that we need to keep this institution running. Information is the currency, the capital, on which this organisation runs. If we are deprived of access to information the whole parliamentary institution is at risk. Of the three points that I have made, Mr Speaker, 1 am sure that you agree with at least one. I hope to think that my advocacy might have brought you around to the other two as well.
Mr SPEAKER-I interpose to make a suggestion. While the Bill is at the second reading stage it is not possible for me to answer issues that may be raised. If, on the other hand, the motion for the second reading were passed-I do not know whether there is opposition to the second reading-and the Bill went before the Committee 1 could come into the chamber, participate in the debate and answer queries. This is the first time a Bill of this type has been before the House. 1 suggest that it may suit the convenience of all members to give the Bill a second reading at this stage and go into Committee. Those speakers who wish to speak on the Bill could speak during the Committee stage. If that were acceptable, it would give me a chance to participate.
Mr Armitage-Is there an undertaking that there will not be a gag?
Mr SPEAKER-There is no undertaking at all. If the honourable member wishes to pursue that point I will not pursue my point.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.

The Bill.

Mr MAHER (Lowe) (10.47)-1 comment on t~e Appr~priation (Parliamentary Departments) Blil essentially to draw attention to the difficulties
of honourable members in this Parliament in having urgent secretarial work dealt with. Prior to my election to the Federal Parliament I served for
eight years in the New South Wales Legislative Assembly. One of the reasons I left the New South

Wales Parliament and contested a Federal seat was my belief that the secretarial facilities for members in Canberra would be excellent. My experience has not been up to that expectation. Members of the Australian Labor Party in the New South Wales Parliament, both in opposition and in government, were served by excellent secretarial facilities. In the national Parliament they are a disgrace. On non-sitting days in New South Wales a pool of four competent secretaries was available at all times to assist Labor members. In this Parliament one secretary is available. She is overworked as she has to serve a much greater number of members. As a new member, I receive a great quantity of representations, particularly as the Lowe by-election attracted a certain amount of publicity. I experienced difficulty in having urgent work of the most important nature-such as urgent electoral work-attended to in this Parliament. The secretaries at Parliament House have been most helpful but they are grossly overworked.
This policy of the Government essentially affects the Opposition because it is a Labor Opposition that generates work. The tradition is that Labor members work harder, they receive the representations and they bring in the work. I have experienced difficulties particularly when there is a public holiday on a Monday in New South Wales and I have to bring all my work from my Saturday interviews to this House.
Mr Ian RobinsonHa, ha!

to learn to speak-that is to say, train themselves to speak in this House off the cuff and not from a written speech. I also draw the attention of the House to another aspect which I think has done a great deal of damage-that is, the increase in the power of the Executive and the bureaucracy over the years. Once again this has meant that the relevancy-I cannot put it any other way-of this Parliament is being steadily eroded. After all, if we are going to speak of Australia being a democracy, it is the elected representatives who should rule this country, who should make the fundamental decisions. But more and more it is becoming-I am talking here of what has happened under honourable members from both sides when in government-a question of the power of the Executive and the power of the bureaucracy. That bureaucracy today is in itself virtually directing the Executive and the Parliament is virtually utterly irrelevant so far as decision making is concerned. Unless this tendency is arrested, the future of democracy in Australia is definitely in doubt.
Finally, I refer to the need for constitutional reform. Once again we come to the question of a democracy. If the events of 1975 occur again where a government elected with a popular majority in the House of Representatives, the seat of government, can be defeated-it is elected once every three years, whereas the Senate is elected once in six years and over half the Senate has not been elected at the last election-and if the situation exists where the Senate can withdraw the power to govern in this House, in the House of Representatives, the seat of government, the chamber where the Prime Minister comes from, then without a doubt democracy is dead in this country. That is the sort of situation with a growing world recession-tonight on PM one hears of it as a depression-where one will find that this generation will not cop it the same way they did during the Great Depression. If those sorts of activities and actions are undertaken we are asking for bloodshed in this country.
Mr TuckeyDon't talk like that.
Mr ARMITAGE-I am not suggesting that. I am making the point that what is happening today and what we saw here today was most unfortunate. The facts are that those people who are put out of work in another depression will not cop it

the same as they did in the last Depression. Accordingly, there could be very serious consequences for the Constitution of this country. This is a situation which arose in 1975. As dramatic and damaging as it was, the Government refuses to take any action to correct the situation. For example, it refuses to take any action to ensure that the Senate is elected at the same time as the House of Representatives or that there is a fixed date for elections. The United States of America and local government have fixed terms, but apparently it must not be so for this Parliament. These very important reforms of the Constitution must occur to protect democracy in this country. That is what we all want. We want this Parliament to work. We want this Parliament to be relevant. Until action is taken on these issues the standards of this Parliament, its relevance and its future could be in jeopardy.
