Sir BILLY SNEDDEN (Bruce) (11.19)-1 decided to speak now and to get your call, Mr Chairman, because 1 did not want to be left in a position in which political debates were going on on the Estimates and the matters to which I wished to refer that were raised in the interests of the Parliament were left unanswered. The speech of the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) was made in the second reading debate but 1 shall deal with it as though it were made in committee. The honourable member for Lalor has not fully understood, with respect, as we may say, the implications of the statement that 1 made earlier today. He indicated that it would be necessary for the Presiding Officers to go cap in hand to the Executive. That is not so. A fundamental element of the arrangements that we have made is that the Presiding Officers will determine the amount of the estimates. Those estimates will go to the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) and the Leader of the Government in the Senate (Senator Sir John Carrick). The Presiding Officers and those two gentlemen will discuss the matters. They will then be agreed.
In fact, I am free to say that the last estimates put forward to those gentlemen were approved as requested by the Presiding Officers. At that time the two gentlemen indicated that there was some need for restraint and asked whether it was possible to have some restraint. 1 examined the estimates, found that there was some area in which 1 could pull back a little, and 1did. But there was no cap in ha\ld, I assure the honourable gentleman of that. Indeed, as he himself pointed out, there is a very considerable increase in the amount of funds of $4.7m. That was on a base of $25m to almost $30m now. 1 must say that in all those years when the estimates were being determined by officers of the Parliament in negotiation with officers of the Treasury, the officers of the Treasury simply had too much power for the officers of the Parliament. For many years the Parliament suffered thereby.

2558 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Appropriation (Parliamentary Depts) Bill

As I indicated in the statement [ made today, the first action I took as Speaker was to issue an order that no officers of the Parliament were to discuss estimates with any officer of the Executive departments. That was to be done only by the Presiding Officers. That is what has happened since, and that is what has enabled us to achieve what was contained in the statement and this elevation of the amount of money.
The honourable member for Lalor also mentioned the House of Commons Commission. I must say to him that the system we have now arranged is, in my judgment, a superior system to that of the Commons Commission because the Commons Commission inevitably did become on many occasions an issue between the parties. That was inevitable and is the fact. I had conversations with members of the Commons who were involved in the matter and they feel that their high hopes for the Commons Commission have not been achieved. I must say also to the honourable member that the Senate-the Senate alone and not the joint departments of the House-has basically adopted a committee system which is, in a manner, similar to that of the Commission. That was not favoured by me because I think there are grounds for political controversy, log-rolling or whatever. I think what is necessary is to give to a person who must be trusted--that is, the Presiding Officer-the right to make the determinations on behalf of the Parliament. I hope that I shall always make them free of any bias, as objectively as possible and in the interests of the Parliament overall.
I refer to the one-line appropriation which the honourable gentleman compared with, for example, that for the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation. The CSIRO one-line appropriation is a matter which has occasioned very great difficulty to the Parliament, both this House and the Senate. The reason for that is that the Parliament has not felt that it h~s been able to get behind the one-line appropriation and find out exactly what the money has been spent on and why, whereas with the Parliamen.t itself and its own appropriations the Parliament-the members and senators---are in control, because the Presiding Officer is their servant and they can ask any question they like. As the honourable gentleman would know, I have created a departure from normal practice which I am sure he appreciates because he has used it ma~y times, and that is, the right to ask what is basically a question on notice in relation to parliamentary departments. I do my best to answer fully and firmly any question that is asked.

The one-line appropriation we then have is the proper use of a one-line appropriation. It separates into only two categories-that is, recurrent expenses and capital expenses. Within the capital expenses we have complete freedom to move expenditure around as we wish to meet contingencies. The same applies with the recurrent expenses. So, we have in fact a one-line appropriation. We could not have a single-line appropriation for all five departments because of the manner in which they are structured. I have no responsibility for the Department of the Senate which is quite separate from the Department of the House of Representatives. Likewise, the President has no responsibility for the House of Representatives. The Joint House Department also has to be treated separately. I would like the Joint House Department, the Library and Hansard to make up a single department--a support and information department. But that is a matter for the future. I would like the honourable gentleman to understand that I am extremely pleased with the arrangements that we have reached with the executive departments and I think that a step forward has been taken in respect of the role of Parliament in its position as one of the three branches of the tripartition of power.
The honourable gentleman mentioned the Parliamentary Librarian. I will not go further into that matter. The fact is that the President and I have not made an appointment. There are certain reasons for that which I will not go into at this stage because of the privacy of the matter. It is true that I see the Parliamentary Library no longer as a mere library; I see it as opening out into a massive information centre, an intellectual resource centre, for members of parliament. I see a situation evolving in which each member of parliament can ask his own questions on his own visual display unit and get the answers back. This will happen without any doubt. In the planning for the new Parliament House, there is provision--
Mr Barry Jones-We will not need to come here any more.
Sir BILLY SNEDDENYes, we will. I hope that we will never change the practice of this House in respect of divisions and quorums which enables the Opposition always to force a role for itself and to put its point of view.

regard that report as private. Whilst there is nothing in it which I would not be willing to have seen, I think it would be a very wrong principle for a private report commissioned for the purpose of assisting the Presiding Officers to make decisions on structure and personnel to be made public.

Whips. I see considerable merit in that proposal and I will certainly look at the matter. If ever I have encountered a person who can push his cause, the honourable member for Lowe (Mr Maher) certainly can. I must say to the honourable gentleman that we have very serious space problems in this House. His solution is to move out ministerial staff. The fact is that it has become common practice in the Parliament over 50 years that Ministers and their staff are located in the House. This practice has become so much a part of our system that the accommodation arrangements in the new Parliament House are based on that precept. However, I do not think we can turn back the clock or have such traumatic reform. I do not think agreement could be reached with everybody on this matter. Therefore, we are in the position of having to make do with the space that
we have in this Parliament until such time as we move into the new Parliament. This will happen in 1988 which is not so long away. However, we have space in East Block which we are using and that arrangement will ease many of the burdens. However, it is necessary to understand that for the time being we are limited in space.
I know that the honourable gentleman has problems in respect of secretarial assistance. I know that he would like more help. However, some honourable members have very serious accommodation difficulties, especially those who have to bring staff to Canberra. I know, for instance, that the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris) was sharing a room with three members of his staff-as a shadow Minister he is entitled to an extra staff member in addition to the two he already has. My memory is that four of them were in one small room. It is indeed a very serious problem.
I will do what I can to assist the honourable gentleman but he must understand this point: The staff that an honourable member has is determined by the Remuneration Tribunal. That determination is two staff members. It is not within my power-nor would I wish to subvert the decision of the Remuneration Tribunal--to provide de facto extra staff in Canberra. If I could do so then I would make representations to the Tribunal. I

assure the honourable member that I have already done so. Now that extra accommodation is available I feel that members could have a staff member in Canberra located in accommodation that can be made available in the other block. I think that that ought to be done. I have made that representation to the Tribunal. So far it has not accepted it. I think that it was argued against but it is inevitable and it ought to come about. Although the honourable gentleman pitched his contribution in a political vein I will ignore that aspect. I think what is important is that every honourable member ought to be able to answer the needs of his constituents, which is, of course, the main thrust of what the honourable gentleman said.
The honourable member for Chilley (Mr Armitage) disappointed me. I cannot recall a single occasion when he has done that before. On this occasion he disappointed me because he mentioned that the cost of the new Parliament House had escalated. The fact is that we cannot continue in this chamber and in this Parliament House. Therefore we must have a new Parliament House. If it is to last for centuries-I hope the parliamentary system will last for centuries-we ought to have our minds in the far distance. When the Parliament House venture was agreed to it was totally agreed to by all honourable members of the House. One of the things that impressed me most about the project was that some of the most vigorous advocates for it were the people who will never benefit from it because they simply will not be there. So one cannot say that selfishness exists. Indeed there is very considerable altruism by people who are willing to accept criticism for their decision knowing they are in the interests of parliamentary democracy generally. As to the price of the new Parliament House, I can tell honourable members that costs, in static terms, including revision of plans et cetera, have risen only 2 per cent. Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (11.40)-The Government's handling of this House today has been abysmal. Today the Government ran away from a debate on the economy and now it has brought on at 20 minutes to 12 tax Bills which it doubtless finds somewhat embarrassing and which it is trying to sneak through when there is not much public attention on what is happening in this place. We find this kind of behaviour, to say the least, highly unsatisfactory. If the Government continues to play this sort of game, it will get a pretty tough response from the Opposition for the rest of this week. The Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) has already thrown around threats about not allowing matters of public importance from the Opposition this week. If he continues to play that sort of game, he will have a pretty rough time for the rest of this session. If he wants a more accommodating approach from the Opposition, the Government ought to be much more amenable to reasonable and decent proposals which are raised by the Opposition in a proper way and which ought to be properly responded to by the Government.
The Bills now before the House are quite important measures. They seek to impose a new tax on debits to cheque accounts kept with the banks. The debits to attract the tax will be those where cheques are drawn on a cheque account, where there are periodical transfer payments such as mortgage payments and where there are charges of interest and bank fees to a cheque account. Because the tax applies on debits to cheque accounts, it will apply mainly in the trading bank area, but not solely. Other banks with cheque drawing facilities, such as savings banks which offer limited chequing facilities, will also be sl,lbject to the tax-or at least their customers will. The basic point to bear in mind in regard to this tax is that it is intended to apply not to the banks as such but to their customers. The Government has bent over backwards to ensure that the banks are not obliged to bear this tax. Thus, the principal Bill gives the banks a statutory right to recover the tax from their customers. The banks will levy the tax on each customer's account periodically, pay the tax to the Australian Taxation Office and then seek to recover the tax from their customers. As I see it, the statutory right is not necessary to enable the banks to do this. As we understand it, it is being provided simply to give the banks an argument to use against their customers if they object to being levied by the bank to make up for the tax which is being paid to the Taxation Office.

Bank Accounts Debits Tax Bill

26 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2561

The Government's desperation to assure the banks that they will not have to bear any of the tax is exemplified by a further provision that, if a bank cannot for some reason recover from the customer the tax on his or her debit that it has paid to the Taxation Office, the bank can recover such tax from the Taxation Office. This shows the enormous extent to which the Government is going to ensure that the banks will not have to bear any of the tax. If they cannot recover any of the tax from their customers, they can recover it from the Taxation Office. The Government is making sure that one way or another the banks will not have to pay any of the tax themselves, despite the fact that they have been highly profitable of late.
The fact that it is a tax on bank customers rather than on the banks is a reasonably important point because of the fact that some non-bank financial institutions such as credit unions and building societies are important customers of banks and so will be substantially affected by this legislation. Indeed, they will be more adversely affected by it than the banks. For this reason and for a number of other reasons the Opposition is concerned by this legislation and views it with disfavour. However, we will not oppose it. The tax will supposedly provide an additional $80m revenue this financial year-that is, if it starts from I January 1983, which now seems to be reasonably doubtful-and will provide $200m in a full year. It is a substantive measure the consequences of which this Government will have to live with. We will, however, move a second reading amendment. I now move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not declining to give the Bill a second reading, the House is of the opinion that the tax proposed by the Bill is inequitable, discriminatory, inflationary and counterproductive because-
(I) it is proportionately greater on small bank debits and will therefore bear more heavily on less affluent persons,
(2) it discriminates unfairly between financial institutions by bearing more heavily on customers of some nonbank financial institutions than those of savings or trading banks,
(3) it will add to costs of all businesses and will thereby add to inflation, and
(4) it will promote the cash economy and the consequent spread of tax evasion by discouraging the use of cheques.
I want to elaborate on those points. Firstly, in regard to the point about inequity, for reasons that are not explained by the Government the rate of tax proposed will be levied in a way that will ensure that its impact will be much heavier on small value debits than on larger amounts. This is

because the tax has been structured in this way. The rate of tax is IOc on debits up to $99; from $100 to $499 it is 25c; from $500 to $4,999 it is 50c and for $5,000 and above it is $1.
The proportionate impact of that tax is much greater on small debits than it is on large debits, as is shown by the percentages for the various levels that I have just mentioned and the tax that applies. The IOc tax on a $1 debit represents an impact of 10 per cent; on $99 it is 0.1 per cent; on $100 it is 0.25 per cent; on $4,999 it is 0.01 per cent; on $5,000 it is 0.01 per cent. On $1 O,OOO-to take an amount in excess of $5,000-it is 0.01 per cent. Of course, for a much larger amount it would be a much smaller proportionate impact.
It is quite clear from just the range from $1 to $10,000 of debit that the proportionate impact decreases from 10 per cent to 0.01 per cent. So quite a substantial range of impact proportionately is imposed by this tax and it is clearly much heavier on small debits. In that situation it is reasonable to conclude that those small debits would be likely to form a larger proportion of transactions for the less affluent section of the community than they would for the more affluent section, so less affluent people will be affected more heavily than more affluent people and businesses whose payments would be generally of larger amounts. The fact that it has this inequitable impact is nothing unusual coming from this Government. It is not noted for its concern with equity. I note that this fits in with the general pattern of this Government's application of taxation in this country.
Secondly, in regard to the charge that it discriminates unfairly between financial institutions, honourable senators will doubtless have received submissions from credit unions and building societies regarding this matter. There is some justification in their protest as we see it. Those nonbank financial institutions are concerned that being in competition with the banks it is unfair that they should have the tax applied to various transactions of theirs when it will not apply to some similar bank transactions. They are particularly concerned that the savings banks will still be able to make available to their customers bank cheques which are payable to a third party without attracting the tax, whereas the credit unions and building societies, which provide limited cheque facilities for their customers, will be levied for the tax when the non-bank financial institution draws on its bank account to make a cheque payable to a nominated third party on behalf of its depositor. Of course, there are millions of such transactions each year by credit unions and building societies drawing cheques on a bank

2562 REPRESENTATIVES 26 October 1982

Bank Accounts Debits Tax Bill

on behalf of their customer for a nominated third party. Each of thm:e cheques will attract the tax.
With all these cheques attracting the tax this will mean increased charges to the customers of the credit unions and building societies unless those institutions are prepared to carry the cost of that tax, which is highly unlikely because the cost will be substantial. As I understand it, the credit unions have estimated that the impact on themthat is, the amount of tax which they will be levied on the cheques that they draw on behalf of their depositorswill be in the range of $5m to $7.5m. This will add to their costs of operation, increasing them by 4t per cent to 6t per cent. These are not insubstantial amounts. Certainly, I think that it is beyond the capacity of those institutions to carry sums of anything like that order; so they will have to increase their charges, pass on the costs to their CUSlOJ~lers.
That total cost that I have mentioned would include not only the tax on cheques where a customer withdraws funds. Also, the credit unions' operating expenses, when paid by cheque, would attract the tax. So if credit unions seek to meet their operating expenses they will have to draw bank cheu LIes in the main and that will add further to 'their costs. But savings banks will not have such taxes imposed on them. There will be no tax on their payments for operating expenses and no tax on debits to non-cheque accounts held in savings banks, which represent the overwhelming number of savings bank accounts, when customers withdraw funds through a bank cheque either by periodic payment or by one-off payment to a nominated third party. I understand that there is a bank charge of 50c for a bank cheque whereas generally building societies and credit unions provide their cheque facilities free of charge. The net result, however, is clearly to disadvantage credit unions and building societies somewhat compared with savings banks. While debits to most savings bank accounts will not attract the tax, many debits to the accounts of the credit unions and the building societies are now to be taxed.
Thirdly, in regard to the inflationary impact of thIs tax, it will clearlv add to the costs of conducting business by incr~asing the costs of cheques. Most businesses will thereby be affected. Just w?at per~entage of the $200m per annum which wIll. be raIsed by this lax will in fact be borne by busll1esses we are not in a position to say-the Governme.nt has not given us any estimate of that -but It would certainly be a substantial proportion. There is, of COurse the added cost of the banks passing on to their' customers, which inclUde businesses, the costs of the banks themselves

raising the tax-that is, levying the tax and passing it on to the Australian Taxation Office. So there will be further charges in that respect. The inflationary impact of all this will not be enormous but it will certainly be positive. It will add to the inflation rate to some degree. Every little step along the way which the Government makes by such measures adds to the difficulty of getting the inflation rate down and ensures that it remains high, as we have discussed earlier today.
In relation to the fourth point about promoting the cash economy, this is, I believe, a quite serious point and one which I think the Government probably overlooked in introducing this tax. Quite clearly, the increase in the cost of cheques will add a discouragement to the use of cheque accounts. Those cheque accounts are already subject to various bank charges. Let me just indicate the kinds of charges which apply at present. They vary from bank to bank. Let us take the Westpac Banking Corporation which is Australia's largest private bank. It has a quarterly base fee of $4 for a cheque account -and an activity fee of 14c per cheque after the first 30 cheques in a quarter. For an active account quite a substantial cost applies after 30 cheques. There is also a collection fee for each deposit. Therefore, at Westpac one pays 12c per cheque after the first 30 cheques which are deposited to one's account. Stamp duty of tOc per cheque applies in New South Wales. Of course, stamp duty applies in all States except Victoria where the position is to be changed very shortly to a financial receipts tax. Taking the case of Westpac and the other banks, a similar situation applies in that there is a set charge per quarter; there is a substantial activity charge-14c a cheque after the first 30-and a stamp duty as well.
Significant charges already apply to cheques. This tax will add considerably to the cost. of operating cheque accounts by putting an additional charge on each cheque depending on its size. Quite clearly, as various people have commented in the Press, this will discourage the use of cheques. It is difficult-indeed, it is impossible-to say to what extent it will have that effect but it will undeniably do so and thereby encourage more resorts to cash transactions. For instance, businessmen who may previously have made a number of small payments by individual cheques will have a substantial incentive to draw cash where possible, paying tax on one withdrawal from their cheque account, and then make a number of individual payments in cash. There will be tax on the one debit, but because it will be a much smaller proportion than the tax which would apply to a whole series of smaller cheques

Bank Accounts Debits Tax Bill

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2563

will be an encouragement to the businessman to draw cash and then payout in cash instead of drawing a number of smaller cheques. With that system being encouraged there will obviously be more cash payments and there will therefore be an extension of the cash economy, with its associated avenues for tax evasion.
As though we did not have enough problems in this country with tax avoidance and evasion already, we are by this measure encouraging the extension of the cash economy and thereby encouraging the spread oftax evasion. It is obviously very difficult to trace transactions when they are in cash and there are great avenues for the nondeclaration of income. The cash economy is already enormous. The Reserve Bank of Australia has estimated that it represents about 10 per cent of our gross domestic product. On that basis it has been estimated by various people that tax evasion is running at over $3,000m a year. If we take action which adds to that it is clearly a retrograde step to say the least. It is also curious that this step is being taken in the context of a Budget which quite clearly recognised that there was a problem with the cash economy and indicated the intention to do something about it later on. Budget Paper No. I, at page 239, contains the following statement:
A tax deduction at source system is to be applied as from I July 1983 to payments for labour and services not subject to PA YE deductions in industries where evasion of tax on such payments is known to be significant. A White Paper on the proposal provides further details.
As far as I am aware there is as yet no such White Paper, so we do not know exactly what the Government has in mind there. But clearly it is saying that in industries such as the building industry and others where we know that the cash economy and tax evasion are fairly widespread, the Government is trying to introduce a pay-asyou-earn system to do something about it. If the Government does that we will certainly support it but it is ironic to say the least that in a Budget which announces that intention there is also announced a measure which will have the completely opposite effect of encouraging the extension of the cash economy and the greater extension of tax evasion.
Finally, I wish to make one other point. The introduction of this tax means that the Government is interfering-perhaps trespassing is a better word-in an area of tax that has hitherto been regarded as a State taxing area. As I mentioned previously, State stamp duty applies to bank cheques and this has been regarded as an area for which the States have had the taxing rights. The Federal Government, in trespassing into that

area, has done so, as I understand it, without any consultation with the State governments as to its intentions. Certainly, it has had no consultation with the Labor State governments and although it may have had consultation with others I think it is probably true that it did not. I think it is rather deleterious to Federal State financial relations for the Federal Government to trespass on what is and has always been a State taxing area without talking to the States in any way about its intention so to do. The Government has simply announced its intention and said: 'Cop that. We are going to start taxing in this area, which you have always thought was your preserve'. It does not do much to help Federal-State financial relations in this country. For all of those reasons the Opposition does not oppose this measure but certainly expresses great reservations about the desirability of introducing it.

Mr Scholes-I second the amendment.

Mr BOURCHIER (Bendigo) (12.0 a.m.)--I should like to draw the attention of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) to a matter that I think is of concern to credit unions and other organisations in this country who will be affected by the Bill. Savings banks are to be exempted from the charge imposed by the Bank Accounts Debit Tax Bill, and it seems to me that that is unfair to those other organisations which are not given the same privilege. I realise that savings banks generally issue bank cheques drawn on their customers' savings accounts. Nevertheless, many of those cheques are issued. The alternative, if one does not have a savings account at a bank, is to have a savings account at another organisation, usually a credit union or perhaps a building society. Building societies and credit unions will be subject to the charge in relation to their cheques but savings banks will not. I find that to be an anomaly, and I ask the Treasurer to take note of the matter. Perhaps at some time in the future, if not tonight, he will bring in an amendment to ensure uniformity in regard to the way in which the charge is distributed.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (12.1 a.m.)-I shall deal briefly with the matters raised by the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) and the honourable member for Bendigo (Mr Bourchier). I should like to take issue with the claim by the honourable member for Gellibrand that because the rate in percentage

2564 REPRESENTATIVES 26 and 27 October 1982

Bank Accounts Debits Tax Bill

terms appears as it does in regard to small transactions that automatically means that the less well off in the community will be affected more by this legislation. The fact of the matter is that there are an infinite variety and number of bank transactions. Something like 20 million bank debits go through the trading bank system in Australia every day. It does not automatically follow that the small cheques are necessarily the cheques of the less well off. Indeed, some of the very largest organisations in Australia issue very small cheques. I do not think the argument the honourable member advanced is quite as compelling as he suggested.
The honourable member raised the question of the cash economy. I would be concerned about anything which gave a spur to the cash economy. I frankly think that objection to this measure has been given more prominence and has been promoted more than in substance it deserves. I do not say that just of the remarks of the honourable member for Gellibrand because others have expressed this view to me. I think the advantages and the security of using cheques are still sufficiently overwhelming not to have the result that the honourable member suggested, and I think this part of the tax has been greatly exaggerated. We are, after all, talking about the introduction of a tax which, in respect of something like 20 million transactions a day, will collect $200m a year. It is not being introduced at a high rate. The great generality of people do not issue a large number of cheques over a period of a month. Is it seriously being suggested that the introduction of this tax will result in a massive drift towards the cash economy? I think that is an exaggeration. If that is the case, I assume that that criticism would be applied in like measure to the taxes introduced by the governments in New South Wales and Victoria, which apply not only to the turnover of banks but also to the turnover of other financial institutions.
Mr Willis-Receipts, actually.
Mr HOWARD-Receipts, I am sorry. I quietly say that I think that comment has been exaggerated. I say to both honourable gentlemen who participated in this debate that I have had in the last few days, and only in the last few days, some very widespread representations on behalf of credit unions and also on behalf of some building s~cieties. Let me make two or three things clear. FIrSt, the tax does not apply to the debits to customers accounts with building societies or with credit unions. In the same way, it does not apply to debits to customers accounts at savings banks.
Mr WillisWe didn't say it did.

Mr HOWARD-I am not saying that you did. I am saying these things because I think some of the criticisms that have been made in respect of the Bill so far as it affects credit unions and building societies has perhaps been misplaced. In that respect savings banks, building societies and credit unions are put on the same footing. If one goes into a credit union and makes a withdrawal of $100 one does not pay the transaction tax. The credit union in turn, because it is a customer of the bank, presumably would deal with the transactions in such a way that every time an individual customer drew something out of a credit union there would not be a separate debit raised with the credit union's bank for each of the transactions; they would obviously be accumulated. So, I think the argument that the credit unions and the permanent building societies will be put at a significant disadvantage will be seen, when it is examined more closely, as not a very strong argument.
We have to bear in mind, of course, that the savings banks in this country, although they operate separately, are all part and parcel and are subsidiaries of the trading banks of this country. In talking about comparability of treatment, the banks would probably argue that they have to carry the administrative burden and the cost burden of collecting this tax, although in respect of individual transactions it is passed on to their customers, but the credit unions and the building societies which are more and more providing facilities analogous to those provided by the trading banks and indeed by the savings banks are freed altogether from any obligation to collect this tax. I think one can make a powerful argument from the banks point of view that may be they have not been treated generously vis-a-vis the credit unions and the building societies.
The other point I make is that until now~1 agree that there has been the beginnings of a change in this trend, and it is a change that the Government will watch very carefully-the credit unions could be seen to have been more in competition in the provision of cheque facilities with the trading banks. Indeed, at the same time, the comment applies in respect of the permanent building societies facility of paying customers accounts by their own cheques. I think it can fairly be argued that that facility is more in competition with the facility provided by the trading banks rather than the savings banks. In more recent times there has been greater resort by the savings banks towards the payments of customers accounts and so forth by the issue of bank cheques. Customers have increasingly used that

Bank Accounts Debits Tax Bill

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2565

facility, I understand. It is obviously a trend that has to be kept in mind.
I point out to the House, of course, that if the tax were to be imposed on bank cheques we would have to discriminate amongst different classes of bank cheques. There are really three classes of bank cheques--a bank cheque purchased on a normal account, a bank cheque issued by the bank itself in respect of its own transactions and a bank cheque purchased across the counter. It would add, of course, a very significant complexity to the legislation if we sought to disciminate amongst those classes. If we introduced a tax on bank cheques one would have to discriminate amongst them, otherwise a bank cheque purchased against an ordinary current account would, in fact, be taxed twice. What happens in practice is that if a cheque is issued on an ordinary trading account it creates a debit in the account which is liable for tax. A further tax on the bank cheque itself would mean in that particular transaction two incidences of the tax. I have, however, arranged to see on Thursday representatives of the credit union movement. The Government will listen very carefully to those representations. I think it is apparent from the remarks that I have made that at this stage the Government does not share all the concerns that have been expressed. I will want to listen to the credit union representatives and to any building society submissions that are put on the matter.
At the very least I say to the House now that the operation of the legislation will be watched very carefully. We would not want, because of changed circumstances and usages, the building societies and the credit unions to be at any significant disadvantage. I think if one examines the whole gamut of the legislation, particularly the fact that the trading banks will have the responsibility of collecting the tax--a responsibility that, in their mind, means that they are at a distinct disadvantage compared with building societies and credit unions--it is worth my while saying that in the discussions I have had with the banks on this subject they have complained to me that this tax discriminates against them in favour of the credit unions and building societies. It would perhaps complete the picture if I were to note that the credit unions and building societies are putting the opposite point of view. I will move an amendment during the Committee stage but I will not say anything on it at this point. I thank the honourable member for Gellibrand for the offer of Opposition support on this measure.

Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-- Treasurer) (12.12 a.m.)--I move:
Clause 4, page I, line 13, omit 'I January 1983', substitute 'a date to be fixed by Proclamation '.
When the Government's decision to introduce a tax on debits to cheque accounts was announced in the Budget. I indicated that the tax would apply from 1 January 1983. Since then it has been put to the Government that there would be serious difficulties for some of the banks, on which primary responsibility for payment of the tax is to fall, in having in place by that date the necessary systems to cope with their administrative functions in relation to the tax. The writing and testing of the necessary computer programs is, of course, a major task. In addition, some banks have been under particular strain in relation to recent bank mergers and also because of the imposition of a number of State taxes.
The Government accepts that there may be a valid case for allowing more time for banks to prepare for the commencement of the tax. It is therefore proposed to delete the present commencement date of I January 1983. By this amendment the tax will commence to apply from a date to be fixed by proclamation. The Government will, of course, in its discussion with the banks seek the earliest possible date for the commencement of the tax, consistent with practical convenience. I have had discussions with the chief executives of the banks regarding this matter and I am satisfied, as is the Reserve Bank of Australia on separate inquiry, that the difficulties are of a purely practical kind. They result from the very large number of debits which are involved and the fact that it is a stepped rate tax. If it were a flat rate tax in respect of all transactions, there would not be the same difficulty; but I think that a change of that nature would have undesirable regressive features. Naturally, we will be seeking from the banks ,he earliest possible implementation date. This change will provide a flexibility that did not exist under the Bill as originally presented.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (12.16 a.m.)--The Opposition does not appose this amendment. I simply note that the Government is expecting to get $80m in this year's Budget from the operation of this tax. If the tax is now to operate from some time after 1 January, presumably the Government will raise somewhat less than $80m-- something like $13m less for every month that the Government fails to get the tax into operation after I January. It would be interesting to know

whether the Treasurer has any idea whether the delay before the Act is proclaimed will involve one or two months or even three months.

The Bill.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (12.19 a.m.)- by leave-I move:
(I) Clause 3. page 2, after the definition of 'Commissioner', insert the following definition:
, "company" means a body corporate, a partnership or any other unincorporated association or body of persons;'
(2) Clause 6, page 7, line 9, omit 'of 6 months commencing on I January 1983', substitute 'commencing on the date fixed by Proclamation under paragraph 4 (a) of the Bank, Account, Debits, Tax Act 1982 and ending on 30 June 1983'.
(3) Clause 12, page 10, after sub-clause (I), insert the following sub-clause:
'( IA) Sub-section (I) does not apply in relation to a taxable debit made before the date fixed by Proclamation under paragraph 4 (a) of the Bank, Account, Debits, Tax Act 1982.'.
The purpose of the first of the proposed amendments is to remedy a technical deficiency in the Bill which, in some places, refers to a company without also saying, as was intended, that the term ?as the meaning which the amendment will give It. An example is the definition of resident of Australia in sub-clause 3 (2) of the Bill used in associated anti-avoidance measures against misuse of Overseas bank accounts. The problem will be met by incorporating in the list of definitions contained in clause 3 a definition of 'company'. The definition requires references to a company to. be read, except where a contrary intention is eVIdent, as including references not only to a body Co~porate but also to a partnership or to any other unmcorporated association or body of persons.

The other two amendments relate to the Government's decision regarding the commencement date of the new tax on debits to cheque accounts. The first of these proposes to amend clause 6 so that the requirement on the Commissioner of Taxation to report annually to the Parliament on the working of the new legislation will in the first instance apply for the part of the 1982-83 financial year commencing on the date on which the tax comes into effect, that is, on proclamation. By the third amendment, by which a new sub-clause will be inserted in clause 12, the banks responsible for paying the new tax will be relieved of any obligation to lodge returns and pay tax in respect of any period prior to the day proclaimed. I commend the amendments to the Committee.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (12.22 a.m.)-The Opposition does not oppose these amendments.

Mr WALLIS (Grey) (12.24 a.m.)-Tonight I wish to raise a matter that I feel should be aired. Last Friday there was a demonstration at Whyalla during which unionists were arrested. Honourable

Adjournment

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2567

members should hear what led up to this issue. An announcement was made a few weeks ago that the Prime Minister (Mf Malcolm Fraser) was to visit WhyalIa. Just after that announcement was made there was also an announcement by the Broken HilI Proprietary Co. Ltd in WhyalIa that 340 jobs were to go as BHP would cut shift work. Of course, this caused quite a bit of concern in WhyalIa. As a result, and in view of the announcement that the Prime Minister was to visit
WhyalIa, the Trades and Labour Council in that city approached me to see whether some talks with the Prime Minister could be arranged with the Prime Minister's office. On Wednesday, 20 October, I was informed that it was not possible for Mr Fraser to meet the representative of the Trades and Labour Council in WhyalIa because of lack of time but that the visit was to take place on Friday, 22 October, on which day Mr Fraser turned up in Whyalla. About 200 people were at the airport to protest at the grave economic situation existing in WhyalIa and also to enable the Secretary of the Trades and Labour Council of that city to present a letter to the Prime Minister. The police were there in full strength. I understand that the star force from Adelaide was on duty. When the Secretary of the Trades and Labour Council endeavoured to move forward to present a letter to the Prime Minister, he was
stopped. When he tried to push past the police they grabbed him. He was apprehended and placed in a police car which took him away.

Many of the people at Whyalla were highly incensed at what the police had done and efforts
were made to block the police car. In the course of
what happened after that other trade unionists were arrested. This whole situation need not have
arisen in a city which had just been informed of
loss of jobs, et cetera, had the Prime Minister been
prepared to allow 10 minutes or a quarter of an
hour of his time to meet the trade union officials in that town. If he had done that I am sure the situation would not have arisen. The crowd was highly
incensed and as a result there were some altercations. In the few minutes I have left I should
like to refer to the letter which the Secretary of
the Trades and Labour Council at Whyalla endeavoured to present to the Prime Minister. The letter states:

The Prime Minister, The Hon. M. Fraser.

21 st October 1982

Prime Minister,

Through the Trades and Labour Council of Whyalla, we
wish to make the following submissions on behalf of steel workers, workers in associated industries, and the citizens of Whyalla.

That your Government take immediate action in calling on BHP. to declare a moratorium on further cutbacks in its workforce.
That assistance be given to the Australian Steel Industry through Federal Government intervention, which would be no less favourable than their counterparts in other steel producing countries.
That immediate measures be undertaken to halt the imports of steel products which can be produced in Australia.
That Government funds be made available to create labour intensive industries into the Whyalla region, as an alternative to the Steel Industry.
The above submissions are made to you as a genuine attempt to halt a repetition of the closure of the Whyalla Shipyards in 1978, at which time 1,800 jobs were lost to the City of Whyalla.
The present trend regarding job opportunities for people in, and also those about to enter the workforce in Whyalla, is to say the least, grim!
We have recently lost the manufacturing industry of Northern Engineering, which at its peak production, employed 140 workers!
Titan Manufacturing (BHP.) is due to cease operations towards the end of November 1982, which at its peak employed in excess of 60 people.
If the present trend continues, the City of Whyalla will stagnate and eventually deteriorate, which would be a shocking indictment of a Government that assisted in bringing people from all parts of Australia as well as the world, to work for a shipbuilding and Steel Industry; one that no longer exists and the other that has reduced its workforce by in excess of 1,100.
lt should be pointed out to you Prime Minister that at the completion of the Stony Point project, less than 60 people will be employed and of these 20 would be staff positions.
We are hopeful that you as Prime Minister will now intervene and take the above submissions into serious consideration.
Yours faithfully,
D. ELKINS,
SECRETARY T.L.c. WHYALLA
That is the letter which the police would not allow Mr Elkins, secretary of the Whyalla Trades and Labour Council, to present to the Prime Minister. Had they done so, the problems that resulted because of the refusal of the police to allow that letter to be presented to the Prime Minister would have been obviated.

Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (12.28 a.m.)~1 want to refer again to the sacking by the Age of the Melbourne journalist Anthony McAdam, which I discussed last week. This matter was also the subject of my personal explanation this morning in answer to the editor's note in the Age last Friday in which he dismissed the suggestion that the McAdam sacking looked like a leftist political operation. I remind the House that the Age for some time suppressed discussion of this matter in

2568 REPRESENTATIVES 26 and 27 October 1982

Adjournment

its columns. When Mr Terry lane, of the Australian Broadcasting Commission, was removed from one program, the Age was indignant that the ABC would not discuss the matter on air and it opened its columns to the discussion. But not in this case.
In his editor's note last Friday, the editor of the Age said that he had received threeonly threeletters saying: that McAdam had been sacked for political reasons. He did not say that two of the three letters between them were signed by 14 writers, all prominent academics and people well known in public life. The editor's note of last Friday stuck to the idea that the sacking was due to an economy drive, despite the fact that there can be no economy when one freelance writer is replaced immediately as radio critic by another, as McAdam was, in this case, replaced by Mr Barry Hill. One can see plainly that Mr Hill is politically more acceptable to the left than Mr McAdam. Where is the economy in this?
There are some other aspects in which the House may be interested and which I naturally could not mention in my personal explanation this morning because it would have meant debating the issue, which would have breached the Standing Orders in a personal explanation. McAdam's troubles began some 18 months ago when he began occasionally to criticise the so-called community radio stations 3CR and 3RRR for their Palestine liberation Organisation and Irish Republican Army terrorist propaganda and some Australian Broadcasting Commission programs for leftist propaganda in general. It was then that there began the various forms of harassment designed, I believe, to insult, drive out and ultimately silence McAdam, a conservative media critic who could not pass the leftist ideological tests.
The harassment took a number of forms. One was the repeated pUblication by the editor of the Green Guide of letters which went beyond the rough and tumble to become simply insulting to McAdam. He was called a racist, a sexist, a mercenary, an Australian Security Intelligence Organisation man, an apologist for imperialism and an apologist for apartheid, and his work was described as professionally incompetent and no~onously inaccurate. I might add that the apartheid 1I1sult was particularly hurtful since McAda:n had been, in earlier years, arrested in RhodeSia for his attacks on racism and had to escape from that country at night into Botswana.
~cAd~m was not permitted by the Green GUide editor to correct misstateme~ts of facts in

these letters before or in most cases after publication, contrary to what the editor implies is Age policy in his editor's note of last Friday. When someone is being defamed in this way week after week after week he must begin to feel that the editors are trying, however crudely, to tell him where they want him to go.
Next McAdam was then censored. The assistant editor of the Age wrote to him in July 1982 instructing him not to express his political opinions in his columns in future, a restriction not imposed on leftist writers and one which was in any case impossible to observe if there was to be a sensible judgment of programs concerning PlO or IRA terrorists.
I should also mention a letter which McAdam received from the editor in September asking him to comment on reports that the editor had received that he, McAdam, had at a public meeting attacked members of the Age staff. I cannot know what was in the editor's mind but McAdam, reasonably enough, in my view, drew from the aggressive and disdainful tone of the letter the implication that if the reports were true the Age would dispense with his services. McAdam says he felt it was a threatening letter. As it turned out, the reports were easily shown to be false and ridiculous and nothing more was heard about the matter. However, in October McAdam was sacked. That is the end of the story except that the central figure, the editor of the Green Guide, has now been promoted to Deputy Chief of Staff and McAdam has been replaced in the Green Guide by a journalist of pronounced left wing views, an admirer of Noam Chomsky. I repeat that the whole thing looks to be a leftist operation to get McAdam. It ill becomes the editor of the Age to dismiss this understanding of events as scurrilous conspiracy mongering, and to pretend everything is to be explained by an economy drive. Age readers used to expect better than that.
Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (12.33 a.m.)- Tonight I want to raise a matter of grave concern in relation to very serious failures in personnel management practices of the Capital Territory Health Commission. In October last year I asked the then Minister for Health the following question on notice:
Is it the practice of the Capital Territory Health Commission to compile dossiers on staff, hy means of tape recordings and interviews, which are alleged to contain damning evidence on individuals and to which individuals have no access.
On 19 August, 10 months later, in Hansard, the present Minister for Health (Mr Carlton) answered the question with one word-no. One may well wonder why that answer took so long to

Adjournment

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2569

produce. Many Capital Territory Health Commission employees and their union, the Hospital Employees Federation of Australia, had suspected that secret personnel files were being maintained and used in staff selections and appointments and that there were, in fact, two sets of books. In December 1981, in the course of an action on behalf of one of its union members, HEF officials discovered that an unofficial file had been kept on this member. Officials of the HEF were able to view this member's secret file, which was amongst hundreds of others. After the secret files were discovered, further complaints were made to the Health Commission and further assurances given. Hedley Rowe, President of the HEF, was informed by Mr M. Sexton-Assistant Commissioner Personnel and Services, at the Capital Territory Health Commission-that Dr Doust, the Chairman of the Health Commission, had directed both Commission hospitals that only the official personnel file was to be kept in existence and that two sets of books were not to be kept. That has since proven not to be the case. The following is printed in the Capital Territory Health Commission Staff Bulletin issue No. 36/82, under 'Registry Procedures, Confidential Files', after the answer of the Minister for Health in August:
No Commission Officer may request a confidential file to be created without the approval of an Assistant Commissioner or above. It is considered that far too many confidential files are currently being created.
It is clear that the practice of creating secret files is rife and is being continued at present despite the negative answer given to this Parliament by the Minister for Health only two months ago. Yesterday in my electorate office I interviewed a nurse very distressed about the fact that her probationary period had been extended because of one adverse assessment against which she apparently had no means of defending herself. During discussions with the personnel manager and the Hospital Employees Federation officials the woman's file was inspected. In four years there was only one detrimental report. But there was an even more disturbing development, and I will quote from this woman's letter to me. She stated:
I went through my file thoroughly and found an envelope marked -Confidential'. The Personnel Manager said I couldn't open it and he opened it instead. I was deeply distressed by this because inside were seven nude photos of me. My exhusband had tried to get me sacked around September 1979 by dropping nude photos through the post to the hospital and as far away as Goulburn and Wollongong. The police were called and as far as I knew they were given the photos. I certainly didn't expect them to be kept on my file for over two years. Why were they on my file? Who allowed them to be there? Who typed the envelope? Who put them in the envelope? How many times did people look at them and retype another envelope? Were they going to be used against me at a

later date? If my file contained such private non-nursing material how many other files contain scurrilous information?
This is a scandalous situation. The maintenance of secret files on Capital Territory Health Commission personnel has obviously not stopped. I ask the Minister to provide an immediate explanation to this House.

. . . still have not produced agreement on a revised price and delivery date for the Royal Australian Navy's new supply ship.
To be called HMAS Success, the ship is about two years behind schedule and will substantially overshoot its original delivery target of next July.

Mr Derley was sentenced by the District Court at Sydney, on 2 February 1979 to a term of 8, years' imprisonment with a non-parole period of 3 years 10 months expiring on 31 December 1982 for conspiring to import prohibited imports (cannabis) contrary to paragraph 86 (1) (a) of the Crimes Act 1914. I repeat, Commonwealth prisoner; not a State prisoner. In other words, there is a complete cover-up. We have heard of call-girl rackets in the Crown Solicitor's Office in Perth. 26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2573 ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Tenosynovitis: Department of Communications
(Question No. 4380)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
(I) In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction, has there been (a) medical examinations of all workers and (b) an ergonomic assessment of the work process, tasks and work environment made by an ergonomist in all areas where repetitious injuries. for example, tenosynovitis and carpal tunnel syndrome, have been reported; if not, why not.
(2) If so. (a) when were assessments made and (b) what recommendations were made.
(3) Have any recommendations made been carried out; if not, why not.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question, based on advice from my Department and the relevant statutory authorities, is as follows:
(I) (a) No. Medical examinations are however arranged/encouraged when officers or employees report symptoms of apparently job-related illnesses.
(b) No. The wide range of occupations across the workforce in which repetition strain injuries are reported would make a program of ergonomic assessment impracticable on economic and resource grounds. Australia Post is examining repetitive injuries as part of a recently instituted review of the adequacy of existing accident prevention and staff safety measures.
Telecom employs an ergonomist who provides some assessment of work areas where repetition injuries have been reported.
(2) (a) Not applicable.
(2) (b) Not applicable.
(3) See (I) (b).
Code of General Principles on Occupational Safety and Health in Australian Government
Employment
(Question No. 4521)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 29 April 1982:
(I) With reference to the 'Occupational Safety and Health in Australian Government Employment-Code of General Principles', (a) which of the occupational health recommendations from this code have not been printed, circulated and applied, and when will they be applied, and (b) what qualifications are required for officers implementing the recommendations, and is he able to state whether officers from the Department of Productivity in South Australia presently responsible for applying the recommendations have these qualifications.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to the fact that 132 of the 194 employees of the Defence Research Centre, Salisbury, South Australia, examined by an audiometrist as part of the Hearing Conservation Program in 1981, had a medically significant noise-induced hearing loss.

(3) Is he able to say whether the audiometric booth designed and built by the Centre meets Australian audiometrical standards.
(4) Have officials from the Minister's Department and the National Acoustic Laboratories Hearing Conservation Program advised the Centre on ways and means by which noise-induced hearing loss could be prevented; if so, is he able to say whether this advice has been acted upon.
(5) Is he able to say when funds are available for noise control at Defence Research Centres they will be adequate to guarantee that employees will not suffer further noise-induced hearing loss.
(6) Will this program be used as a basis for noise-induced hearing loss prevention programs for Commonwealth employees.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) The Code of General Principles on Occupational Safety and Health in Australian Government Employment defines responsibilities for occupational safety and health and states, in broad terms, the action that shall be taken to fulfil them. However, it does not provide detailed information on the standards required. This information is to be made available in provisions collated from accepted standards and codes of practice. recommendations agreed by State Labour Departments, State regulations and other information, as applicable.
In terms of the Code, the Director-General of Health is Occupational Health Authority for Federal Government employment and is responsible for health aspects of the Code. In accordance with that role, the Director-General of Health determines periodically National Health and Medical Research Council (NH and MRC) Occupational Health Guides as recommendations for the purposes of the Code. These determinations are published in general issues of the Commonwealth of Australia Gazette and copies of the Guides are circulated to Federal Departments and statutory authorities.
Occupational Health Guides adopted by the NH and MRC are, where relevant to Federal employment, determined by the Director-General of Health. Application of the occupational health recommendations is a responsibility of Head Officers, having regard to the specific needs of individual workplaces. Head Officers are defined for the purposes of the Code of General Principles as the permanent heads of Departments or the chief functional officers of instrumentalities.
(b) No qualifications are prescribed by the Code of General Principles in relation to Head Officers. However. Head Officers have access to the expertise available in my Department to assist in resolving matters concerning occupational health.
(2) I am advised that in 1981, the Defence Research Centre, Salisbury (DRCS) introduced a pilot audiometric study along National Acoustic Laboratories (NAL) suggested guidelines. 200 personnel employed in known noise locations were invited to participate in the study and 194 were given a basic audiometric test. 132 failed the test and are considered to have hearing levels outside normal limits.

2574 REPRESENTATIVES 26 and 27 October 1982

Answers to Questions

In accordance with DRCS hearing conservation audiometry policy, the employees who failed the test have been examined by Commonwealth Medical Officers. Those appearing to have medically treatable hearing losses have been referred to their own doctors. Where the audiogram suggested the possibility of noise-induced hearing loss, each employee has been advised of the implication and importance of the use of ear protection and his/her compensation rights.
(3) The original test booth, although satisfactory with modifications, did not meet the total requirement of AS 1269-1979, in particular with regard to the attenuation of background noise levels. The booth will be modified to meet Australian audiometric standards before a full audiometric survey is implemented.
(4) Yes. The DRCS Hearing Conservation Committee acted upon advice from NAL and my Department when formulating a DRCS management approved hearing conservation policy and procedures program.
(5) The DRCS management committee has recently considered the results of the pilot study. Specific contractual proposals are now being formulated to meet the foreseen requirements of audiometric survey and follow-up audiometric tests to evaluate results of protective measures. Based on present known requirements, funds will be available to implement the proposed program.
(6) The NH and MRC Occupational Health Guide 'Hearing Conservation Program' was determined by the Director General of Health on 23 December 1980. The objective of the Guide is to provide assistance in the establishment of a program to prevent hearing loss at the workplace. As mentioned above, responsibility for implementation of the provisions of the Guide rests with Head Officers.
Income Tax System: Inquiry
(Question No. 4752)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a call by the National President of the Australian Society of Accountants, Mr J. O. MiIler, for a full inquiry into Australia's income tax system.
(2) If so, did Mr Miller suggest that the terms of reference of such an inquiry should be to (a) inquire into the effects of income tax on Australian citizens and corporations and to identify undesirable features and anomalies, (b) suggest means by which the tax load could be allocated more equitably, (c) reCOmmend the actions necessary to identify and deter tax evaders, (d) examine the extent to which trusts have changed the tax burden and (e) identify alternate sources of taxation.
(3) Has this proposal received consideration; if so, what was the result.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) .There have been several inquiries in recent years into general or specific aspects of the taxation system, including the Asprey Taxation Review Committee and the Committee of Inquiry into Inflation and Taxation. In addition some other recent inquiries, such as the Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Fmancial System, the lAC Inquiry into Certain Budgetary Assistance to Industry and the Study Group on Structural Adjustment, have examined various taxation issues as part of wider investigations. The government has Implemented a number of the recommendations of some of

these inquiries and continues to have other recommendations under review.
With regard to the term of reference suggested by Mr Miller concerning tax evasion, I would emphasise that the Government has already introduced or announced a range of measures which it believes will be effective in curbing tax avoidance and evasion practices. In addition this area is constantly being monitored with a view to instituting further remedial action if that should become necessary.
With the foregoing in mind, I see no need at this time for a further wide ranging taxation review of the kind referred to in (I) and (2).
Artillery Training Area in New South Wales
(Question No. 4758)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
Have investigations into the acquisition of a major artillery training area in New South Wales to supplement or replace existing ranges at Holsworthy, Enoggera and Townsville been completed; if so, (a) what are the results of those investigations and (b) what action is contemplated.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No.
The investigations to which you refer are of areas in New South Wales where Army could conduct major training exercises, and to which the School of Artillery, North Head, Sydney, and the Infantry Centre, Singleton, could be relocated.
The investigations will involve extensive consultation with State authorities, and careful examination of social, economic and environmental aspects of different options, and are likely to take some time.
The investigations result mainly from the fact that an area formerly used by Army for major training exercises, at ColoPutty, is no longer available, and from the Commonwealth's obligation to transfer the land now occupied by the School of Artillery to the New South Wales Government, under the Sydney Harbour Foreshores Agreement.
Poverty: Welfare Programs
(Question No. 4764)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the disclosures made on the Australian Broadcasting Commission's Nationwide program on poverty.
(2) Has the Government any planned program to alleviate the hardship facing an estimated 13 per cent of our population.
(3) Will steps be taken to ensure that welfare programs will be increased or reviewed so that they provide the maximum benefit to people who are On or below the poverty line.
(4) Will welfare benefits be raised to an acceptable level to provide that percentage of the Australian population most in need with adequate incomes.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.

Answers to Questions

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2575

(2) - (4) A number of commentators have suggested that at the end of the 1970s two million Australians about 13 per cent of the population had incomes less than the Henderson poverty line. The Government has not officially endorsed the use of the Henderson line as a benchmark for measuring poverty in Australia, but I note that the Social Welfare Policy Secretariat in its report on Poverty Measurement estimated that the number of adult income units below the detailed Henderson poverty line in 1978-79 was approximately 460.000. These units included about 1,070,000 people. This represented 9.3 per cent of adult income units, excluding the self-employed, and 9.5 per cent of people in such units. It should be noted that the assessments of numbers made are based on income only and do not take into account capital assets which may be owned by some persons on low money incomes.
The Government is well aware that hardships exist in areas of the community and a large proportion of its outlays are directed to assisting those in need. Spending on social security and welfare alone represents about 28 per cent of total outlays. The Government has announced a number of further measures in its 1982-83 Budget and, of course, will continue to give consideration to ways in which hardship might be relieved. The relevant changes include increases in the rates of unemployment and sickness benefits for youth and for single unemployment beneficiaries without dependents, an increase in the rate of supplementary assistance/allowance for those who rent and a 50 per cent increase in rates of family allowances for first and second children. In addition, most rates of pension and benefit will continue to be adjusted automatically each six months for movements in the consumer price index. Pensioners with private incomes will be further assisted by increases in allowable income limits for pensions and fringe benefits, the introduction of a new pensioner tax rebate, the general reductions in personal income tax announced in the Budget and the substantial increase in funds for the welfare housing program under the Commonwealth State Housing Agreement. Non-pensioners/beneficiaries on low incomes will also be assisted by general tax relief measures, including relief for home mortgage costs, and the new family income supplement which is due to commence operation in May 1983. Concessional pharmaceuticals will be extended, from January 1983. to holders of Health Care cards and to pensioners not eligible for fringe benefits.
