Nuclear Weapons: SALT II Proposals

Â«Question No. 4973)

Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 21 September 1982:

(I) Did the Australian Delegation to the United Nations oppose, move to amend, or fail to vote on, the proposal of the
Soviet Union at the 36th General Assembly to declare first use of nuclear weapons a crime.

(2) What (a) aspects of the proposal are compatible with
or (b) minimum changes in the proposal can make it compatible with, Australia's commitment to (i) non-proliferation of nuclear weapons, (ii) verifiable and enforceable nuclear disarmament, (iii) the development of means for peaceful settle-
ment of international disputes and (iv) public education in the development of effective, representative international peace-keeping, law and order.

(3) Can he say what are the United States of America's objections to the SALT II proposals accepted by the USSR.

(4) Has Australia indicated to the United States the view the US I.eaders have the right to intitiate nuclear war if USSR conventional forces appeal likely to defeat NATO forces.
~5) Has ~~stralia supported the view that a greater firststnke capablhty for the United States is a better deterrent to a ~oviet con~entional attack on NATO powers than near parity m conventional forces with staged disarmament.

Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's questions is as follows:

(I) The resolution proposed by the Soviet Union at the 36th Untted NatIOns General Assembly, which included a declarat~on that the first use of nuclear weapons would constitute a cnme, was a resolution entitled 'Declaration on the Prevention of Nuclear Catastrophe'. The resolution was the subject of some revisions by the Soviet Union and of amendments submitted by Mexico. It was adopted by the General As-
sembly WIth a vote of 82 for, 19 against, 41 abstentions. Together WIth t~e Untted States, United Kingdom and other western. countnes, Australia voted against the resolution. A~straha dId n?t see.k ~o amend it. The subject of the resolution was on hnes sImIlar to draft resolutions which for a number of years, have been submitted annually by the'Soviet Union to the General Assembly.

(2) The operative part of the Soviet resolution takes the form of a 'Solemn Proclamation '. The more usual objective of sponsors of disarmament resolutions is to seek a commitment by member states to take specific practical steps. The resolution is thus merely declaratory in character and seeks to gain political advantage. The Government considers that there cannot be progress in measures of arms control and disarmament unless practical proposals are made which are balanced, and which provide for effective methods of verification of compliance with obligations entered into. The absence from the Soviet resolution of any of these elements, together with the fact that it provides nO basis for practical action, indicates that it was not susceptible of amendment in a way which would advance the objectives suggested.
(3) [n the view of the United States the SALT Agreements have not meaningfully constrained or reduced Soviet nuclear capabilities but have promoted a massive build-up of Soviet strategic forces that threatens to significantly affect the strategic balance. The SALT agreements have done little to promote equality in United States and Soviet strategic capabilities. They have not led to more constructive international behaviour by the Soviet Union or to an improved relationship between the Soviet Union and the United States. For these reaSOns the United States Administration has proposed the resumption of strategic arms reduction talks (START) on the basis of firm United States proposals which would require substantial reductions in strategic arms on both sides.
(4) Australia has indicated that it supports NATO miltary doctrine, which is entirely defensive. NATO has made clear that it will not be the first to resort to force, conventional Or nuclear. NATO weapons will never be used except in response to an attack. NATO's stance involves the maintenance of conventional and nuclear forces able to deter any Soviet aggression at the lowest possible level of conflict
(5) Australia has indicated to the United States that it supports the policy of deterrence, that is, the need for the United States and the West to maintain weapons and forces of a nature and size which will demonstrate to the Soviet Union that it cannot benefit from the initiation of conflict against the West, particularly nuclear conflict.
Royal Australian Air Force Base, Butterworth, Malaysia: Locally Engaged Employees
(Question No. 5035)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 September 1982:
(I) What is the status of locally engaged employees working for the Australian Government at the RAAF Base, Butterworth, Malaysia.
(2) Is employment of a permanent nature.
(3) Do these employees qualify for long service leave and other forms of accrued benefit.
(4) Is the RAAF the designated employer of local civilian labour.
(5) Are conditions of employment fixed by the Australian Public Service Board.
(6) What conditions apply, to eligibility for gratuity payments for civil employees.
(7) When did the RAAF cease to be the official employer of domestic help for Australian Service families.
(8) Do domestic employees accrue rights for the full period of service to Australian personnel.
(9) What was the reason for the withdrawal of the RAAF as primary employer of domestic help.
(10) [n what way do the terms of employment vary from Australian civilians employed at overseas locations.

Answers to Questions

26 and 27 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2577

Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The locally engaged civilian employees currently working for the Australian Department of Defence at Air Base Butterworth are 'exempt' employees, engaged under the Public Service Act. Terms and conditions of employment are set down in the Department of Defence (Commonwealth of Australia) West Malayasia Employees Agreement 1970 which in turn is given force as a Determination by the Public Service Board under provisions of section 88 of the Public Service Act.
(2) The Five Power Defence Arrangement does envisage that nations party to the arrangement would require to employ locally engaged civilians in support of the presence. However this employment is not permanent. The nature of any foreign presence (and indeed the term of such presence) is a matter to be determined by the respective Governments.
(3)The above mentioned Agreement does include provision for sick, recreation and other forms of entitlement. In each case, the entitlement is cast at a level consistent with good employer practice with Malaysia, or with standards prescribed under Malaysian law. An entitlement exists for Long Service Leave under provisions of the Long Service Leave (Commonwealth Government Employees) Act 1976 for those locally engaged civilians in employment on 20 December 1976. This entitlement applies only to service after I April 1970 when the Agreement referred to came into formal being, by virtue of a Public Service Board Determination. Long Service Leave is not a feature of public or private employment in Malaysia.
(4) Under the agreement referred to above, the Department of Defence rather than the RAAF, is responsible for policy and general administration of locally engaged civilians in Malaysia.
(5) Conditions of employment are fixed by the Public Service Board in the manner outlined in (I) above.
(6) The Agreement contains entitlements for gratuity payments to locally engaged civilians who, for various reasons, cease their employment with the Commonwealth Government. The entitlements are:
(a) Voluntary resignation or age retirement: three weeks pay for each year of continuous service (as defined in the Agreement), provided that at least five years qualifying service has been completed.
(b) Retirement on medical grounds or death during service: one month's pay for each year of continuous service (as defined in the Agreement), provided that at least five years, qualifying service has been completed.
(c) Redundancy, or resignation attracting redundancy terms: 1.2 months pay for each year of continuous service (as defined) after I April 1970 and one month's pay for each year of continuous service (as defined); prior to this date. It should be noted that the Department is currently involved in negotiations with the Union representing Base employees on the question of retrenchment benefits. This follows a claim by the Union for the three months pay for each year of completed service. This claim was made after my colleague Sir James Killen announced the Government's plans for the withdrawal of No. 75 Squadron, a move which will necessitate an appreciable reduction in the number of locally engaged civilians employed at Air Base Butterworth from about mid 1983. The Union has rejected an offer by the Department of 1.75 months pay for each year's service after I April 1970. Survey material available from an independent source indicates that 45% of Malaysian private employers sampled pay retrenchment benefits of 1.2 months pay

per year of service or less while 94% of those sampled pay 1.5 months pay per year of service or less.
(7) Employment of domestic help by individual Australian Service families commenced on I July 1964.
(8) No, each period of employment with an Australian Service family stands alone for purposes of accrual of rights.
(9) In early to mid 1964, all foreign components of the FEAF presence in Malaysia (i.e. RAF, RNZAF and RAAF) changed their earlier practice of centrally employing domestic staff for individual Service families. The decision to change the arrangement, as it affected Australian Service families at Butterworth was based upon:
(a) The impracticability of continuing a practice inconsistent with that followed for UK and New Zealand Forces; and
(b) the practice for Australian civilians at overseas posts, which is for the family to employ domestic assistance directly.
(10) Australian civilians employed overseas receive similar conditions to those which would apply for employment within Australia. The main area of difference would be that conditions are applicable by virtue of Public Service Board Determinations in recognition of special features of overseas service. By contrast, locally engaged civilian employees receive conditions which are in keeping with those generally in evidence in the country of their residence.

Mirage Aircraft in 'Operation Western Reward'
(Question No. 5097)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
(I) How long did it take Mirage aircraft deployed from Williamstown, NSW to Learmonth, Western Australia in 'Operation Western Reward' to reach their destination.
(2) What stops were made on the way.
(3) What weapons and ammunition were carried by the Mirages on their flight.
(4) How long did it take additional logistic support necessary to deploy the Mirages in combat situation to reach Learmonth.
(5) Were estimates made of time and cost savings that might have been available if Derby, Western Australia air base had been useable; if so, what are the details.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) RAAF Mirages, participating in Exercise Western Reward, departed RAAF Base Williamstown at 1200 hours on 27 August 1982. The last aircraft arrived at Learmonth at 1830 hours on 28 August 1982.
(2) All the aircraft flew via Townsville and Darwin to reach Learmonth.
(3) No weapons or ammunition were carried by the Mirage aircraft on deployment to Learmonth.
(4) Logistic support for Exercise Western Reward was, in general, prepositioned at Learmonth. However, two CI30 aircraft tasked in support of the Mirages. during their deployment, arrived at Learmonth on 28 August 1982.
(5) In view of Derby airfield's present unsuitability as a deployment base for RAAF Mirages, its use for this exercise was not considered and, hence, no estimates of time and cost savings were made.
Visits by Nuclear Powered Warships
(Question No. 5098)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
What assistance is provided by the Natural Disasters Organisation to port aurthorities to assist in monitoring visits by nuclear powered warships.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Natural Disasters Organisation provides no monitoring assistance to port authorities for nuclear powered warship visits. Defence authorities in each State and the Australian

Atomic Energy Commission (AAEC) make the relevant arrangements with the State port authorities and the State's Nuclear Powered Warship Committee. The AAEC provides monitoring equipment and personnel for each visit.
Visits by Nuclear Powered Warships
(Question No. 5099)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
(I) What assistance is made available by the Natural Disasters Organisation to State Emergency Services to plan for and organise safety procedures associated with the possibility of accident in nuclear powered warships visiting Australian ports.
(2) Which State Emergency Services have made use of this assistance and in what way.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I refer the honourable member to Annex C of the May 1976 document Environmental Considerations of Visits of Nuclear Powered Warships to Australia, which sets out the safety procedures that have been established for all ports used by nuclear powered warships (NPWs). The natural Disasters Organisation (NDO) is represented on the interdepartmental committee which co-ordinates NPW visits arrangements, including safety arrangements but the NDO makes no separate contribution to the development of the relevant safety procedures.
(2) See (I) above. The State Emergency Services are those of Western Australia, Tasmania, Victoria, Northern Territory, and Queensland.

Answers to Questions

Upgrading of Major Airports
(Question No. 4732)

Independent Audit: Department of National Development and Energy
(Question No. 4773)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The conditions under which grants are made by my Department provide that a grantee shall furnish a return supported by a certificate, provided by a registered companies auditor or a qualified public accountant who has examined the accounts and records of the grantee, stating that grant moneys have been expended for the purpose of the grant and in accordance WIth any other conditions of the grant.

Customs (Endangered Species) Regulations: Disposal of Leopard Skin
(Question No. 4911)
Mr Maher asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
How was the leopard skin. forfeited under the Customs (Endangered Species) Regulations on 26 May 1982, disposed of.
Mr Peacock-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Specimens of Endangered Species which are not imported in accordance with the Provisions of the Customs (Endangered Species) Regulations are disposed of in such a manner as to ensure that they are not used for private or commercial purposes.
The Australian War Memorial Museum, which is mounting a display of military bands, expressed an interest in the skin.
I am advised that the leopard skin was delivered to the Australian War Mc;r,,)rial Museum Canberra on 20 August 1982.
Aboriginal Community Land Holders: Rights and Obligations
(Question No. 5023)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 8 September 1982:
Do Aboriginal community landholders have the same rights and obligations in the Northern Territory as private landholders with regard to, (a) ownership of unmustered or feral cattle, (b) control of unmustered cattle by fencing, in brucellosis eradication, etc, (c) payment of dues and levies with respect to cattle they sell for slaughter or otherwise, (d) negotiation of royalties, compensation and conditions for mineral exploration and mining on their land, (e) control of access to the land by other persons and, (f) conditions imposed on others entering their land; if not, what are the principal differences in each case.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) Yes.
(b) Yes.
(c) Yes.
(d) Aboriginal communities holding pastoral leases have the same rights and obligations as other pastoral leaseholders in relation to mineral exploration and mining development. But where land is vested in Aboriginal Land Trusts under Aboriginal Land Rights (Northern Territory) Act, exploration or mining can take place only with the consent of the relevant Land Council and the Minister, subject to exceptions set out in the Act, and such consent may be given subject to agreed terms and conditions.
(e) and (f) Aboriginal communities holding pastoral leases have the same rights and obligations in relation to control of entry to the leases as other pastoral leaseholders, but access to land held by Aboriginal Land Trusts is controlled by the

2582 REPRESENTATIVES 26 and 27 October 1982

Answers to Questions

Northern Territor) Aboriginal Land Act 1978, which provides for a special permit system and allows conditions to be attached to permits.

DCIO Aircraft
(Question No. 5201)
Mr Maher asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 23 September 1982:
(I) Is he able to say whether inquiries have been made concerning the cause of the crash on take-off of a DC I0 aircraft in Spain on 14 September 1982, reportedly from engine failure.
(2) How many DCIOaircraft operate within Australia.
(3) What action has been taken to ensure the safety of such aircraft in Australia.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The unfortunate accident at Malaga in Spain on 14 September 1982 involving a Spantex DC I0 aircraft in being investigated by the Spanish authorities with the assistance of United States officials and techical experts. The Australian Bureau of Air Safety Investigation is monitoring the progress of the investigation and has already received a number of information releases. The Spanish authorities have stated that a preliminary examination of the flight data recorder shows normal operation of all engines.
(2) There are no McDonnell Douglas DC I0 aircraft on the Australian Register but eight international airlines operate these aircraft on regular services into Australian airports.
(3) Even when the causes of the accident have been established the Department of Aviation in Australia would have neither the responsibility nor the authority to direct any remedial action in respect of foreign registered DC IO aircraft. Nethertheless the causes, when established, may have relevance to other types of aircraft in this country and that, principally, is why the Australian Bureau is keeping in touch with the findings of the Spanish investigation.
